See p. 304.\n\n189\n193\n387\n\na\na\n\n8\n\nORIGINAL PAGE\nBLACK AND WHITE PHOTOGRAPH\n\nI\n\nIntroduction\nThe idea for a Field Workshop in South India originally came from Bob Newton.\nHe and his colleagues had been working in Southern India for several years on problems related to granulite formation, and realized that the time was ripe for an international meeting to discuss the various theories in the field, where we could all\nexamine the spectacular exposures of granulite-in-the-making.Newton first\napproached Bill Phinney and me with this idea at the 1985 Spring AGU meeting in\nBaltimore and, with the encouragement of Kevin Burke, we began looking into the\npossibility of organizing the workshop. The visit of A. S. Janardhan of the University of Mysore to the LPI in the summer of 1985 was timely, and he offered some\nkey suggestions about logistics and support, including the brilliant idea of seeking the\nhelp of Dr. B. P. Radhakrishna of the Geological Society of India. During the next\nyear, while Janardhan and Radhakrishna worked out the details from the Indian\nside, we requested and received funding from the U.S. National Science Foundation\nand obtained approval from NASA to hold the workshop. Pam Jones and her capable staff in the LPI Projects Office were still recovering from a similar workshop in\nWest Greenland, but were eager for a new adventure, and they began organizing\nthe complex logistics. On January 8, 1988, 35 scientists from the U.S., U.K., Canada, Germany, Greenland, Australia, and Holland met an equal number of Indian\nscientists in Bangalore, and the workshop began.\nDuring the next two weeks we visited dozens of outcrops and quarries, argued\nabout the superbly exposed features we saw between Bangalore and Trivandrum\n(over a distance of more than 9 0km), and experienced the spectacular scenery,\n0\nculture, and cuisine of South India. Interspersed with the field excursions were four\ndays of technical sessions, during which we presented and discussed the results of\nour research. Largely through the organizational efforts of K. V. Krishnamurthy,\nDirector-in-Charge of Operations Karnataka & Goa of the Geological Survey of\nIndia, and his staff, the entire workshop operated smoothly and efficiently.\nThe scientific accomplishments of the workshop are already obvious. Some\nwidely accepted ideas about the origin of granulites, particularly relating to the role\nof metamorphic fluids, will have to be modified. Many new international collaborative arrangements were initiated. Hundreds of kilograms of samples were collected,\nand these are likely to provide interesting new data for many years to come.\nThis volume contains extended abstracts of the papers presented at the technical\nsessions, summaries of the attendant discussions, up-to-date accounts of the geology\nof the South Indian Precambrian Shield, and detailed field trip guides to all areas\nvisited. I hope that the report will serve as a convenient source of information and\nreference to all those interested in one of the classic Precambrian high-grade terranes on Earth.\n\nLewis D. Ashwal\nHouston, Texas\n\nApnl, 1988\n\nProgram\nJ a n u a r y 9th-Institution\n\no Engineers, Bangalore\nf\n\n8:30 a.m.\nIntroduction to the Workshop\nChairman: Kurien Jacob\nWelcome address: Dr. Kurien Jacob, President, Geological Society of India\nInauguration of the Workshop: Sri. N.\n\n+-\n\nJ :\n\nE?\n7\n\nSUMMARY\n\nSUMMARIES OF TECHNICAL SESSIONS\nT h e Kolar Schist Belt and Other\nSupracrustal Rocks\nE. 1. Krogstad\n\nV. Rajamani introduced the Kolar Schist Belt and\nsummarized the talks to follow about specific aspects of\nthe Belt and surrounding terranes. The talk provided an\noverview of the various lithologies within the Belt and in\nthe surrounding gneisses. The amphibolite-dominated schist\nbelt includes at least four \xe2\x80\x9cpackages\xe2\x80\x9d of metatholeiites and\nmetakomatiites distinct in major and trace element\ncharacteristics. Those amphibolites of the west side of the\nschist belt resemble what may have been the Archean\nequivalent of mid-ocean ridge basalt (MORB); those on\nthe east side were, by contrast, derived from a LREEenriched source, like those of present-day ocean island\nbasalts (OIB) or island arc volcanics. Gneisses of largely\ngranodioritic composition surround the schist belt and are\nbroadly similar in composition, although they are of various\nages and are derived from different sources. Rajamani\npresented the model of the JNU-Stony Brook group, which\nis that the Kolar Schist Belt is the site of a late Archean\nor earliest Proterozoic suture zone.\nIn discussion, Rajamani was asked by K. Burke to explain\nhow the REE data from the Kolar amphibolites could be used\nto relate them to modem MORBs. Rajamni responded by\nsaying that some of the Kolar amphibolites are a\xe2\x80\x99epkted in\nLREE, as are present-day MORBs, but some are LREEenriched, like present-day OIBs.\n\nD. K. Mukhopadhyay spoke next on the structural\nevolution of the Kolar Schist Belt. He described his evidence\nfrom structures in the ferrigenous quartzite within the schist\nbelt for two periods of nearly coaxial isoclinal folding\nattributable to E-W compression. This folding was followed\nby collapse of the F,/F2 folds, forming open F3 folds with\nNNE-SSW axes. Finally, a period of N-S shortening caused\na broad warping of the earlier N-S trending fold axes. There\nis evidence within the gneisses for shearing produced by\nsimilar, nearly E-W compression.\nMukhopadhyay was asked by 1. McLelland whether there\nwere any stretching lineations produced by the deformation\nin the KSB area. Mukhopadhyay responded by stating that\nstretching lineations were produced both by the F, and F2\nfolding episodes. McLelland then asked whether there is any\n\nevidence for the formations of sheath folds in the area.\nMukhopadhyay stated that protosheaths were not well\ndeveloped because of insufficient defmation. G. V. Anantha\nIyer asked whether changes in elemental abundances resulted\nfrom the deformation. Mukhopadhyay responded by saying\nthat there is no evidence for this in the Kolar Schist Belt.\nK. Gopalan inquired about the timing of the compression\nof the Kolar area. Mukhopadhyay suggested that the timing\nof compression may be that represented by the 2420 Ma\n40\nAr/39Ar age of muscovite of Krogstad et al. (this session).\nK. Naha asked if the F, and F2 structures are nearly parallel\nwhere both are seen. Mukhopadhyay asked if there is any\nevidence to indicate whether F, and Fz fold axes are parallel,\nperpendicular, or oblique to the direction of maximum\nelongation. Mukhopadhyay responded by stating that there\nare presently insufficient data to make this distinction.\n\nS. Balakrishnan spoke next on the geochemistry of\namphibolites from the Kolar Schist Belt. He described how\nthe Nd isotope data suggest that the amphibolites from\nthe schist belt were derived from long-term depleted mantle\nsources at about 2.7 Ga (q+,ranging from +2 to +8).Trace\nelement and Pb isotope data from the amphibolites also\nsuggest that the sources for the amphibolites on the western\nand eastern sides of the narrow schist belt were derived\nfrom different sources. The Pb data from one outcrop of\nthe central tholeiitic amphibolites lie on a 2.7-Ga isochron\nwith a low mqdel fit. The other amphibolites (W komatiitic,\nE komatiitic, and E tholeiitic) do not define isochrons, but\nsuggest that they were derived from sources with distinct\nhistories of U/Pb. There is some suggestion that the E\nkomatiitic amphibolites may have been contaminated by\nfluids carrying Pb from a long-term, high U/Pb source,\nsuch as the old granitic crust on the west side of the schist\nbelt. This is consistent with published galena Pb isotope\ndata from the ore lodes within the belt, which also show\na history of long-term U/Pb enrichment.\n\nR. Arculus suggested that the high values of fNd reported\nby Balakrishnan are without parallel in the literature fur\nArchean rocks. Morgan. It was shown that for granulites\nto be formed in the middle crust, heat must be advected\nby either magma or by volatile fluids, such as water or\nCO,. Models of channelized fluid flow indicate that there\nis little thermal difference between channelized and\npervasive fluid flow, for the same total fluid flux, unless\nthe channel spacing is of the same order or greater than\nthe thickness of the layer through which the fluids flow.\nThe volumes of volatile fluids required are very large and\nare only likely to be found associated with dehydration\nof a subducting slab, if volatile fluids are the sole heat\nsource for granulite metamorphism.\n\nDiscussion of this paper emphasized that magmas are\ncommonly associated with granulites and there is little evidence\nfor the large volumes of volatile fluids required for the heat\nsource. M. Kusky presented the first of\nfour talks on tectonics. He reported on detailed field studies\nof selected areas in the greenstone belts of the Slave\nProvince of Canada. This area has long been cited as a\ntype area by supporters of the (now generally abandoned)\nrift model of greenstone belts (see abstract by K. Burke\nand C. Sengor in Workshop on the Tectonic Evolution of\nGreenstone Belts, edited by M. J. deWit and L. D. Ashwal,\nLPI Tech. Rpt. 86-10). Kusky showed that a plate tectonic\ninterpretation accounted more successfully for the regional\ngeology and identified four \xe2\x80\x9cterranes\xe2\x80\x9d that had experienced\ncomplex divergent and convergent histories between 2.7\nand 3.4 Ga. A dismembered ophiolite has been identified\nand a late episode of widespread granitic intrusion has been\nrecognized.\n\nIn discussion, Arculus asked about the depth exposed in\nthis terrane. Kusky replied that about 15 km were visible.\nJ. Percival asked how this interpretation of Slave Province\ntectonics related to P. Hoffmunn\xe2\x80\x99s migrating urc model. Kusky\nreplied that his interpretation had some features in common\nwith Hoffmann\xe2\x80\x99s model. Arculus asked about the tectonic\nenvironment represented by the late granites. Kusky replied\nthat perhaps these might represent a genuinely distinctive\nArchean phenomenon related to greater heat generation. At\nlate stages of orogenic activity mountain belts might have\nsuffered sufficient intemul heating to partially melt. Burke\nexpressed doubt as to whether the amount of \xe2\x80\x9cblock and\nfragment\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cterrain\xe2\x80\x9d terminology of others) assembly described\ncould be achieved without leaving some evidence of strikeslip motion. Kusky said they saw none.\n\nW. S. F. Kidd then presented his paper, which illustrated\nhow late Archean tectonics could be seen to have operated\nin the Slave Province. Lithospheric thinning and stretching,\nwith the formation of rifted margins (to continental or\nisland arc fragments), and lithospheric flexural loading of\nthe kind familiar in arcs and mountain belts could be\ndiscerned.\n\nIn discussion, Meaer commented that now that evidence\nof plate tectonic activity is being so widely desnibed from\nthe Archean, it is surprising that blueschists remain\nundescribed. Perhaps the Slave Province would be a good place\nto look, at least for blueschist mineral pseudomorphs. R.\nSrinivasan asked Kidd what kind of model he could envisage\nthat accounts for the dying-out of volcanic activity as seen\nin the Dharwars. It is hard to understand the absence of\nvolcanic rocks from the Middle Dhanuars.\n\nV. R. McGregor then discussed granulite metamorphism\nin the neighborhood of Godthaab, West Greenland. After\nexpressing his appreciation of the opportunity of seeing\nthe classical exposures of South India, he described three\ndistinct episodes and occurrences of granulite metamorphism in West Greenland: (1) The oldest fragmentary\ngranulites occur within the 3.6-Ga Amitsoq gneisses and\nappear to have formed 200 Ma after the continental crust\nin which they lie. Spatially associated rapakivi granites have\nzircon cores as old as 3.8 Ga, but Rb-Sr, whole-rock PbPb, and all other systems give 3.6 Ga, so these granulites\napparently represent a later metamorphic event. (2) 3.0Ga granulites of the Nordlandet Peninsula NW of\nGodthaab, developed immediately after crustal formation\nin hot, dry conditions, are carbonate-free, associated with\nvoluminous tonalite, and formed at peak metamorphic\nconditions of 800\xc2\xb0C and 7-8 kbar. Synmetamorphic\ntrondhjemite abounds and the activity of H 2 0 has been\nindicated by J. Pilar (of Tarney\xe2\x80\x99s group) to have varied\ngreatly. A possible mechanism of origin resembles that\nsuggested by P. R. Wells, and involves accretion of tonalites,\nwhich heated the material beneath, causing partial melting\nand extraction of water. This heat source could possibly\nhave been subduction-related emplacement of calc-alkaline\nmagma into the middle crust. (3) 2.8-Ga granulites south\nof Godthaab, originally described by Wells, lie to the south\nof retrogressed amphibolite terranes. Prograde amphibolitegranulite transitions are clearly preserved only locally at\nthe southern end of this block, near Bjornesund, south\nof Fiskenaesset. Progressively deeper parts of the crust are\nexposed from south to north as a major thrust fault is\napproached. Characteristic \xe2\x80\x9cbig hornblende pegmatites,\xe2\x80\x9d\nwhich outcrop close to the thrust in the east, have been\nformed by replacement of orthopyroxene. McGregor noted\nthat comparable features were not seen in South Indian\ngranulites during the Field Workshop. Features of the 2.8Ga granulites are: (a) they formed from cool, dry protoliths\n\n16 Deep Continental Crust ofSouth India\n\n150-200 Ma after the crust that contains them; (b) they\ncontain no carbonate; (c) they are associated with\nsyngranitic \xe2\x80\x9crapakivi\xe2\x80\x9d (sensu-lato) ferrodiorite sheets\nintruded at P-T conditions well below the amphibolitegranulite transitions; (d) patchy outcrops (like those at\nKabbaldurga)occur; and (e) little evidence of partial melting\nis seen in the area. Mechanisms of origin for the 2.8-Ga\ngranulites must accommodate the existence of both a dry\nprotolith and the rapakivi ferrodiorites. Possibilities include\ndry melting of deep crust\xe2\x80\x99contaminated by basic magmas,\nunderplating by basic magmas, COz infiltration or the\nproximity of a heat source during the C02 flux, and\nunderplating. McGregor concluded that no one mechanism\naccounts for the origin of all granulites in West Greenland.\nVarious processes have interacted in different ways, and\nwhat happened in individual areas must be worked out\nby considering all possible processes.\nIn discussion, Ashwal asked if his 2860 Ma Sm-Nd age\nfor the Fiskenuesset anorthosite indicated an association with\nthe granulite-forming went. McGregor replied that this was\ndoubtful. Touret asked whether the \xe2\x80\x9cKabbaldurga-like\xe2\x80\x9d\noutcrops shaved depletion comparable to that at the type\nlocality. McGregor replied that they were not yet studied.\nAnantha Iyer drew attention to big hypersthene-bearing rocks\nnear Sittampundi. R. C. Chainnan M. Raith adjoumed\nthe session at this time.\nGranulite Terranes: Characteristics and Transitions\n\nJ. Morrison\nM. Raith opened the afternoon session by addressing\nthe nature of the chemical changes that occur across the\n\namphibolite to granulite transition in a single sample from\nthe Kabbal quarry. The transition from hornblende-biotitebearing granodiorite gneiss to charnockite is apparently\naccompanied by an increase in K, Rb, Ba, and Si, and a\ndecrease in Fe, Mg,Ca, and Ti. Mineralogic changes across\nthe transition include a 12% increase in K-feldspar, 6%\nincrease in quartz, and 1% increase in orthopyroxene.\nDecreases of 10% in plagioclase, 6% in hornblende, 2%\nin biotite, and 1% in Fe-Ti oxides also accompany the\ntransition. Citing the presence of C02-richfluid inclusions\nin the charnockite, as well as the chemical and mineralogic\nchanges across the transition, Raith interprets the transition\nto reflect K-metasomatism in response to infiltration of\nexternally-derived carbonic fluids. He concludes that the\nfluids were probably deep-seated in origin and were \xe2\x80\x9ctapped\xe2\x80\x9d\nfrom depth by shearing, and hence the occurrence of\ncharnockites is structurally controlled by shear zones.\n\nIn the discussion that followed, F. Barker pointed out that\nthe composition of the charnockite is intermediate between\n\nhbld-biot granodiorite and the aplite. Hence rather than\ninvoking metasomatism, the apparent major and minor element\nchanges could easily be accounted for by incorporating the\naplite into the \xe2\x80\x9cpre-charnockite\xe2\x80\x9d amphibolite facies gneiss.\nIn this case the only difference between the amphibolite and\nchamockite is the HzO/C02. B. R. Frost pointed out that\nRaith\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation is dependent on the assumption that\na CO, phase was present. Frost argued that the data are\nperfectly consistent with the extraction of a melt phase and\nin the absence of strong evidence for the presence of a C0,rich fluid, melt extraction must be c o n s h e d as a likely\nexplanation. Finally, R. C. Newton emphasized the variable\nscales upon which the \xe2\x80\x9cchamockitization process\xe2\x80\x9d operate.\nRaith described C02-flooding on the hand sample scale, and\nNewton argued that the same fluid-dominated process occurs\non huge scales as well.\n\nThe second talk was a delightfully entertaining discourse\n\nby G. V. Anantha Iyer. He explained that \xe2\x80\x9cmy specialization\nis generalization\xe2\x80\x9d and emphasized the importance of the\n\xe2\x80\x9cbig picture\xe2\x80\x9d in geology. The \xe2\x80\x9cbig picture\xe2\x80\x9d of the Indian\ncrust contains essentially three components: (1) the upper\ncrust, which is composed of platformal sedimentary\nsequences (including banded iron formations), (2) the\nmiddle crust, which is represented by the Peninsular gneiss,\nand (3) the deep crust, which is composed predominantly\nof charnockites. Anantha Iyer addressed the charnocki\ntization controversy by stating that \xe2\x80\x9cCOzis absolutely not\nessential for charnockite formation,\xe2\x80\x9d and suggested rather\nthat molecular hydrogen movements play an important role.\nThe third talk by R. Sharma included a description of\nthe granulites from the NW Indian Shield. The granulites\nare found with the 3.5-Ga amphibolite facies banded\n\nTechnical Report 88-06 17\n\nGneissic Complex. Detailed barometry and thermometry\nsuggest a counter-clockwise pressure-temperature-timepath\nfor formation of the Sand Mata granulites, which is similar\nto that of the South India granulites.\nIn the discussion, Newton inquired about the age of the\ngranulites. S h a m responded that the best age estimate is\n-3.5 Ga. K. Mezger pointed out that in calculating the PT-t relations from rim compositions, if the calculations are\ndone at lower temperatures, then the resultant P-T-t path\nwould most likely be isobaric.\nThe final talk of the afternoon was given by J. Myers,\nand included an updated summary of geological and\ngeochronological studies in the Western Australian Shield.\nThis terrane bears many similarities to the Indian Shield\nsince they were neighboring parts of Gondwanaland.\nWestern Australia consists of two cratons (Pilbara and\nYilgarn) and four orogenic belts (Capricorn, Pingarra,\nAlbany-Fraser, and Patterson), as well as some relatively\nyoung (1.6 to 0.75 Ga) sedimentary rocks. The two cratonic\nblocks are both older than about 2.5 Ga, and the orogenic\nbelts range in age from 2.0 to 0.65 Ga.\nAnorthosites and Related Rocks\nD.J. Henry\n\nbelt. Wiebe then reported on the metamorphism of\nthe Oddanchatram anorthosite, Madurai district, Tamil\nNadu, India. This body was intrusive into a 2.6-Ga granulite\nterrane and contains many inclusions of the country rock\nnear the margins. It has textural and compositional features\ntypical of Proterozoic anorthosites, but is deformed and\nmetamorphosed after emplacement. Geothermobarometry\nsuggests maximum metamorphic conditions of 900\xc2\xb0C and\n10-11 kbar, but there is some evidence from grain edges\nfor later conditions of 600-800\xc2\xb0C and 6-7 kbar. Wiebe\nsuggested that the mid-Proterozoic crust in this region was\nroughly 75 km thick as a consequence of continental\ncollision and underthrusting of the eastern margin of the\nSouth India Shield below a converging continent.\n\nThe session opened with an overview of anorthosites\nIn discussion, J. Valley asked Wiebe if he thought the\nby L. D. Ashwal. He classified anorthosites into six types:\n(1) Archean megacrystic, (2) Proterozoic massif-type, (3) presence of anorthosite dikes was compatible with the general\nstratiform, (4)\noceanic, (5) inclusions, and (6) extraterres- model that anorthosite is emplaced within the crust as a crystal\ntrial. He then discussed and attempted to dispel some of\nthe anorthosite \xe2\x80\x9cmythology,\xe2\x80\x9d such as the existence of a\ndistinct, catastrophic anorthosite \xe2\x80\x9cevent\xe2\x80\x9d in the late\nProterozoic, the misconception that anorthosite is a major\nconstituent of the lower continental crust, and the\nmisconception that Archean anorthosites represent\nmetamorphosed equivalents of mafic layered intrusions such\nas Bushveld or Stillwater. He offered a general statement\nabout the origin of all anorthosites: They are cumulates\nof plagioclase from mantle-derived basaltic magmas.\n\nmush. Wiebe replied that leuconoritic dikes probably represent\nliquids that have become abnormally feldspathic by resorption\nof suspended plagioclase in periodically replenished magma\nchambers. They are derivative liquids, not parental ones, and\nseem to be restricted to younger plutons in a complex.\nPlagioclase accumulation (from a basaltic parent ?)is probably\nthe dominant process to produce pure anorthosites.\n\nThe next talk was given by J. The most probable mechanism\nfor anorthosite genesis is thought to be ponding of gabbroic\nmagmas at the Moho. Burke inquired about the age of the older\nmetamorphism. McLelland replied that this took place some\ntime before 1320-1420 Ma, based on dates of foliated\ninclusions in meta-igneous granitoids. S. Haggerty asked about\nthe geochemicalcharacteristics of the mantle lithosphere during\nanorthosite genesis, particularly in relation to density and\ndepletion. Ashwal responded to this question by stating that\nisotopic data fiom Grenwilk Province anorthosites indicate\nderivation from depleted mantle. The apparently enriched\nsignatures of anorthosites northwest of the Grenwille Front\nin Lulnador were produced by assimilation of early Archean\nbasement gneisses. R. Arculw commented that in many\nanorthosite bodies, orthopyroxene is the main mafic phase.\nThis is not a nonnal sequence of crystallization. He wondered\nif this was due to unusual source Characteristics OT possible\ncontamination. McLelland responded that normul picritic\nbasalts will differentiate olivine and clinopyroxene and push\nthe residue to the appropriate composition. Ashwal added that\northopyroxene-bearing anorthosites have higher initial Sr ratios\nand lower initial Nd ratios compared to oliwine-bearing ones,\nsuggesting that crustal contamination may be an important\nfactor.\n\nB. R. Frost then addressed what he termed \xe2\x80\x9cthe granulite\nuncertainty principle,\xe2\x80\x9d which states that it is difficult or\nimpossible to determine with certainty the maximum P\nand T that a granulite has experienced. Also, geochemical\nfingerprinting cannot always be used reliably in the nebulous\nregion that is transitional between metamorphic and\nigneous environments. Ion exchange thermometers are\ntypically useful to approximately 800\xc2\xb0C in slowly cooled\nplutonic rocks unless one uses a reintegration technique\non unmixed minerals, or unless a metastable mineral\nassemblage can be observed. Frost argued that in most\ngranulites, fossil temperatures are typically obliterated by\nreequilibration and/or deformation during slow cooling.\nGranulite metamorphism may be further complicated by\nthe common association with igneous activity. The\npreviously-used geochemical indicators such as high K/Rb\nratios and LIL depletion may not be strictly the result of\ngranulite facies metamorphic depletion, but also may result\nfrom igneous processes, which depend on bulk and mineral\ncompositions and on the mineralogy of the protolith.\nDetailed geologic mapping will be the ultimate arbitrator\nof whether a given geochemical signature is the result of\nigneous or metamorphic processes.\nJ. Perciwal commented that noncontacting mafic minerals\nmay provide a fossil thermometer giving 200-300\xc2\xb0C higher\n\ntemperatures than phases not in contact. He wondered if there\nwas a rationale for this procedure. Frost replied that the\nresetting of ion-exchange thennometers will be controlled by\n(1) diffusion of ions to grain margim and (2) transport of\nions in the fluid phase from one grain to another. The second\nprocess will be strongly affected by the transport distance and\nthe presence u absence of a fluid phase. Clearly, transport\nr\nof ions (Le., retrogression) will be favored in systems where\npyroxenes are touching or where ample fluid is present. Thus,\nhe suggested, Perciwal\xe2\x80\x99s observations were reasonable. E.\nHansen commented that he and colleagues have preliminary\ndata that indicate that high KIRb ratios in biotites correlate\nwith high KIRb ratios in the whole rock. He wondered how\nFrost would explain this in a magmatic model. Frost responded\nthat it is difficult to answer this question without knowing\nthe bulk-rock and feldspar K/Rb ratios and the origin of the\nbiotite. Magmatic biotites may have \xe2\x80\x9cnormal\xe2\x80\x9d KIRb, whereas\nmetamorphic biotite formed from orthopyroxene + K-feldspar\nhave higher KIRb. Arculw asked if there are any other possible\ngeochemical signatures. Frost replied that the data were\npennissive.\n\nW. C. Phinney then reviewed the occurrences of\nmegacrystic anorthosite and basalt in a variety of geologic\nsettings and found that these rock types occur in a variety\nof tectonic settings. Anorthosites and megacrystic basalts\nare petrogenetically related and are found in oceanic\nvolcanic crust, cratons, and shelf environments. Although\nmegacrystic basalts are most common in Archean terranes,\nsimilar occurrences are observed in rocks of early\nProterozoic age, and even in young terranes such as the\nGalapagos hotspot. Based on inferences from experimental\npetrology, all of the occurrences are apparently associated\nwith similar parental melts that are relatively Fe-rich\ntholeiites. The megacrystic rocks exhibit a two-(or-more)stage development of plagioclase, with the megacrysts\nhaving relatively uniform composition produced under\nnearly isothermal and isochemical conditions over\nsubstanstial periods of time. The anorthosites appear to\nhave intruded various crustal levels from very deep to very\nshallow. The petrogenetic indicators, however, suggest that\nconditions of formation of the Precambrian examples were\ndifferent from Phanerozoic occurrences.\nIn discussion, Leebnandam wondered how Phinney\naccounted for the absence of meganysts in the Archean\nSittampundi anorthosite complex. Phinney replied that these\nwere likely obliterated by intense deformation and\nrecrys tallization.\nIn the next talk, D. A. Morrison considered the\npetrogenetic significance of plagioclase megacryst-bearing\nArchean rocks. He suggested that these developed in midto upper-crustal magma chambers that have been repeatedly\nreplenished. Crystallization of megacrysts from a primitive\n\nTechnical Reptrt 88-06 19\n\nliquid that evolves to an Fe-rich tholeiite (with LREE\nenrichment) is nearly isothermal and is an equilibrium\nprocess. Cumulates probably form near the margins of the\nchambers and liquids with megacrysts are periodically\nextracted and can appear as volcanics. Some flows and\nintrusives are found in arc-like settings in greenstone belts.\nMegacrystic dikes represent large volumes of melt and dike\nswarms such as the Metachawan swarm of Ontario suggest\nmultiple sources of similar compositions. A complex series\nof melt ponding and migration are probable and involve\nlarge amounts of liquid.\n\ntectonic divisions of the South India high-grade terrane.\nThe Dharwar (Karnataka) Craton can be divided into two\nblocks separated by the linear, N-S-trending Closepet\nGranite, and which differ in the nature of supracrustal\nrocks and the amount of subsequent basement reactivation.\nThese terranes have been interpreted to represent failed\nrifting with subsequent compression. To the south, an EW-trending charnockite belt is interpreted as a deep crustal\nequivalent of the Dharwar terrane. To the south of the\nCauvery shear zone is a distinctly different terrane\nconsisting of a quartzite-carbonate pelite suite within\nmigmatitic charnockite gneisses, and further south still is\nthe Kerala khondalite-leptynite-charnockite terrane. The\nlong mobile belt of the Eastern Ghats differs from the Kerala\nterrane in that it contains, in addition, Mn-rich marbles\nand quartzites. Ramakrishnan argued that the distinctive\nshear zones in the Precambrian of South India do not\nrepresent sutures, but instead formed by intracontinental\nreactivation of preexisting faults.\n\nIn discussion, Anantha Iyer asked if electron spin resonance\nwork has been done on the plagioclase to check fur the presence\nof Fe3+.Morrison replied that a group from Japan is currently\ninvestigating this. Hansen wondered why these rock types are\nrestricted to the Archean. Morrison responded that some have\nbeen found in Proterozoic terranes, and that there may be\nan exposure problem. Barker commented that there is a possible\nTriassic equivalent in the Wrangellia terrane of Alaska. This\nterrane contains buck-arc Fe tholeiite with a 30 km2\nIn discussion, R. C. Naha then discussed the structural features of the\nPeninsular gneisses and compared them with those in the\ncharnockitic rocks. On this basis, three types of charnockite\noccurrences can be recognized: (1) those involved in isoclinal\nfolds that\xe2\x80\x98 have been later boudinaged and refolded, (2)\nthose affected by migmatization synkinematic with early\nisoclinal folding, and (3) incipient charnockites formed in\nlow-pressure zones of fold-hinges and boudin-necks (e.g.,\nKabbaldurga). Naha concluded that at least two, if not\nthree, stages of charnockite formation are required by these\nstructural data.\nIn discussion, Newton asked if the two stages of c h a m k i t e\nformation could have been very close in time, perhaps as part\nof a single deformational episode. Naha replied that this was\nnot possible because fold axes of the different c h a m k i t e forming events are at right angles. Burke commented that\nhe was unsure about the extent to which there was evidence\nfor two stages of folding and charnockite formation in the\nsame areas. He wondered if separate phases of the same euent\ncould have been expressed differently in different areas. Naha\nresponded that the features he described were ubiquitously\ndistributed throughout the Kamataka Craton.\n\nD. C. Mishra then discussed the regional gravity and\nmagnetic features of the South Indian Shield. The\nprominent regional gravity low of 20-30 mgls over the\ncharnockite terrane of South India, coupled with the\ncorrelation of a steep gravity gradient with a prominent\nshear zone to the north, can be interpreted in terms of\nincreased crustal thickness in the South Indian high-grade\nterrane. There is some support for this from deep seismic\nsounding. The magnetic signature of the high-grade terrane\nis also distinctive, and Mishra argued that the PalghatTiruchi line might represent a Precambrian boundary such\nas a suture between two distinct crustal blocks.\n\nP. Taylor then gave the first of two talks on geochronology\nof samples from the Indian Shield. He presented new SmNd data for the Singhbhum granite, which give model ages\n(TDM) of 3.36-3.40 Ga, essentially equivalent to ages of\nincluded gneissic remnants of the older metamorphic group\n(OMG) (TDM = 3.35-3.41 Ga). Lead-lead and Rb-Sr ages\nof the granite and OMG range between 3.28-3.38 Ga.\nThese results are considerably younger than the 3775 +\n89 Ma Sm-Nd isochron of Basu et al., which Taylor and\ncolleagues interpret as an artifact caused by regressing two\nsuites of unrelated rock samples.\nIn discussion, G. V. Anantha Iyer asked Taylor to comment\non J. Longhi\xe2\x80\x99s warning about the need for a renewed sense\nof caution in interpreting Sm-Nd ages. Taylor endorsed this,\nand reiterated that care must be taken to ensure that samples\nconsideredfor isochron construction be truly cogenetic. Gopalan\nasked if Taylor had attempted to obtain any mineral isochrom\nfor these samples. Taylor replied that this was a good idea\nin pnnciple, but that he and colleagues were provided only\nwith powdered samples.\nIn his second talk, Taylor presented additional new\nisotopic data for samples from the Karnataka Craton.\nSamarium-neodymium data for some Peninsular Gneisses\nand Granites give T D M ages of 3.15-3.25 Ga, some 100-150\nMa older than their Pb-Pb whole-rock ages, reflecting time\ndifferences between crystallization age and \xe2\x80\x9cmantle\nseparation\xe2\x80\x9d age. For the Chitradurga Granite and Dharwar\nacid volcanics, however, TDM = 2.99-3.06 Ga, whereas the\nPb-Pb system gives -2.6 Ga, suggesting a significant\ncontribution from reworked older continental crust.\nKyanite schists attributed to the Sargur supracrustal suite\nhave T D M = 3.09-3.18 Ga, indicating that deposition of\nat least some of the Sargur supracrustal rocks postdated\nthe earliest phases of the Peninsular Gneisses. Lead-lead\nages for the Closepet Granite (2529-2578 Ma) indicate\na major tectonothermal event at about 2.5 Ga.\n\nIn discussion, P. Morgan asked if any data on free-air gravity\nanomalies were available. Mishra replied that he had calculated\nIn discussion, K. V. Srinivasan,\nwho summarized the present status of Precambrian\ngeochronologyof South India. He offered support for Raith\xe2\x80\x98s\nconclusion of an extensive 3.3-3.4-Ga tonalite-forming\nevent. Evidence that the Sargur supracrustal sequence\npredates this event, however, remains equivocal. The only\nreliably dated supracrustal rocks are t h e -3.0-Ga\nChitradurga acid volcanics (data of Taylor and colleagues),\nand these are separated from the older Bababudan\nsupracrustals by a major gneiss-forming event. A major\nunsolved problem relates to the timing of the Sargur\nsupracrustals in relation to the basal units of the Dharwar\nsuccession. Srinivasan made an appeal for more geochronological work on South Indian samples.\n\nIn discussion, Ramukrishnan questioned whether geochro-\n\nnology could hope to resolve the issue of the relative ages\nof Sargur and Dharwar suprucrustals, and wondered further\nhow a single folding episode could uccount fur the dijferent\norientations of folds in the two units. Arculus, Dept. of Geological Sciences, University of Michigan,MI48109\n.\nThe genesis and evolution of the continental crust are first-order\ngeologic problems which remain unresolved. We still face several difficulties\nwith respect to obtaining fundamental data necessary for solving the problems\nin the form of the composition and structure of the deep crust, and the nature\nof the crust-upper mantle boundary. While it is clear from a number of\ndifferent approaches that the upper continental crust is generally of granitoid\ncomposition, the processes of upper crust production from middle and lower\ncrust are unfortunately still obscure given the difficulty of inverting the\ngeochemical data obtained on minimum and near-minimum melt magmas to the bulk\ncomposition of the sources. Thus it seems that the hope of using the granite\ncycle as a geochemical probe of the middle and lower crustal source regions\nis not going to be fulfilled, and other methods need to be employed. This is\nnot to deny the use of granite geochemistry as a constraint on the isotopic\nspectrum of the source components and time-integrated parent/daughter isotopic\nratios of these components.\nThe other major lines of evidence both direct and indirect that we have\nfor the nature of the lower continental crust are: 1 exposed "deep crustal"\n)\nsegments; 2)xenoliths brought up in explosive, fast-moving alkalic igneous\nrocks; and 31 geophysical evidence (seismic wave travel times, electrical\nconductivity, gravity and the density-composition relations inferred from\nthese data)\nThere has been considerable debate in the literature concerning the\ntrue nature and relevance to normal lower crust of uplifted slices of the\ncontinental crust that have been metamorphosed at high grades. For example,\nrecent estimates of the P-T maxima reached by para- and orthogneisses of the\nOntario (Canada) Grenville province are 10 12 kbar and approximately 8OO0C,\nwhich would allow these materials to have been subjected to the conditions\nprevailing at the base of the normal thickness of continental crust.\nNevertheless, there is still a thickness of some 30 40 km of crust beneath\nthe present-day exposed Grenville and the exposed gneisses would appear to\nhave been "middle" crust in some overthickened sequence about 1.2 Ga ago.\nA plausible mechanism for a subjecting supracrustals to lower crustal\n)\nconditions; b) allowing these to be exhumed to the surface is a Himalayanstyle continent-continent collision, erosion and rebound. The conclusion\ntherefore is that the Grenville terrain itself is not a direct sample of normal\nlower continental crust.\nStudies of lower crustal xenolithic material have been increasing in\nnumber in recent years because of the difficulties encountered in other, large\nscale approaches. Although these studies are plagued by the minute size of\nsample, possible misrepresentative sampling of the lower crust and non-survival\nof some facies in hostile host magmas, and the lack of dimensional relations\nwith surrounding lithologies, some interesting conclusions have emerged.\nFor example, it is fair to say that one of the major conclusions of xenolith\nstudies based on materials from southern Africa, the Colorado Plateau,\nnorthern Mexico, the Massif Central and eastern Australia is that the lower\ncrust is predominantly mafic in character with polygenetic origins but\npredominantly of assimilation-modified under- and intrapiated basaltic magmas\nand cumulates derived therefrom. As an eTample of the different conclusions\nthat might be drawn from studies of Grenville-age outcrops and deep-seated\n\n-\n\n-\n\nI\n\nCRUSTAL GROWTH- SOME MAJOR PROBLEMS\n\nR. J. Arculus\n29\n\nxenoliths (entrained in Tertiary basalts) in Mexico, Ruiz et al. ( 9 7 ( )\n18)1\nhave shown that the exposed granulites are on average of andesitic\ncompostion ( 63 wt% SiOz), whereas the xenoliths are significantly more\nmafic ( average 53 wt% Si02).\nThere are however, difficulties in the way of accepting the hypothesis\nthat the lower continental crust is everywhere mafic in character. Although\npetrologic and geophysical evidence for a mafic lower crust are satisfyingly\n2\nin accord in the case of eastern Australia ( ) (thermobarometry, crustal\nvelocity structure, lack of Moho ), the same is not everywhere true.\nWhile increasingly there is recognition that the Moho is not necessarily a\nsimple ultramafic/intermediate rock contact, the persistence of rapid changes\nin seismic wave velocity at the base of the crust is incompatible with the\npresence of thick sequences of mafic materials in granulite-eclogite facies.\nIn more general terms, our models of crustal formation which depend\nheavily on the modern plate tectonic cycle are also in trouble, Despite\noccasional opinions expressed to the contrary, there is a consensus amongst\npetrologists actively engaged in the study of island arc magma genesis that\nthe primary flux of material to the arc crust is basaltic in character.\nThis flux may be expressed as intermediate to silicic eruptives in some arcs,\nexpecially those constructed on pre-existing continental crust, but the\npoint remains that modern-day crustal growth in arcs appears to involve\nbasalt as the prime building block.\nThe granite extraction cycle can of course operate on this building\nblock without the same volumetric lever that would exist in the case of an\nintermediate composition protolith. The major difficulties with this type\nof model are 1 the creation of an embarassingly large mafic-ultramafic\n)\nrestite that has not been recognized petrologically, but may not be\ndistinguishable seismically from peridotite in the upper mantle; 2) the\nnecessity of general disposal of this restite at least from the crust given\nthe lack of evidence for its residence at the base of the crust,\nIt might be argued that present-day arc petrogenesis is not directly\nrelevant to the problem of the major periods of Archean and Proterozoic\ncrustal growth. For example, higher temperatures gnerally prevailing in\nsubduction zones might have permitted the direct anatexis of hydrated ocean\nfloor (solidus at about 8 0 0 O C in the pressure range 5 - 25 kbar ) rather than\nthe devolatilization inferred at present. Two points should be made with\nrespect to this model o f direct silicic magma production in the Archean:\na) the products of wet melting of basalt are not like "calcalkalic" series;\nb) the pervasive invasion of peridotite overlying the subducted lithosphere\nby melts of elevated Si/Mg ratios should surely have raised this ratio in\nupper mantle peridotite above chondritic, whereas the observed ratio is less\nthan chondritic and thereby a major constaaint on models of mantle formation.\nIt is clear that major problems persist with our detailed understanding\nof continental crustal growth, and that uniformitarianism may not be a\nhelpful concept in the resolution of the difficulties.\nReferences\nRuia, J. et al. (1987) Contrib. Mineral. Petrol. (submitted).\n2. Jackson, I. and Arculus, R . J . Ashwal, Lunar and Planetary I n s t i t u t e , 3303 NASA Road 1, Houston, TX 77058\nAshwal and Burke (1) o f f e r e d a p r o v i s i o n a l c l a s s i f i c a t i o n o f anorthosite,\nwhich i s given here i n Table 1 along w i t h t h e general c h a r a c t e r i s t i c s o f each\ntype. I use t h i s as a basis t o discuss and dispel c e r t a i n misconceptions about\nanorthosites. Ia l s o include here i n Table 2 some i n t e r e s t i n g f a c t s about\nt e r r e s t r i a l anorthosites f o r those i n t e r e s t e d i n world records.\nMyth t l .\n\nThere was a d i s t i n c t anorthosite "event" i n the l a t e Proterozoic.\n\n. T e r r e s t r i a1 anorthosites are commonly perceived as uniquely Precambrian\nrocks which formed during a catastrophic anorthosite "event" about one b i l l i o n\nyears ago (2). This myth probably owes i t s o r i g i n t o the f a c t t h a t i n t h e\nG r e n v i l l e Province o f t h e Canadian Shield, a terrane unusually r i c h i n\nanorthosite, many (but c e r t a i n l y n o t a l l ) o f t h e massifs have been s t r o n g l y\ndeformed and metamorphosed by t h e intense l a t e Proterozoic G r e n v i l l i a n orogeny,\na probable T i b e t a n - s t y l e continent-continent c o l l i s i o n . The G r e n v i l l i a n event\ne f f e c t i v e l y r e s e t i s o t o p i c systems i n c l u d i n g K-Ar, Rb-Sr, U-Pb, and even t o some\nextent Sm-Nd. There has been some success, however, p a r t i c u l a r l y w i t h t h e\nwhole-rock Sm-Nd method, i n revealing pre-metamorphic ages (3).\nIt i s clear\nt h a t even h i g h l y deformed massifs such as i n t h e Adirondacks, N.Y. pre-date the\nG r e n v i l l i a n event, and i f the i n t e r p r e t a t i o n o f Ashwal and Wooden (3) i s\ncorrect, as much as 300 Ma may separate emplacement o f t h e Marcy anorthosite\nmassif and t h e younger metamorphism. I n any case, r e l i a b l e ages o f anorthosite\nmassifs i n t h e eastern Canadian Shield range between about 1.1 and 1.65 Ga\n( 4 , 5 ) , and p o s s i b l y as o l d as 2.55 Ga, i f t h e River Valley a n o r t h o s i t i c p l u t o n\no f t h e southwestern G r e n v i l l e Province (6) i s considered a t r u e massif. There\ni s no evidence, therefore, f o r a d i s t i n c t anorthosite event. Massif-type\nanorthosites do seem t o be, however, a s t r i c t l y Proterozoic phenomenon, and a\ns a t i s f a c t o r y explanation f o r t h i s i s as yet unavailable. I f other anorthosite\ntypes are included, i t may be stated t h a t anorthosite has been produced over t h e\ne n t i r e range o f geologic time, and i s forming today (Table 1).\nMyth #2.\n\nAnorthosites are a major c o n s t i t u e n t o f t h e lower c r u s t .\n\nThere has been and continues t o be, i n t h e minds o f many, an u n f a i r\nconnection between anorthosites and g r a n u l i t e f a c i e s metamorphism. Perhaps t h i s\ni s because t h e b e t t e r known, o r more e a s i l y accessible occurrences have been\npunished t h i s badly. I n addition, o r possibly as a r e s u l t , a popular hypothesis\nf o r t h e o r i g i n o f massif-type anorthosites invloved i n t r u s i o n , c r y s t a l 1 i z a t i o n ,\nand c o o l i n g o f t h e massifs i n t h e lower c r u s t (e.g. 7). Decades o f work i n t h e\nr e l a t i v e l y inaccessible p a r t s o f Labrador by E.P. Wheeler and S.A. Morse and\nt h e i r colleagues show t h a t t h e voluminous anorthosites o f t h e Nain Province were\nemplaced i n t o t h e upper crust, a t depths no more than about 5 km (8,9). More\nr e c e n t l y i t has been shown on the basis of oxygen i s o t o p i c measurements t h a t the\nAdirondack anorthosite, although metamorphosed t o high pressure g r a n u l i t e\nfacies, was o r i g i n a l l y emplaced a t a shallow l e v e l , probably l e s s than 10 km\ndepth (10). Although deep emplacement o f anorthosite i s a p o s s i b i l i t y i n some\ncases, these appear t o be t h e exception r a t h e r than t h e r u l e .\nA r e l a t e d myth holds t h a t anorthosite can form as a r e f r a c t o r y residue\nduring anatectic m e l t i n g w i t h i n t h e c r u s t . There i s some support f o r t h i s\nhypothesis from high pressure experimental petrology ( l l ) , and t h e idea o f\nresidual anorthosite a f t e r e x t r a c t i o n o f broadly g r a n i t i c melts from t h e deep\n\n...\n\nANOR\'I\'HOSITES: CLASSIFICATION\nAshwal, L. D\n.\n\n31\n\ncrust has been incorporated into some widely accep ed tectonic models of\nTibetan-style continental collision zones (e.g. 12 . There is no convincing\nevidence that ANY known anorthosite formed in this way. Rather, anorthosites\nare plutonic igneous rocks which crystal1 ized from mantle-derived si1 icate\nmagmas.\nSeveral authors have speculated that anorthos te should be a substantial\n(12,13), if not a major (11,14) constituent of the lower continental crust.\nSeismic studies (151 Dermit. but do not Drove this Admittedly, some deep\n.\ncrustal terranes exposed at\'the surface do contain anorthosite\n(e.g. Adirondacks,West Greenland, South India), but as discussed above, this is\na vagary of collisional tectonics. Many high-grade terranes, such as New Quebec\n(16) are anorthosi te-free. The relative paucity of anorthosite among lower\ncrustal nodule suites of kimberlites and alkali basalts also argues against an\nanorthositic lower crust. There is no reason, therefore, to suspect that\nanorthosite is any more abundant in the lower crust than it is on the Earth\'s\nsurface.\nI\n\n.\n\nMyth #3. Archean anorthosites are metamorphosed equivalents of 1 ayered mafic\nintrusions.\nA variety of origins have been proposed for Archean calcic anorthosites\n(summarized in ref. 17). One popular notion, based primarily on the\nFiskenaesset anorthosite of West Greenland, and subsequently extended to other\noccurrences, is that these anorthosites represent metamorphosed and deformed\nequivalents of anorthosite-bearing layered intrusions ("stratiform type") such\nas the Bushveld or Stillwater (18). The differences among these two anorthosite\ntypes far outweigh their similarities. Archean anorthosites are characterized\nby a distinctive texture consisting of equant, calcic plagioclase megacrysts in\na mafic groundmass which is commonly basaltic in composition. This texture can\nbe recognized in nearly all Archean anorthosite occurrences, even in those\naffected by high grade metamorphism (e.g. 19), and is absent from\nanorthosite-bearing layered intrusions. Although there is some overlap in\nplagioclase composition between the two anorthosite types, Archean anorthosites\nare uniformly highly calcic (Table 1). In contrast to Archean anorthosites,\nlayered mafic intrusions are not temporally restricted (Table 1). The tectonic\nsetting of Archean anorthosites i s still poorly understood, but many are\nassociated with mafic volcanic units of greenstone belts, suggesting the\nprobability of an oceanic setting (20,21).\n\nA Simple Unified Theory\n\nBasalt is a mantle-derived partial melt of peridotite. A similar\nfundamental statement about the origin of anorthosite cannot be made with equal\ncertainty. Anorthositologists cannot yet agree as to whether the parental me1 ts\nof anorthositic rocks were crustal- or mantle-derived, let alone what the\ncomposition o f these melts was. I believe, however, that sufficient geological ,\npetrological, mineralogical, geochemical, and isotopic information exists about\nanorthosites of all types to make a general statement concerning their origin:\n"anorthosites are cumulates of plagioclase feldspar from mantle-derived basal tic\nmagmas." I offer this simple statement as a provisional hypothesis applicable to\n- anorthosite types, both terrestrial and extraterrestrial.\nall\n\nANORTHOSITES: CLASSIFICATION...\nAshwal, L D\n. .\n32\n\nREFERENCES: (1) Ashwal LD and Burke K (1987) Lunar Planet. Geol. Survey,Box 25046, Stop\n9 1 3 , Denver, CO 80225 and 981 Nat. Center, Reston, VA 22092\nTwo geochemical and geochronological traverses across the\n1,760-km-long and 50-150 km-wide continental margin Coast bathdlith (Coast Plutonic Complex in Canadian nomenclature),of southeastern Alaska and British Columbia at Skagway and Ketchikan-toHyder show:\n(a) episodic intrusion at ca. 127Ma, 57-5511a, 54-52Ma, 48\nMa and 32-19Ma (a minor, postsubduction event) of\n(b) transversely localized and longitudinally extensive\nrock suites,\n(c) each of which consists of part of the calcalkaline\ntrend hornblende-biotite diorite-quartz diorite-tonalite-quartz monzodiorite-granodiorite-granite (IUGS\nterminology): gneissic diorite to tonalite at 127Ma,\nquartz diorite and tonalite at 57-55Ma, tonalite and\ngranodiorite at 54Ma, granodiorite and granite at 54-52\nMa, granite at 48Ma and gabbro and granite at 32-19Ma;\ndistributed s o that\n(d) the western part of the batholith is largely diorite\nto tonalite and the eastern part tonalite to granite.\nAll rocks show high concentrations of Sr and Ba, medium to\nhigh K and moderate light REE enrichment with small or no Eu\nanomalies. 87Sr/86Sri ratios of 0.7047-0.7066 show mild decrease\nwith age and a larger range at higher Si02 contents. Five 143Nd/\n144Ndi ratios are 0.51229-0.51264 and are of island-arc or\nimmature-crustal values. Compositions at Si02 of ca. 55-631 are\nlike those of Gill\'s average medium-K and high-K orogenic andesites. Pillowform inclusions of high-A1 basalt are found in several suites and represent coeval magma derived from the underlying subduction zone.\nJust west of Coast batholith, as in the region east and\nnorth of Ketchikan, intrusives of quartz diorite and tonalite\nare found. These are 93-89Ma old, chemically resemble Coast\nbatholithic rocks, but show ower 87Sr/86Sri ratios (0.70410.7049), generally higher 14\'Nd/144Ndi\nratios (0.51246-0.51265)\nand lower K. These plutons may not have been emplaced in their\npresent positions (relative to Coast batholith), but their chemical character indicates origin above a subduction zone.\nCoast batholith not only formed in direct response to subduction of Pacific plates, but it is wholly bounded by accreted\nterranes of oceanic or slope origin. Unlike Sierra Nevada, Idaho\nand Peninsula Ranges batholiths, Coast batholith formed hundreds\nof kilometers from Precambrian crustal rocks. Its compositional\ntrend is probably in large part a result of damp fractionation\nof gabbroic or dioritic magmas, with the exception that the\ngranites may contain large crustal components.\n\nNATURE OF COAST BATHOLITH\nBarker C Arth\n, .*\n\n37\n\nAre analogs of Coast batholith found in the Archean? Like\nmany Archean plutonic suites, Coast batholith formed in relatively young volcanic and sedimentary rocks. However, the abundant rocks of intermediate S i O q content of the western half of\nthe batholith are not common in the Archean, whereas the abunJ\ndant trondhjemitic plutons of the Archean are rare to absent\nin Coast batholith (except as seams formed by inTplace melting\nof metabasalt inliers). The granites and granodiorites of Coast\nbatholith tend to be less radiogenic than its quartz diorite and\ntonalite, in opposition to typical Archean occurrences. The\nanswer, perhaps, is "no". Archean plate-tectonic processes, In\nproducing evolved magmas different from those of Phanerozoic\nsybduction zones, probably were unique.\n\nN89-22201\n/ .\n\n/\n-\n\n77\'\n\nHOW WIDELY IS THE ANDEAN TYPE OF CONTINENTAL MARGIN REPRESENTED\nIN THE ARCHEAN? Kevin Burke, Lunar and Planetary I n s t i t u t e , 3303 Nasa Road\nOne, Houston, TX 77058 and Department of Geosciences, University of Houston,\nUniversity Park, Houston, TX 77004\n\nContinents a r e elevated above the ocean f l o o r because continental c r u s t s\na r e made up of material l i g h t e r t h a n the overwhelmingly b a s a l t i c oceanic\nc r u s t . The g r e a t b u l k of igneous rocks l e s s dense than b a s a l t forming today\ni s made a t convergent p l a t e boundaries and f o r this reason processes a t\nconvergent boundaries a r e considered most l i k e l y t o have been dominant i n the\nproduction o f the continental crust.\nConvergent p l a t e boundaries can be characterized as: Island a r c s , Andean\nMargins and Collision zones (both arc-continent c o l l i s i o n zones and continentcontinent c o l l i s i o n zones). Only isJand-arc convergent boundaries can\no r i g i n a t e e n t i r e l y w i t h i n the ocean (perhaps nucleating on oceanic f r a c t u r e\nzones) and f o r this reason this type of boundary i s l i k e l y t o have been\ninvolved i n forming the world\'s f i r s t "continental c r u s t " a t more than 4 Ga.\nCompositions of rocks formed a t i s l a n d arc boundaries i n the Late Phanerozoic\n( f o r example, the Greater Antillean Island Arc (11 show close resemblances\nt o some Archean rocks and i t seems l i k e l y t h a t this k i n d of material i s\nwidely represented within the Archean a1 though some differences i n source\nmagmas and i n proportions of rock types have been suggested.\nAndean and c o l l i s i o n a l convergent b o u n d a r i e s a r e l i k e l y t o i n v o l v e ( 1 )\ncontamination of material newly-derived from the mantle by material already\ni n the continentaJ c r u s t and ( 2 ) p a r t i a l m e l t i n g of t h a t c r u s t . These\nprocesses produce recognizable geochemical signatures ( e . g . h i g h i n i t i a l\nstrontium isotopic r a t i o s ) which a r e widespread among Archean rocks.\nI t therefore seems possible t h a t Andean margins and both kinds of\nc o l l i s i o n a l boundaries a r e represented w i t h i n the Archean and I here draw\na t t e n t i o n t o a simple s t r u c t u r a l c r i t e r i o n t h a t may be applied t o discriminate\nbetween Andean margins and continental c o l l i s i o n zones. Continental c o l l i s i o n\nzones a r e enormous i n area (106 km2) (e.g. Tibet today) and have been so i n\nthe p a s t , (e.g. the Grenville Province and the Pan African). I t i s hard t o\nrecognize such huge areas among Archean rocks because o the limited extent\nof most preserved Archean provinces, b u t the 0 . 5 ~ 1 0km area of g r a n u l i t e\n~\nw i t h i n t h e Superior Province of Labrador i s the most l i k e l y candidate. By\nc o n t r a s t , Andean margins a r e long (-1O3km) and narrow ( a s the Andes today)\nand volcanism w i t h i n Andean provinces i s usually r e s t r i c t e d t o a narrow zone\nless than 100 km wide expanding t o a broader area (such as i n South America\ntoday) only i n areas of extreme shortening of the basement [2].\nThe Phanerozoic h i s t o r y of t h e Andes shows t h a t a p a r t from r a f t i n g i n of\na r c and microcontinental material ( " t e r r a n e s " o f some authors) which was\nimportant i n the Paleozoic i n the South [3] and has been important again\nw i t h i n the l a s t 100 M i n the North [1], t h e r e have been episodes of c r e s t a l\na\nr i f t i n g [4] and marginal basin formation [5] w i t h i n the Andean a r c . Possible\nanalogues of these f e a t u r e s a r e common i n the Archean record (e.g. 6).\nIn summary: Andean margins a r e l i k e l y t o be recognized i n the Archean as:\n(1) the s i t e of abundant g r a n o d i o r i t i c t o g r a n i t i c intrusions w i t h e i t h e r o r\nboth of mantle and older continental i s o t o p i c signatures, ( 2 ) occupying a\nlength of hundreds of kilometers, b u t ( 3 ) only a w i d t h one o r two hundred\nkm, ( 4 ) c u t by mafic dikes representing episodes of extension within the a r c ,\n(5) the s i t e of c r e s t a l - r i f t volcanic rocks ( l i k e those of Taupo i n New\nZealand, e.g. [ r e f . 61 and, ( 6 ) the s i t e o f marginal basins ( l i k e the Rocas\nVerdes [5]).\n\nz\n\nANDEAN ARCHEAN MARGINS?\nBurke, K.\n\n39\n\nAlthough i t i s c l e a r t h a t no Archean Andean margins can have survived\nw i t h i n continents, Andean margin remains have been recognized i n t h e Superior\nProvince o f Canada Cref.71 and the Closepet " g r a n i t e " o f Southern I n d i a may\nrepresent another example. R L Friend, Dept.of Geology, Oxford Polytechnic, Oxford OX3 OBP,\nU.K.;A P Nutman, Dept. of Earth Sciences, MUN, St. John\'s Newfoundland, Canada, and V. R McCregor,\n. .\nAtammik, 3912 Sukkertoppen, Greenland, Denmark\nWithin a distance of s.60 km across the mouth of Godthiibsfjord (Fig. l), three different Archaean\ns\ngranulite facies events are represented. Fust,that which affected the Amftsoq gneisses at c.3600 Ma (1) i\npreserved only in relatively small areas. Second, that at c.3ooo Ma affecting Nordlandet. Third, that at @Ul\nMa,wbich we discuss here, affecting the region south of the Qarliit nunaat thrust and south to Bjphesund\n(F43.1).\nThis granulite facies event has been dated (Pb/Pb) at 2800+70 Ma (2) and at 27952 Ma (zircons) from an\nintrusive ferrodiorite/rap&vi (s.1.) granite suite (3). The block-comprises probable middle Archaean gneisses,\nsupracrustal rocks dominated by amphibolites and intrusive, layered gabbro/anorthosite complexes (sec 4 for\nfurther references). Early deformation episodes culminated in granulite facies conditions, the assemblages of\nwhich were extensively retrogressed to amphibolite facies, frequently obliterating the early history, during the\nlate Archaean. Two different boundaries, both now highly modified by the later events, have been recognised:\n(a) Southern boundary\nThe boundary occurringin and around Bjeesund (Fq. have been variably retrogressed, but toward\n1)\nthe bead of the fjord is well-preserved and is shown to originally have been prograde. The boundary comprises\nthe grey biotite + hornblende and amphibolite-facies gneisses traversed by a network of brownish,\northopyroxene-&ring mnes and cut by felsic, orthopyroxene-bearing pegmatite sheets. Orthopyroxene\ngrowth clearly overprints the amphibolite facies structures and fabrics on many scales. Owing to the proximity\nof granulite and amphibolite facies assemblages and the intricacy of the network, the relationship is interpreted\nas prograde and to have formed by fluid-dominated processes, similar to those in southern India (e.g. 5).\n(b) Northernboundary\nThis is demonstrated to be a tectonic feature, the Qarliit nunaat thrust (Fw. 2) which was folded\n1 and\nand metamorphosed in the late Archaean. This deformation decreases inland and the thrust becomes more\napparent. South of the thrust granulite facies rocks are brownish weathering and toward the thrust relid brown\ncores surrounded by greyish-white, bleached rocks occur (Fig. 2). These bleached rocks have one or a\ncombmation of two fabrics within them. At bigher structural and topographic levels new amphibolite facies\nminerals statically overprint and mimic the steeply dipping granulite facies fabrics. At lower levels the\namphibolite facies minerals form a progressively more gently southerly dipping fabric sub-parallel with the\n1\nthrust (fig. and 2). Generally, deeper structural levels (at 2800 Ma) are represented in this northern part of\nthe block with preserved conditions of - 10 kbar and 8oOc (e.g. 6; our unpublished data).\nc.\nDISCUSSION\nPrevious interpretations of the region are at variance with that presented here. The southern boundary\nwas recognised to be prograde\nbut the patchy distribution of granulite assemblages in the Fiskenaesset\nregion was interpreted to represent the original prograde boundaries whicb had been little modified by later\nretrogression (8). The northern boundary, despite detailed l:#wXIoscale mapping, was also identified as\nprograde (6; 9). This misinterpretation is considered to be due to the interaction of the flat-lying foliation\nassociated with the thrust and the steeply dipping foliation of the granulites during folding (Fg.\n3).\nAdditionally, PT data were assembled to represent PT conditions for synchronous amphibolite and granulite\nfacies metamorphism (6) which is now known not to be the case. Recent data (lo), the new data presented\nhere and other unpublished results demonstrate that the granulite facies block evolved separately before being\ntectonically excavated and juxtaposed against lower grade rocks. Much of the granulite facies terrane,\nespecially close to the thrust boundary, is dominated by the process of hydrous retrogression. Geochemical\nrelationships in most of the northern part are thus considered to reflect wtrvgmde rather than prograde\nprocesses. The use of the earlier interpretations to construct theories for the general evolution of continental\ncrust (6,9,11) must be regarded with caution.\n\n(a,\n\nGRANULITE FACIES BOUNDARIES, SW GREENLAND\nFriend, et al.\n47\n\nkm\n\n..\n\nAkia terrane dominated by 3050-3000 Ma NOk gneisses\nc. 3000 Ma QranuMefacies\nVariably overprinted by 2750-2600 Ma\n\'\n0\nc. 3000 Ma amphibolite facies\namphiolite facies assemblages\nTre Brsdre terrane dominated by 2800-2750 Ma ikatoq gneisses\n\' 0 2750-2600 Ma amphibolite facies assemblages\nFaeringehavn terrane dominated by pre-3600 Ma Amltsoq gneisses\n2750-2600 Ma amphbolite facies assemblages\nTasiusarsuaq terrane dominated by pre-2800 Ma Nitk-like gneisses\nA A c. 2800 Ma granclite facies assemblages\nA A c. 2800 Ma amphibolite facies assemblages\n/ Tectonic boundary between terranes\nJ Position of prograde amphibolite- granulite facies transitions\nr\n.\n,\nPost-tectonic 06rqut granite complex c. 2550 Ma\n\n1\n\n&&\n\nI\n\n/\'\n\nfaults\n\nF g 1. Haggerty and Paul B. Toft, Department of Geology,\nUniversity of Massachusetts, Amherst, MA 01003, U . S . A .\nNotwithstanding the attention given over the past several\ndecades to the genesis of the crust, interpretations of the\nnature of the continental lower crust remain diverse and opinions\ndiffer widely as to whether the lower crust is hydrated or\nanhydrous, and mafic or felsic. The present study attempts to\nconstrain models of the lower crust and upper mantle through\nintegration of petrochemical and petrophysical properties of a\nsuite of granulites (garnet anorthosites to garnet pyroxenites)\nand eclogites from the Man Shield of the West African Craton.\nAge provinces in the shield are Leonean ( - 3 . 0 Ga), Liberian ( - 2 . 7\nGa), and Eburnean (-2.0 Ga), and these are fault bounded by the\n-550 Ma Pan African age province. Crustal granulites and upper\nmantle eclogites were sampled as xenoliths from diamondiferous\nkimberlites of Cretaceous age (90-120 Ma) which were intruded\ninto Liberian age province granitic gneisses in Liberia and\nSierra Leone.\nMost of the granulites are typical of those from elsewhere\nin Africa (1) and other world-wide locations ( Z ) , but some\napparently differ in three significant respects: firstly,in the\npresence of iron metal ( 3 ) , secondly in the effects of metasomatism ( 4 1 , and thirdly in aspects of partial melting. Native iron\nof low Ni and Co content, in association with scapolite and/or a\nhighly aluminous (18-21 wt% A1203) tschermakitic amphibole, was,\nformed by decomposition of almandine-rich (Alm51-56Fyr2?-a2\nGrossi4-1?) garnet. Iron metal also resulted from ilmenite\ndecomposition in which iron, ulvzspinel, troilite, and FeTiS were\nformed. Temperature estimates are 830-1OOOoC at f02\'s at or\nbelow iron-whtite (IW). Metasomatism is manifest in the\nformation of scapolite (60-75 % Me), and also in rims of ferrofreudenbergite (NazFeTi7016) around nucleii of rutile, a reaction\nin which ilmenite, perovskite, and sphene were also formed ( 4 ) .\nMetasomatic fluids or partial melts were enriched in Na, Fe, Ca,\nand Si in contrast to those typical of upper mantle metasomatism\nin which metasomatic enrichment is characterized by K, Ba, Sr,\nTi, LREE, Nb, and Zr (5). Partial melting of the granulites,\nspecifically of garnet and plagioclase, yielded clinopyroxene +\nkyanite + scapolite. A characteristic feature of these classes\nof granulites is the presence of graphite.\nMajor element XRF analyses, petrographic and electron\nmicrobeam mineral chemistries, densities, magnetic properties,\nand calculated P-T and seismic P-wave velocities ( V p ) have been\ndetermined for most of the larger specimens of granulite, and for\nselected eclogites and anorthosites. A chemical continuum\nbetween these lithologies has been established (Fig. 1). High Mg\neclogites, approaching komatiitic basalts in composition, grade\nprogressively into low Mg eclogites (of alkali hawaiite affinity), granulites (of high alumina alkali basalt composition), and\n\nLOWER CRUST & MOHO WEST AFRICA\nHAGG ERTY &TOFT\n59\n\ngarnet anorthosites. For these xenoliths, specific gravity (SG)\nis directly proportional to FeO + MgO and inversely proportional\nto alkalies and to SiOz. Seismic P-wave velocities for the low\nMg eclogites and the granulites, estimated from SG (6), show a\nrange from 6.6 to 8.7 km/sec with a transitional group between\ntypical crustal and mantle values which is comprised of both\nlithologies (Fig. 2). Magnetic susceptibilities and NRM values\nof the granulites are shown as functions of SG, wt% FeO, and wt%\nSiOz in Fig. 3 ; assuming that Vp , SG, and FeO increase and Si02\ndecreases with depth then the induced and remanent magnetizations\nincrease in intensity within the lower crust (7). Ferromagnetism\nmay persist within the upper mantle to 90 km depth ( 3 ) , or to\nshallower depths (-70 km) if upper mantle\'metasomatism induces\nrelatively oxidized horizons (8). P-T estimates and a geothermal\ngradient derived for the eclogites and granulites ( 9 ) lie between\nthe gradients commonly assumed for cratonic surface heat flows\n( - 4 0 mW/m2 ) and typical rift environments ( * 90 mW/m2 ) .\nGranulites, eclogites, and garnet anorthosite xenoliths from\nthe West African Craton appear to be petrologically, geochemically, and geophysically related, although some show evidence of\nsubsolidus reduction and decomposition, metasomatism, and partial\nmelting. The continuum in bulk chemistry and P-wave velocity\nstrongly implies that the lower crust - upper mantle boundary\nbetween 40 and 70 km (Fig. 2 ) is not a simple petrological discontinuity but is instead at least in part an intercalated\ngranulite-eclogite transition zone that may have resulted from\nigneous fractionation, metamorphism, and partial melt underplating in a developing continental lithosphere. From diamond\ninclusion evidence, the subcratonic lithosphere is dominantly\nultramafic and is inferred to be at least 200 km thick and\nArchean in age, an age in accord with the oldest surface rocks of\nthe West African Craton. Felsic rocks form a minor component of\nthe lower crust of this craton, and hydrated mineralogies are\nclearly superimposed or are the by-product of re-equilibration.\nHence the continental lower crust of the Man Shield is dominantly\nanhydrous, mafic, and reduced in redox state.\nReferences: (1) Rogers, N . W . (1977) Nature 270, 681;\n(2) Rollinson, H.R. (1981) Lithos 14,225; ( 3 ) Haggerty, S . E .\nand Toft, P.B. (1985) Science 229, 647; (4) Haggerty, S.E.\n(1983) N. Jb. Min. Mh. 8, 375; (5) Haggerty, S.E. (1987) In:\nMantle Xenoliths, Ed. P.H. Nixon, Wiley, 671; (6) Hall, J.\n(1986) In: Dawson, J.B., et al., Geol. SOC. Spec. C. Jacob\nTacy\nAuthorship in alphabetical order\n\nE.\n\n1)\n2)\n\n, K.\n\nMordenl, R. Reddi2\n\n61\n\n, P.\n\nGeology Department, Hope College. Holland, Michigan\nDepartment of Applied Geology, University o f Madras, Madras\n\nCharnockitic rocks were first described by Sir Thomas Holland\n(1900) from t h e hills around the village o f Pallavaram south of\nMadras.\nIn this area a series of acid t o intermediate magmas\nintruded an interbedded sequence of predominatly pelitic and mafic\nrocks which were latter metamorphosed t o high grade (Subramaniam,\n1959). According\nto\nWeaver (1980) chemical trends in the\ncharnockites indicate a period o f metasomatism and partial melting\nimmediately preceeding the granulite-facies metamorphism which h e\nsuggests was due to an influx o f C02-rich\nfluids. On the other\nhand, Bhattacharya and Sen (1986) concluded that n o pervasive\nfluid was present during the high-grade metamorphism\nwhich\ninvolved internal buffering of fluids and dehydration melting.\nThey based their conclusions\nlargely\non\ncalculations\nof\nmetamorphic water activities which are different for different\nrock types and show systematic variations with mineral chemistry.\nWe have examined rocks from a quarry southeast of Pallavaram for\nevidence indicating the concentration o f carbon-dioxide\nin the\nmetamorphic-fluid phase.\nCharnockitic rocks are the major rock type exposed in t h e\nquarry. These rocks are cut by coarse-grained dykes and veins\nalso made up of dark charnockite. Mafic granulites occur as\nenclaves. One especially large mafic enclave contains light\ncolored veins made up of calcite and scapolite with smaller\namounts of diopside and quartz. Garnets are concentrated at the\nmargins of these veins.\nThe dark host rock has a granulitic\ntexture and is made up of hornblende, diopside, plagioclase and\nquartz with sporadic garnet.\nResults o f EDS electron-microprobe analyses on minerals from\nthe mafic host and calcite-bearing veins are given in Table 1.\nW . D . S . analyses of the scapolite indicate only small amounts (less\nthan 0.1 wtX) of sulfur or chlorine. The mineral assemblage at\nt h e edges o f the vein allow us to estimate the C 0 2 concentration\nin the metamorphic fluid using the reaction:\nmeionite + 5 calcite+ 3 quartz = 3 grossular + CO2.\nCalculations using the thermodynamic data of Holland and Powell\n(1985) indicate a nearly pure C O 2 fluid under the metamorphic\ntemperatures (750-800OC) and pressures (6.5 - 7.5 kbars) obtained\nfor the area by Bhattacherya and Sen (1986).\nCarbonic fluid inclusions are abundant in a sample of one o f\nthe coarse-grained charnockitic veins collected near the mafic\nenclave. The vein is roughly granitic in composition containing\nthe assemblage alkali-feldspar, plagioclase, quartz, orthopyroxene\nand opaques. The fluid inclusions occur in planar arrays and are\nhence secondary or pseudosecondary. Melting temperatures obtained\non these inclusions are all within l0C o f the melting temperatures\no f a pure C 0 2 standard. Thus the fluid is nearly pure C 0 2\nalthough small (up to about 20%) amounts o f water may be present\n\nEvidence for C02-rich Fluids\nHansen, E , Hunt, W , Jacob, S C , firden, K , Reddi, R. , TaCY, p.\n.\n..\n.\n62\n\nTable 1\n\n-\n\nGarnet\nVein\nHost Rock\nSi02\nA1203\nFeO\nMnO\nMgO\n\nCaO\nNa20\nTotal\n\n37.6\n21.5\n27.4\n1.3\n2.3\n\nMineral Compositions\nDiopside\nHost Rock\nVein\n50.5\n2.3\n\n16.8\n\n10.8\n\n48.3\n2.5\n24.1\n\n8.9\n21.9\n\n37.7\n21.0\n25.9\n0.8\n1.5\n13.3\n\n21.7\n\nSi\n\n2.94\n\n2.99\n\n1.94\n\n1.99\n1.79\n\n1.96\n1.72\n0.06\n0.18\n1.13\n\n0.10\n0.54\n\n1.92\n0.12\n0.80\n\n0.52\n0.90\n\n0.27\n0.93\n\n0.09\n0.26\n0.91\n\n42.9\n30.2\n\n49.7\n\n20.4\n\n15.1\n\n33.1\n\n4.5\n\nA1\nFe\nMn\nMg\nCa\nNa\n\nScapolite Plag\nVein\nHost Rock\n\n6.59\n5.47\n\n2.26\n\n1.77\n\n3.37\n\n0.74\n\n0.47\n\n0.19\n\nas a thin, undetected, immiscible layer against the walls of t h e\nincgusions. Homogenization temperatures cluster between -$C\nand\n-18 C a n d hence have specific volume between 4 3 and 4 5 cm /mole.\nIsochores for those two volumes, calculated with the equations of\nTouret and Bottinga (1979) are given in Figure 1. The box in the\nfigure outlines the metamorphic conditions for the area deduced b y\nBhattacharya and Sen (1986). Isochores for the denser fluid\ninclusions pass through this box.\nIf small amounts of water a r e\npresent as an immiscible fluid, then the isochores i n Figure 1\nshould be moved to slightly higher pressures a n d this would\nincrease the overlap with the metamorphic conditions.\n\nFigure 1\n\n+oo\n-\n\nL\n\n600\n\nT empcraturr (\'C)\n\nb\n\n800\n\nEvidence for C02-rich Fluids\nHansen, E., Hunt, w., Jacob, S.W., MOrden, K . , Reddi, R., Tacy, P.\n\n63\n\nThe fluid inclusions indicate that a dense COB-rich fluid was\npresent at some point in the history of the charnockite. It is\ndifficult to see how the mineral assemblages in the charnockite or\nits igneous precursor could have generated CO2, hence i t probably\nflowed in from the outside. The densities of these fluids are\napproximately consistent with entrapment at peak metamorphic\nconditions as would be predicted by the COB-influx model of Weaver\n(1980).\nHowever, high densities are no guarantee that the fluid\nactually represents the peak metamorphic fluid nor is the presence\nof COB-rich fluids necessarily incompatible with other models of\ngranulite-facies metamorphism (Crawford and Hollister, 1986).\nAn\ninflux of C02 should lead to "carbonated" mineral\nassemblages in rocks of the appropriate bulk compositions. This\nmay be the case with the carbonate-bearing veins in the mafic\nenclave, although i t is by no means certain that the veins are\nmetasomatic. The sporadic occurrence of garnet in the host rock\nwithout scapolite or calcite suggests lower C02 fugacities than in\nthe veins and hence indicates some heterogenity in the metamorphic\nfluid phase. According to Subramaniam (personal communication,\n1983) wollastonite-bearing veins had also been found in the mafic\nenclaves in this quarry. Wollastonite is not stable in the\npresence of a CO2-rich fluid during granulite facies conditions\n(Valley, 1985) and hence its presence may\nindicate\nlarge\nheterogenites in the metamorphic fluid. Unfortunately, we were\nunable to locate any of the wollastonite-bearing veins and\nhave little indication of how they fit into the metamorphic\nhistory of the area.\nWe have evidence that a dense COB-rich fluid was once\npresent in the rocks exposed in the type charnockite area around\nPallavaram. This gives some support to the idea proposed by\nWeaver (1980) that the granulite-facies metamorphism in this area\nwas due to the influx of a COB-rich fluid. However, important\nquestions still remain about the timing of CO2 influx, the\npervasiveness of the CO2-rich fluid, and its source. Thus, for\nexample, our results are also consistent with a model in which the\nof\nthe granulite-facies metamorphism occurred through\nbulk\ndehydration melting accompanied or followed by some localized\nmigration of CO2-rich fluids. This is a model very similar to the\none proposed by Bhattacharya and Sen (1986). More information\nwill be needed, especially about the thermal history of the area\nand stable isotope compositions, before the role of the COB-rich\nfluid can be resolved.\nReferences\nBhattacharya and Sen (1986) Journ.
