J. Henry, Department of Geology and Geophysics,\nState University, Baton Rouge, Louisiana 70803 USA\n\nLouisiana\n\nINTRODUCTION. Although C1-rich minerals have been recognized\nto develop in a number of petrologic environments ranging from\nsubmarine hydrothermal vents C 1 1 to late mafic pegmatitic dikes\nand Pt-rich horizons in layered magmatic intrusives C2,31, they\nare most commonly found in granulite facies rocks. I t is probably\nthe earlier work o n the high grade terrains of South India that\nmost clearly demonstrated this Ce.g.\n4,5,6,7,81. Nonetheless,\nmost of the investigations o n C1-rich minerals from granulites\ndealt primarily with their chemical characterization but have not\nconsidered their petrologic implications. Clearly, before fully\nunderstanding granulite facies metamorphism the role of C1 in\ngranulites must b e explored.\nThere are three factors that influence the incorporation of\nC1 into hydrous silicates and phosphates: fluid composition,\ntemperature and crystallochemical constraints.\nI t has been\nexperimentally confirmed that a s chlorinity and acidity in a n\naqueous fluid increase and/or a s temperature increases, the C1\nconcentrations in the coexisting micas also increase C9,lOI.\nExperimental studies have also established that the compositional\nparameters (especially increasing ferrous Fe contents) that\nenable the hydroxyl site to approach ideal hexagonal symmetry in\nhydrous silicates strongly favor C1 incorporation Ell].\nWith these factors in mind, the hydrous minerals of the\ngranulite facies ironstones from the Beartooth Mountains, Montana\nare considered in this investigation.\nBACKGROUND. The eastern portion o f the Beartooth Mountains,\ncomposed\npredominantly of 2800 Ma granitic\nto\ntonalitic\ngranitoids, gneisses and migmatites, contain enclaves of various\nsupracrustal\nlithologies t h a t range in size f r o m a f e w cm to a\nfew k m C 2 .\n1 1\nThe supracrustal lithologies, including pelitic\nschists,\nfelsic\ngneisses,\namphibolites,\nmafic\ngneisses,\nquartzites and ironstones, typically display mineral assemblages\nthat are indicative of granulite facies metamorphism C131.\nApplication of a series of geothermobarometers indicate that the\npeak metamorphism took place at pressures of 5-6 kbar and\ntemperatures of 750-800 C. Some of the lithologies are partiallyto-completely\nreset\nby\na subsequent\namphibolite\nfacies\nmetamorphism that is attributed to the major magmatic event at\n2800 Ma. The Rb-Sr\nisotopic systematics on the supracrustal\nlithologies\nproduce\na\npoorly\nconstrained\nisochron\nof\napproximately 3400 Ma that has been interpreted a s the time of\nthe granulite facies metamorphism C 2 .\n1 1\nMINERALOGY A N D PETROLOGY OF THE IRONSTONES. The iron-rich\nmetasedimentary rock of the eastern Beartooth Mountains is a\ntypical\nquartz-magnetite\nbanded\niron formation with\nbulk\ncompositions ranging from 47 to 61 weight % SiO,,\n30 to 40 % F e O\n\nCL-RICH MINERALS IN GRANULITES\nHenry, D. J.\n68\n\nand 1 to 3 % Ale?O=. The bulk compositions and REE abundance5\nindicate an origin of the ferruginous sediment on a continental\nshelf rather than a eugeoclinal depositional environment C l 2 3 .\nThe mineral assemblages of the ironstones are, in general,\nsimilar to other granulite facies ironstones C 1 4 3 w i t h the most\ncommon assemblage being quartz + magnetite + ferrohypersthene +\nalmandine + clinopyroxene. In addition, there are trace or minor\namounts of a blue-green amphibole and dark brown biotite that are\nfound a s both inclusions and matrix phases. A s such, they are\ninterpreted as having been involved in the prograde metamorphism\nof the ironstones. Very minor amounts of cummingtonite after\nferrohypersthene and actinolite after clinopyroxene localized\nalong later fractures indicate the subsequent amphibolite facies\nmetamorphism d i d not significantly affect the ironstones.\nBased on calculations of coexisting mineral compositions i t\nis estimated that the fluid phase has an X(HEO)<0.3 and is\nrelatively oxidizing (near the NNO buffer).\n"Primary" fluid\ninclusions are apparently CO,-rich\nsuggesting CO, is the dominant\ncomponent in the fluid phase.\nBoth the biotites and amphiboles found in these ironstones\nhave some of the highest C1 levels that have been documented to\ndate (with the biotites and amphiboles in the matrix containing\nmore C1 than the biotite and amphibole inclusions in garnet and\nferrohypersthene). The biotites not only contain up to 2 9 wt %\n.\nC1 (22 % of the OH site), but also have substantial amounts of Ba\n(up to 10.5 wt%) and Ti (up to 6.9 wt % )\n(see Table 1 ) .\nThe\namphiboles contain up to 2.8 wt X C1 (40 % of the total OH site)\nand range in composition from a ferroan pargasite to a Cl-rich\npotassium hastingsite (see Table 1 ) . In these minerals there is a\ngeneral positive correlation among C1 levels and Fe, Ba and K (ia\namphiboles). These trends are in accordance with the expected\ncrytallochemical controls of C1 incorporation.\nNot all o f the C1 variations, however, are purely controlled\nb y the crystallochemical constraints. Based on the C1 contents of\nthe biotites, the relative f(H,O)/f(HCl) ratios C 1 0 3 range from\n0.02 to 1 suggesting that some o f the biotite variability must\nresult from changes in the local fluid compositions. These\ncalculations indicate the that the amount of HC1 in the fluid\nmust be substantial and are probably only attained in an aqueous\nfluid. Furthermore, the increasing C1-enrichment of the matrix\nbiotite and amphibole relative to the included biotite and\namphibole\nsuggest\nan increasing\nchlorinity\nwith\ngrade.\nNonetheless, based on fluid calculations and fluid inclusion work\nit is known that the dominant fluid is a C0,-rich\nfluid. Such a\nfluid will not support the h i g h levels of C1 necessary to produce\nthe elevated C1 contents in the silicates Ccf. 151. Consequently,\nwe must entertain the possibilty of an immiscible h i g h l y saline\nand acidic aqueous fluid coexisting with the dominant CO,\nfluid\ntypically found in granulites.\nIMPLICATIONS. Evidence for COet-brine immiscibility have\nbeen found in some medium grade metamorphic rocks Ce.9.\n15,161.\nHowever, this current study indicates that t h i s immiscible\nbehavior of fluids may extend into granulite facies conditions.\n\nCL-RICH MINERALS IN GRANULITES\n\nHenry, D. J.\n\n69\n\nThe presence of such a high grade immiscible fluid m a y have\nsignificant affects on the behavior of granulite fluids. The\naqueous brines may be preferentially absorbed on the surfaces o f\nminerals relative to the nonpolar CO, fluid phase resulting in\ndifferential movement o f the unmixed brine and C , fluids C173.\nO\nDue to its capacity to form complexes, C1-rich aqueous brines\nhave been implicated in the movement o f elements such a s Pt group\nelements, P b and rare earth elements C2,183.\nReferences, C 1 1 Vanko, D. Hollister, Department of Geological and\nGeophysical Sciences, Princeton University, Princeton, N J 08544\n\nThe discovery of pure CO2 fluid inclusions in granulite facies rocks\nstimulated models (1,2) attributing a causative role of C02 fluids to the\nformation of granulite facies rocks. The studies of Janardhan et al. (3) and\nHansen et al. ( 4 ) make a strong case that charnockites in south India w e r e\nformed by CO2 infiltration into gneiss; the argument i s primarily supported by\nthe occurrence in t h e charnockites of abundant, "pure" CO2 fluid inclusions.\nHansen et al. ( 4 ) and Newton (5) show t h a t a fluid in equilibrium with the\nmineral assemblages at the calculated metamorphic conditions would be\nC02-rich, and that the fluid inclusions not only a r e C02-rich but also have\ndensities appropriate for the metamorphic conditions.\nEven though the\ninclusions appear to be p u r e COz, a s much as 30 mole percent H 2 0 may be\npresent as an optically unresolvable film on the walls of the inclusions (4,6).\nThis allows some reconciliation of the results of the microthermometric data\nwith those of mineral equilibria calculations.\nReports of C o p r i c h fluid inclusions from parageneses for which HgO-rich\nfluids have been predicted, however, raise the possibility that the agreement\nbetween prediction and observation for the granulite facies terranes may be\ncoincidental.\nThere m a y be a common process which leads to formation of\nCO2-rich secondary inclusions i n metamorphic rocks. This possibility needs be\ntested b y well constrained studies of fluid inclusions a t all grades of\nmetamorphism and for metamorphic rocks of known tectonic setting.\nExamples of discordance of composition of fluid inclusions with predicted\ncomposition include the greenschist to amphibolite facies terrane of\nsouth-central Maine for which a n H20-rich synmetamorphic fluid has been\npredicted (7), leucosomes of migmatites for which X H ~ Oof the fluid phase had\nto be greater than 0.7 in order to have melt present at the reported P-T\nconditions (8), and graphite-bearing granulites for which calculations show\nthat C o p r i c h compositions a r e not in equilibrium with the metamorphic\nassemblages ( 9 , l O ) .\nFor the f i r s t two examples, Cog-rich inclusions with\ndensities appropriate for the metamorphic conditions have been reported\n(11,12,13).\nI n t h e third case, t h e CO2-rich inclusions have lower densities\nthan t h e fluids would have had at peak metamorphic conditions (9,lO). The\noccurrence and densities of the Cog-rich inclusions from the greenschist\nfacies rocks of southern Maine (11,12) are remarkably similar to those reported\nfor southern Karnataka, India (4).\nI n a study of fluid inclusions (14) across the retrograde orthoamphibole\nisograd i n the southern marginal zone of the Archean Limpopo belt of South\nAfrica (15), patterns of composition and density of inclusions were also found\nto be similar to those reported from other granulite facies terranes, most\nnotably Kerala, India (16):\na few apparently pure CO2 inclusions with\ndensities appropriate for the P-T conditions, many CO2 inclusions with lower\ndensities, and aqueous inclusions of variable salinity and containing no\ndetectable CO2.\nThe retrograde orthoamphibole isograd w a s apparently\nestablished by hydration of hot granulite facies rocks that had been t h r u s t\nover a low grade granite-greenstone terrane (17).\nDuring or shortly after\nthrusting, volatiles generated by post thrusting heating of the footwall\ngreenstones a r e hypothesized to have entered the granulite facies rocks of\nthe hanging wall, leading to the hydration of the immediately overlying\n\nC02-RICH FLUID INCLUSIONS\nHollister, L . S .\n\n71\n\ngranulites and establishment of the retrograde orthopyroxene isograd.\nMetamorphic conditions at the isograd require that the equilibrium fluid had\nthere an Xco2 of about 0.8. Our results suggest that the hydrating fluid may\nbe represented by secondary COZ-rich fluid inclusions, which may contain u p\nto 30 mole percent HzO.\nThe similarities of fluid inclusion populations a r e more striking than their\ndifferences for the above metamorphic terranes which have markedly different\nthermal histories. It appears, therefore, t h a t w e have s o m e way to go before\nwe can confidently relate, in all cases, entrapment of fluid inclusions t o peak\nmetamorphic conditions.\nThis is not to say that fluid inclusion research in\nmetamorphic rocks should not be pursued vigorously. Crawford and Hollister\n(18) review cases where fluid inclusions have been shown to be related to\npeak metamorphic conditions, Recently, Olsen ( 19) related fluid inclusions to\nconditions during anatexis.\nAnd a very productive use of studies of fluid\ninclusions in metamorphic rocks has been in constraining the post metamorphic\nexhumation histories of metamorphic terranes (16,20,21,22).\nREFERENCES\n(1) Janardhan A.S., Newton R.C. and Smith J.V. No. 7250, Akkulam,\nTrivandrum 695 03 1, INDIA.\n\nCarbon dioxide-rich fluid inclusions from the high grade terrane of\nSouth India have been cited as evidence for granulite metamorphism\nresulting from pervasive carbon dioxide flushing, possibly from a deep\nseated source. This study tests the model of external C02-buffering and\ninvestigates the source of the carbon dioxide.\nThe terrain is thought to be of Archean age, and is segmented by\nProterozoic shear zones. Samples of massive charnockites, precursor\namphibolite gneisses and gneiss-incipient charnockite pairs from eight\nquarries throughout the high grade region have been analysed and\nrepresentative results are shown in Table 1. Gas was extracted from fluid\ninclusions within quartz grains by a stepped heating technique\'.\nAll samples measured show similar and simple release patterns. A\nmaximum carbon dioxide release is found between 600\xc2\xb0C and 8OO0C, which\nis characterised by the isotopically heaviest carbon, ranging between\n-12"A and -7"A.\nOptical fluid inclusion studies (M. Santosh) show that the\nmajority of fluid inclusions in these samples rupture between 500\xc2\xb0C and\n800\xc2\xb0C confirming them as the source for the analysed carbon dioxide.\n\nStable Isotope Studies\nJackson, D.H., Santosh, M., Mattey, D.P., Harris, N.B.W.\n73\n\nThe data when plotted on figure 1 illustrate that no systematic\nisotopic variation can be seen between gneiss and incipient charnockite as\nfound in Kabbaldurga and Ponmudi. Furthermore massive charnockite which\nis exposed on a regional scale as in Madras or the Nilgiris has similar\nisotopic characteristics to incipient charnockite.\nHowever the data clearly show that in all gneiss-incipient\ncharnockite pairs quartz from the charnockite contains about three times\nmore carbon dioxide than quartz from the gneiss. In the case of\ncharnockites from South Kerala it is possible that some C02 results from\noxidation of the graphite, which is present in significant amounts.\nHowever in Kabbaldurga and Koddakad where no graphite or other source of\ncarbon is present fluid influx from an external source is the probable\nmechanism.\nThe uniformity of the d 3 C values of both gneisses and charnockites\n(averaging -10 f PAo)\nfrom a wide area of South India indicates either that\nexternally buffered C02 equilibrated with the gneiss or that the C 0 2 now\nin the incipient charnockites represents a redistribution of the C02 in the\nprecursor gneiss during charnockite formation. However we suggest that\nthe greater abundance of C02 in incipient charnockites is compliant with\nan externally buffered C02 source rather than a closed system process. It\nseems unlikely that the source of the C 0 2 can be wholly derived from\ncrustal carbon (i.e. carbonates OoA and organic derived carbon -20 to\n-30%,) because of the apparent isotopic uniformity of the fluid. The range\nof 8l 3C values for South Indian gneisses and charnockites are comparable\nto the composition of similar high pressure fluid inclusions preserved in\nupper mantle xenoliths (-8 to -14%0)2 suggesting that such fluids may\ncontain a significant mantle component. Many of the problems identified by\nthis study may be resolved by ongoing analyses which will determine the\ncarbon isotope characteristics of gneisses not associated with\ncharnockites, and also the carbon isotope characteristics from fluid\ninclusions within charnockite phases critical to granulite formation such\nas biotiite and pyroxene.\nReferences cited:\n\n1 Santosh M. et al. OBP. U.K.\n/\n\n9 8\n\nThe Archaean Closepet Granite ( M 2500 Ma) is a Polyphase\nbody intruding the Peninsular Gneiss Complex and the associated\nsupracrustal rocks. The granite out-crop runs for nearly 5 0 0 km\nwith an approximate width of 20-25 km and cut across the regional\nmetamorphic structure passing from granulite facies in the South\nand green schist facies in the north. In the amphibolitegranulite facies transition zone the granite is intimatly mixed\nwith migmatites and charnockite. Field observations suggests\nthat anatexis of Peninsular gneisses led to the formation of\ngranite melt, and there is a space relationship between migmatite\nformation, charnockite development and production and emplacement\nof granite magma.\nBased on texture and cross cutting relationships four major\ngranite phases are recognised. Relationships are not consistant\nfrom quarry to quarry, however, there is a general evolutionary\ntrend ranging from an early granodiorite to late granite. The\nchronological sequence of emplacement of major granite phases are\nas follows\n1.\n\n2.\n3.\n4.\n\nPyroxene bearing dark grey granite\nPorphyritec granite\nEquigranular grey granite\nEquigranular pink granite\n\nAdditionally there are small areas of \'K\' and \'Na\' rich\nrocks such as brick red rocks ( 9 . 7 % K 0) and albitite ( 1 1 . 6 %\nNa 0). Field and geochemical featureg suggests that they could\non$y have arisen by extensive metasomatism.\nThe granite is medium to coarse grained and exhibit\nhypidiomorphic granular to porphyritic texture. The modal\ncomposition varies from granite granodiorite to quartz monzonite.\nWhere the order of crystallization is deduced, biotite generally\nforms an early phase in the melt followed by plagioclase and\nquartz or quartz followed by plagioclase. Though K-feldspar\ngenerally a late phase begin to crystallize, still there was\nsufficient space for it to crystallize as subhedral phenocrysts.\nAmphibole is also an important mafic phase which is quite unstable\nbreaking down in to biotite symplectites. Textural evidence\nsuggests that part of the amphibole crystallized from the melt\n\nSOUTHERN CLOSEPET GRANITE\nJayananda, et a1\n78\n.\xe2\x80\x99. i A\nClinopyroxene occurs only in dark grey granite, where it is\ninterstitial to late phase and textural evidence supports\nprimary igneous origin. The accessories such as zircon may be\nderived and apatite, allanite and sphene are the early phases\nin the melt.\nGeochemical variation of the granite suite is consistant\nwith either fractional crystallization or partial melting, but\nin both the cases biotite + feldspar must be involved as fractionating or residual phases during melting to account trace\nelement chemistry. The trace element data has been plotted on\ndiscriminant diagrams, where majority of samples plot in volconic arc and within plate, tectonic environments. However,\nfield observations suggest a within plate environment likely\nto have prevailed during the evolution of the granite. When\nthe calculated mesonormative minerologies (qtz-plag-k-feld) are\nplotted on phase diagrams, they suggest the derivation of\ngranite by equilibrium fusion (batch melting) of the Peninsular\ngneisses. A quantitative trace element modelling has been\ntested. The trace element modelling suggests that partial\nmelting to certain extent fractional crystallization were in\noperation during the evolution of the granite suite.\nThe granite show distinct REE patterns with variable total\nREE content. Textural evidence argues that large fraction of\nREE resides in accessory phases such as zircon, apatite, allanite and sphene. The REE abundances observed indicate no\nevidence for progressively more fractionated REE patterns from\ngranodiorite to granite. The dark grey granite contains high\ntotal REE and show coherent patterns without any significant\nEu anomalies. The Porphyritic pink granite exhibits variable\ntotal REE and fractionated patterns without any significant\nEu anomalies. The porphyritic grey and equigranular grey\ngranite with variable total REE show HREE enrichment, and negative Eu anomalies. The equigranular pink granite with variable\ntotal REE show slight LREE enrichment and negligible Eu anomalies. The REE patterns and overall abundances suggests that\nthe granite suite ltepresents a product of partial melting of\ncrustal source in which fractional crystallization operated\nin a limited number of cases.\n\nLATE ARCHEAN GREENSTONE TECTONICS -- EVIDENCE FOR THERMAL AND THRUSTLOADING LITHOSPHERIC SUBSIDENCE FROM STRATIGRAPHIC SECTIONS IN THE SLAVE\nPROVINCE, CANADA; W.S.F. Kidd, Dept. Geol. Sci., SUNYA, Albany, NY 12222, USA,\nT.M. Kusky, Dept. Earth Sci., Johns Hopkins Univ., Baltimore, MD 21218, USA,\nD.C. Bradley, LDGO, Palisades, NY 10964, USA\nSubsidence in rifts and passive continental margins is driven by stretching\nand subsequent cooling and thickening of the lithosphere(1); subsidence in\nforeland trough basins is a result of thrust-loading and flexure of the\nlithosphere(2). Sediment sequences localized by these different mechanisms\nhave distinctive sequences of facies and are one of the more convincing forms\nof evidence for the operation of plate tectonics in the Palaeozoic and\nProterozoic. Archean examples of such sequences have not been so obvious,\nperhaps because none are preserved in little deformed state, and the most\nlikely candidates on the basis of lithological assemblages for passive margin\nsequences ( 3 . 4 ) are not only highly deformed and structurally dismembered but\nare also strongly metamorphosed. We have identified intact stratigraphic\nsections of passive margin type, and others of foreland trough-type in\ndeformed but low-grade Archean rocks in several extremely well-exposed areas\nof the Slave Province, N.W.T., Canada. These sequences are similar in most\nrespects to younger examples and support the hypothesis ( 5 , 6 , 7 ) that tectonic\nprocesses similar to those operating now were active in the Archean.\nOn both the north and south arms of Point Lake an intact stratigraphic\nsequence above a thick conglomerate containing shallow-water arenites and\nintercalated mafic and felsic volcaniclastics and volcanics starts with a\nunit of black pyritiferous slate and argillite of 60-200 m present thickness.\nThis contains a minor proportion of siderite iron formation, calcarenitesiltite and limestone breccia beds and, adjacent to the volcanics at the\nbase, local quartzose and tuffaceous silts and arenites. These black slates\nare conformably succeeded by a thick sequence of quartzofeldspathic turbidite\ngreywackes and pelites, with local magnetite iron formation in some of the\npelitic intervals. The turbidites coarsen and thicken upward through the\nbasal 20-100 m. The overall sequence of turbidites is several k thick and\nm\nis imbricated by thrusts directed westwards. We interpret this sequence to\nbe the product of submarine thrust-loading subsidence, with the black slates\nbeing\n\nt h e outer\n\ntrench s l o p e d e p o s i t s and the t u r b i d i t e s t h e trench f l o o r\n\ndeposits, subsequently incorporated into an accretionary thrust stack.\nSections in the Cameron River belt, in the area of Upper Ross Lake-Victory\nLake-Detour Lake, lie with observed unconformity on tonalitic gneisses. At\nDetour Lake, deformed but low grade sediments consisting largely of quartz\nand carbonate arenites, with a basal biotite phyllite matrix conglomerate and\na local upper unit of calcsilicates and marbles, form a section about 500 m\nthick. Similar sediments are much thinner nearby, along strike, but 500 m of\nquartzites in the same tectonic position are reported ( 8 ) in the Beaulieu River\nregion farther north. These shallow water sediments, which we interpret as a\npassive margin sequence, are truncated by a major thrust carrying greenstone\nbelt lithologies generally southwestward, except near Detour Lake, where the\nsection passes up through a 100 m thick interval of pelite and iron formation\nto a quartzofelspathic turbidite sequence, of overall great thickness, and\nprobably cut by many thrusts. This change to turbidite deposition we also\ninterpret as the development of a submarine foreland-basin caused by thrustloading.\n\nLATE ARCHEAN GREENSTONE TECTONICS\nW. S F. Kidd et a l .\n.\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nSequences like these are not unique to the Slave Province but the\nrecognition of their significance depends on good outcrop and being able to\nidentify which contacts are stratigraphic and which are important faults.\nSimilar passive margin-type sequences are well documented from Zimbabwe (9)\nand the Superior Province (10); in both cases the sediments lie unconformably\non older basement and are only a maximum of a hundred to a few hundred meters\nthick. We interpret adjacent volcanics, including komatiites in Zimbabwe, to\nbe in tectonic contact with the sediments, as they are observed to be in the\nSlave Province. Within the Archean, the Witwatersrand basin has been\nsuggested to be a foreland basin due to thrust-loading subsidence (11); within\nArchean greenstone belts, the only documented thrust-loading subsidence\nsequence is that of the Barberton Xtn Land (12); they are probably common,\nand several potential examples, which we have not yet had the opportunity to\nexamine, occur elsewhere in the Slave Province (data in 13). The change\nbetween the Bababudan and Chitradurga Groups (14) of southern India, and much\nof the sedimentation in the Chitradurga Group, is possibly of the same origin.\nThe thrust-subsidence sequences in the Slave Province are very similar in\nfacies and thickness to Phanerozoic examples, particularly those from places\nwhere island arc terranes dominate, for example central Newfoundland (15).\nThe combined effect of lithospheric thickness and the size of the load provided\nby the thrust stack must therefore have resembled the same combination of\nthese two factors in more recent times. In contrast, the passive margin\nsequences, while of very similar lithologies in similar order to those in\nyounger examples, are consistently thin in comparison with them, even allowing\nfor thickness reduction by ductile strain. This difference can be interpreted\nin at least two different ways; one is that a higher mantle heat production\ncaused slower lithospheric thickening and a smaller total equilibrium thickness\nafter a rifting event, resulting in less overall thermal subsidence of rifted\nmargins. Another is that rifted margins were more swiftly incorporated into\nconvergent tectonic systems than in later times. Unless independent evidence\non lithospheric thickness can be obtained from other aspects of the Archean\nrecord or these sequences can be much more precisely dated than at present, it\nmay prove difficult to distinguish between these possibilities. This evidence\ndoes show, however, that tectonic processes active in the Archean had primary\neffects on the lithosphere indistinguishable from those of present plate\ntectonics.\nReferences \xe2\x80\x98(1) XcKenzie, D., (1978) Earth Planet. Its history\nspans the interval from 3.5 Gal the age of \'old gray gneisses\' exposed\n.\n2.5 Gal the\nin an anticlinal culmination in the Wopmay Orogen, to 2 6\nage of major granitic plutonism throughout the province. Most of the\nvolcanic and sedimentary rocks formed in the 2.7\n2.6 Ga interval.\nTraditional tectonic models for the Slave treat the Province as one\nrecording continental extension, with the volcanics and sediments\nfilling normal fault bounded linear troughs developed on pre-existing\nsiallic crust (eg., 1-6). Hoffman ( 7 , 8 ) and Kusky (9,10,11) have\nrecently pointed out major problems with applying a continental rift\nmodel to the Slave Province. The regional geology is described here in\nthe light of a collisional tectonic model in which different belts in\nthe province are regarded as accreted terranes whose suturing formed the\nArchean Slave Province. Alt.hough the model is preliminary and largely\nspeculative, it explains many aspects of the geology of the province\nthat are ignored or contradicted by the continental rift model, and it\nserves as a testable hypothesis on which future field efforts may be\nfocused\nFigure 1 is a cartoon terrane map of the Slave Province; it was\nconstructed by first taking lithological maps of the province (2, 12,\n131, graphically removing relatively young granitic intrusive rocks, and\nthen extrapolating contacts between regions that have not been removed\nby granites. Field work in the 1985, 1986, and 1987 seasons\nconcentrated on determining the nature of terrane boundaries\n(particularly in the western and central terranes), kinematics of major\nmovement zones, and characterizing rock suites of different terranes.\nExtrapolation was aided by SEASAT orbital radar images processed in the\nLaboratory for Terrestrial Physics at NASA\'s Goddard Space Flight Center\nand by maps prepared by the Geological Survey of Canada and the Geology\nDivision of the Department of Indian and Northern Affairs, Canada. On\nthe terrane map the Slave is divided into four major tectonic zones with\ndifferent characteristics and ages; from west to east these are here\nnamed the Anton Terrane, the Sleepy Dragon Terrane, The Contwoyto\nTerrane, and the Hackett River Terrane. The characteristics of and\ndifferences between the terranes is discussed in more detail elsewhere\n\n-\n\n-\n\n.\n\n(14).\n\nThe Anton Terrane stretches from Yellowknife in the southern portion\n\nof the province to Anialiak River in the north (Figure 11, and it hosts\nsome of the oldest ages reported from the Slave Province. Included is a\n3 . 4 8 Ga tonalltic gneiss exposed in an anticlinal culmination in the\nWopmay Orogen west of Point Lake (S. Bowring, pers. comm.), and a 3.1 Ga\nage on granitoid rocks from a diatreme near Yellowknife ( 1 5 ) .\nQuartzofeldspathic gneisses are widely distributed throughout the Anton\nTerrane, and these are worthy of intensive geochronologic studies to\ndetermine if even older rocks are present. To the northwest of\nYellowknife and southwest of Point Lake a series of mafic volcanic and\nmetasedimentary rocks are preserved; their relationships to surrounding\nrocks are not clearly understood, although a similar suite of rocks\nimmediately southwest of Point Lake is bounded on all sides by mylonites\nand is clearly a klippe. The Anton Terrane is interpreted as the\nremnants of an older Archean continent or microcontinent.\nThe Sleepy Dragon Terrane includes quartzofeldspathic gneissic\ncomplexes such as the 2 . 8 - 2 . 7 Ga Sleepy Dragon Complex in the south, and\na 3.1 Ga chloritic granite on Point Lake. The gneisses are locally\nI\'.\n\nAccretion of the Slave Province\nTim Kuskv\n88\n\nFigure 1. Terrane map of the Slave province with young granitic rocks\nremoved. Bold arrows indicate approximate transport directions, thin lines\nwith arrows show fold axial planes and vergence. YK = Yellowknife, PL =\nPoint Lake, AR = Anialiak River, HB = Hope Bay.\n\nAccretion of the Slave Province\nT i m Kuskv\n\noverlain by shallow water sedimentary sequences with strong affinities\nto Phanerozoic passive margin sequences (19). The gneisses together\nwith mafic greenstone belts of oceanic affinity (11) are presently\ndisposed in a westward verging fold and thrust belt. The Sleepy Dragon\nTerrane could thus simply be an imbricated and westward transported\nsection of the Anton Terrane, or it could be a separate accreted\nmicrocontinent.\nRocks of the Contwoyto Terrane consist almost entirely of graywacke\nturbidites (disregarding the intrusive granites), disposed in a series\nof westward verging folds and thrusts (15,161. At Point Lake these\ngrade down into a flexural loading sequence related to westward directed\nthrusting (19). Greenstone belts within the accretionary ~ o m p l e xare\ninterpreted as oceanic material scraped off an eastward dipping\nsubduction zone.\nThe Hackett River Terrane consists of a series of northwest\nstriking intermediate, felsic and mafic volcanic belts along with some\ngranitic and gneissic rocks in the south. Caldera complexes and\ntransitions from subaerial to subaqueous volcanic deposits are locally\npreserved (15). These volcanic belts differ significantly from\ngreenstone belts to the west which consist primarily of mafic volcanic\nand plutonic rocks (12). The Hackett River Terrane is interpreted as an\nisland arc formed above an east dipping subduction zone, with the\nContwoyto Terrane representing an accretionary complex located in the\nforearc position.\nThe formation of the Slave Province can thus be simply explained by\nan island arc (Hackett River Terrane) and forearc accretionary prism\n(Contwoyto Terrane) moving westward above an east dipping subduction\nzonel which collided with and partially overrode an older continent\n(Anton Terrane).\n(1) Henderson, J . (1986) J.Geol,\n, p.129-143.\n\nN89-22221\nDEEP CRUSTAL DEFORMATION BY SHEATH FOLDING IN\nTHE ADIRONDACK MTS., U.S.A.\nJ.M. McLelland, Colgate University\nAs described by McLelland and Isachsenl, the southern\nhalf of the Adirondacks are underlain by major isoclinal\n(F1) and open-upright (F2) folds whose axes are parallel,\ntrend approx. E-W, and plunge gently about the horizontal.\nThese large\nstructures (50-100 km along strike) are\nthemselves folded by open upright folds trending NNE (F3).\nMcLelland2 pointed out that elongation lineations in these\nrocks are parallel to X of the finite strain ellipsoid\ndeveloped during progressive rotational strain.\nThese\nlinear elements are most spectacular in ribbon gneisses\nconsisting of quartz and feldspar ribbons up to 60 cm long,\n1 cm wide, and 1-2 mm in thickness.\nThe ribbons can be\nshown to\nevolve from\nprogressively sheared feldspar\nmegacrysts as well as aggregates of quartz grains, both\nindigenous to inequigranular granitic plutonites.\nThe parallelism between F1 qnd F2 fold axes and\nelongation lineations led McLelland\nto hypothesize that\nprogressive\nrotational\nstrain,\nwith a west-directed\ntectonic\ntransport,\nrotated\nearlier\nF1-folds\ninto\nparallelism\nwith\nthe\nevolving elongation lineation.\nRotation is accomplished by ductile, passive flow of F1axes into extremely arcuate, E-W hinges, i.e., sheath\nfolds. F2 folds represent either response to convergence\nin the ductile flow field or are the crests and troughs of\nlarge sheath folds with which they are contemporaneous.\nIn order to test these hypotheses a number of large\nfolds were mapped in the eastern Adirondacks. The largest\nof these (McLelland and Isachsenl) lies just south of the\nMarcy anorthosite massif and is referred to as the F2,\nPharoah Mt. anticline. This anticline has a wavelength of\n20 km and plunges gently to the east at its eastern end.\nThe charnockites coring the anticline may be followed for\nat least 50 km to the east and reappear in the Ticonderoga\n10 km to the east. On the flanks of the anticline\ndome\nare distinctive marbles of the Paradox Lake Formation whose\ncontact with the charnockites gives clear expression to the\nAs these marbles are followed north or south\nanticline.\nfrom the anticlinal hinge they can be traced around\nvertical isoclinal (Fa) fold hinges that occur on both\nflanks of the F2 anticline. The only way for this geometry\nto be consistent is if the Pharoah Mt. anticline is a\nflattened sheath fold with horizontal hinges that are\nisoclinal.\n\n-\n\n-\n\nSHEATH FOLDS\n\nMcLelland, J.M.\n94\n\nOther evidence supporting the existence of sheath\nfolds in the Adirondacks is the presence, on a map scale,\nof synforms whose limbs pass through the vertical and into\nantiforms. This type of outcrop pattern is best explained\nby intersecting a horizontal plane with the double\ncurvature of sheath folds.\nIt is proposed that sheath folding is a common\nresponse of hot, ductile rocks to rotational strain at deep\ncrustal levels. At shallower levels the crust responds to\nthe same forces by developing thrust faults such as those\nmapped by McLelland\nand\nIsachsenl in\nthe eastern\nAdirondacks.\nThe development of sheath folds is probably\ncommonplace within the high grade cores of major mobile\nbelts. The presence of such structures should be suspected\nwhenever well developed elongation lineations parallel\nearly fold axes, especially when these are isoclinal. Of\nparamount importance are tectonic interpretations related\nto sheath folding, because, unless recognized as parts of\nsheaths, the isoclinal fold hinges may be misinterpreted as\nperpendicular to the long axis (X) of the finite strain\nellipsoid when, actually, they are parallel to it.\nThus,\nthe recognition of sheath\nfolds in the Adirondacks\nreconciles the E-W orientation of fold axes with an E-W\nelongation lineation.\nThese folds appear to have formed\nduring, or shortly prior\nto, peak\ngranulite facies\nmetamorphism at\n1050 Ma3.\nThey fold an earlier high\ngrade (garnet-sillimanite-K-feldspar4 foliation which is\nbelieved\nto pre-date\n-1300 Ma\nton litic gneiss.\nOrthogneisses emplaced\nat 1160-1130 Ma5 are clearly\neffected by the sheath folding. The Sacandaga Fm. that\nenvelopes the Piseco anticline of the southern Adirondacks\nis believed to be a mylonitized migmatite envelope around\nthe 1150 Ma3 granitic gneiss coring the anticline.\nThe\nmylonitization formed during sheath folding and ribbon\nlineation formation along the anticline.\n\n-\n\nl.McLelland, J. and Isachsen, Y. (1986) The Grenville\nProvince. Geol. Assoc. Can. Sp. Pap 31: 75-95.\n2.McLelland, J. (1984) J. Struc. Geol. 6:\n\n147-157.\n\n3.Chiarenzelli, J., McLelland, J., Bickford, M., Isachsen,\nY., Whitney, P. (1987) Geol. SOC. Am. Abstracts with\nPrograms 19: in press.\n\nN89-22222\ny-3\n\nU-PB ZIRCON GEOCHRONOLOGY AND EVOLUTION OF SOME\nADIRONDACK META-IGNEOUS ROCKS\nJ.M.\n\n-\n\n95\n\nI55 pJip3-,?\n\nMcLelland, Colgate University\n\nA total\nof 18\nnew U-Pb zircon ages from the\nanorthosite-mangerite-charnockite-granite~alaskite (AMCAL)suite of the Adirondacks yield the following results\n(Chiarenzelli et all) :\n\n(1) Emplacement\nages\nof\nthe\nmangeritic\nand\ncharnockitic rocks are constrained in the interval 11601130 Ma;\n(2) Hornblende granitic gneiss gives zircon ages of\n-1100 Ma but cores of -1150 Ma have been separated\nsuggesting that 1110 Ma represents a mixed age;\n( 3 ) Migmatitic alaskitic gneiss yields ages of -1070\nMa.\nZircons are clear and unzoned in these minimum melt\nmigmatites, suggesting that they grew during anatexis;\n\n(4) Zircons in anorthosites are small, equant, multifaceted, and clear similar to metamorphic zircons in mafic\ngranulites. These zircons yield ages of -1050 Ma and are\ninterpreted as metamorphic with Zr exsolved from Fe, Tioxides and/or pyroxenes.\n( 5 ) Sphene ages in the Adirondack Highlands occur in\nthe interval 1050-950 Ma and this is assumed to be the age\nof peak granulite facies metamorphism.\n\nThese results leave the age of the anorthositic rocks\nunresolved with the only direct determinati n being a Nd/Sm\nage of 1288+36 Ma by Ashwal and Wooden\nBased up0\napparently mutually cross-cutting relationships, McLelland 9\ninterpreted the anorthositic and mangeritic/charnockitic\nrocks as coeval.\nThis conclusion is consistent with the\nclose association of these rock types on a global scale, as\nwell as the repeated zonal envelopment of anorthositic\nmassifs by acidic rocks. That the acidic and mafic rocks\nconstitute a bimodal, n n-comagmatic suite has been shown\nby McLelland and whitneya on the basis of chemical data and\nfield relationships.\n\n.\n\nThe presence of xenocrysts of andesine in charnockite\n10-15 km away from the nearest anorthosite indicates that\nthe acidic rocks were largely liquid to at least t ese\ndistances when they acquired the xenocrysts. Hargraves\n\n9\n\nADIRONDACK META-IGNEOUS ROCKS\nJ .M. McLel1 and\n\nand Isachsen et ale6 have argued that the acidic rocks are\nolder gneisses melted by the intruding anorthosite slab\nwhich, upon solidification, was intruded by the stillmolten mangerites and charnockites. In order to test this\nhypothesis the heat flow equation was solved for a 4 km\nthick sill in a semi-infinite half space with grad. T =\n3OoC/km. A series of different initial conditions were\napplied, and it was found that even for the unrealistically\nextreme case of a totally liquid anorthosite (T=1300\xc2\xb0C)\nintruding into anhydrous granitic gneiss (TINIT\nmelting is limited to -55% at the contact anF%=9000c1\necreases\nquickly to 0% at 4 km above the sill. It is clear that the\ninitial and latent heat reservoirs of the anorthosite are\ninsufficient to produce the magmatic rocks of the AMCALsuite. In contrast to in situ anatexis by the anorthosite,\nit is a simple matter for gabbroic magmas ponded at the\ncrust-mantle interface to melt lower crustal rocks. This\nis because repeated influxes of differentiating mafic magma\ncan supply almost unlimited heat during differentiation\ntowards more feldspathic compositions.\nLower crustal\n(1)\nanatectites are liable to be high in K20 since:\northoclase is\na near-solidus phase in tonalitic and\ngranodioritic rocks, and (2) anhydrous minima in the Qt-AbOr system move away from Qt with increasing P. These Krich anatectites gather into batches and rise either as\ndiscrete plutons or as envelopes of acidic magmas about a\ncore of feldspathic gabbro (leuconorite?) whose plagioclase\ncumulates will give rise to anorthosites. This is the mode\nof origin envisaged for the Adirondack AMCAL suite.\nThe emplacement of the AMCAL suite appears to have\ntaken place under anorogenic conditions but was preceded by\na regional metamorphism of garnet-sillimanite-K-feldspar\ngrade and of unknown age.\nIn the southern Adirondacks\ntonalitic gneiss dated by U-Pb zircon methods are, at\nleast, 1320 Ma old and contain foliated xenoliths of\nmetasediment.\nAlong the St. Lawrence River foliated\nxenoliths are clearly evident in leucogranitic gneiss of\nthe Rockport pluton dated at 141556 Ma by U-Pb zircon\nmethods.\nThus the pre-AMCAL suite metamorphism may be\nolder than -1415 Ma.\nThe chemical signatures of these\nolder meta-igneous rocks are calcalkaline and orogenic.\nThe anorogenic AMCAL suite is evidently bracketed by\ncompressional orogenies.\n\nI\n\nADIRONDACK META- IGNEOUS ROCKS\nJ M. EOS 67, p. 407.\n\nN89- 2 2 2 2 5\nGEOPHYSICAL EVIDENCES FOR A TBICK CRUST SOUTE OF PAUXAT-TIRUCHI\nGAP IN TEE EIGE GRADE TERRAINS OF SOUTE 1NDIA;D.C.MISHRA\nNational\nGeophysical Research Institute, Hyderabad 500 007, India.\nThe Bouguer anomaly map of India presents a prominent low (30-40 mgls)\nover the southern part of the continent (....\nNGRI,\n\n1978) which coincides for\n\na consederable part with the exposed charnockites (Fig.la, Subrahmanyam and\nNorth of this \'low\' is an east-west, elongated \'high\' of\napproximately 20 mgals almost perpendicular to the regional strke in the\n\nVerma 1986).\narea.\n\nA fraction of these anomalies might be due t o t h e shallow features\n\nbut their l a r g e w a v e l e n g t h s suggest m a i n l y deep seated sources.\nSignificantly the gradient between these two anomalies coincide with the\nPalghat-Tiruchi line which I s a prominent shear zone (Naqvi and Rogers\n\n1987). The northern gradient of the Bouguer \'high\' coincides with the\nBhavani fault almost parallel to the Palghat-Tiruchi line which suggests its\nextension to a considerable depth.\n\nThe occurrences of anorthosite bodies on\n\neither side of this gravity \'high\' (Fig. la) also suggest a deep-seated\norigin for this anomaly.\n\nTopographically also, Palghat and Tiruchi depicts\n\ngaps in the western and eastern ghats respectively which might be\nmanifestation of deep-seated structures.\n\nA north-south profile across this \'high\' and \'low\' (Fig.lb) present a\nkind of Bouguer anomaly which is characteristic of the variations in the\nMoho signifying changes in the crustal thicknesses, \'low\' corresponding to a\nthick crust and \'high\' a thin one (Mishra et al. 1987).\n\nThis inference has\n\nbeen supported also from deep seismic sounding studies in different tectonic\nIn\nregimes of the country including Peninsular Shield (Kaila et a1 1979).\nthis regard the occurrence of this \'low\' over the high grade terrain of\nSoIndia is very significant as it suggests a thick crust in this region.\nSuch a situation under high grade terrains can arise only if the crustal\naccretion has taken place after the erosion of the upper crust or due to\nunder-plating along a shear zone or old suture zone as described by Fountain\nand Salisbury (1981). The absence of oceanic sediments or volcanic and\nultramafic rocks or their equivalents in this area does not favour the\nlatter possibility of a suture zone. The Palghat-Tiruchi line may not be a\ntrue suture zone but can be considered as line of juxtaposition between two\nblocks as has been described by Thomas andTanner (1975), inside 100 k m of\n\nThick Crust South of Palghat-Tiruchi gap\nMishra, D.C.\n\n104\n\nthe Grenville Province.\nThe magnetic characteristics (Suryanarayana and Bhan 1985) around\nPalghat also changes significantly. The southern part depicting more\nintense magnetic anomalies than the northern part. MAGSAT has also shown an\nanomalous magnetic crust in this region (Mishra and Venkatrayudu 1985).The\nPalghat-Tiruchi line separating the\n\nlow\' and the \' i h ,\nhg\'\n\ntherefore is very\n\nsignificant representing probably the junction of two blocks during the preCambrian period. These blocks might have over riden each other forming a\nthick crust towards the south from which even If the upper part is eroded\naway the remaining part is still thicker than a normal crust. The Bhavani\nfault towards north might have formed during this process sympathetic and\nparallel to this line.\n\nA closely-spaced profile recorded recently across\n\nthese anomalies will be modelled and presented in the workshop to highlight\nthe variations in the physical parameters and crustal thicknesses in the\nregion.\nReferences\n\nFountain, D M\n. . and Salisbury, MH,\n..\nLetters, 56, p.263-277.\nKaila, KL,\n..\n\nRoy Choudhury, K,\n.\n\n(19811, Earth and Planetary Science\n\nReddy, PR,\n..\n\nSubbotin, SI,\n..\n\nSollogub, VB,\n..\n\nLazarenko, MA\n..\n\nand Ilchenko, TV,\n..\n\nKrishna, VG,\n..\n\nChekunov, AV,\n..\n\nHari Narain,\n\nKharetchko,\n\nGE,\n..\n\n(1979), J Geol. IS\nOF POOR QUALITY\n\nN89-2222\n\n&3\n109\n\nHEAT TRANSFER BY FLUIDS IN GRANULITE METAMORPHISM;\nPaul Morgan, Geology Department, Box 6030,Northern Arizona University, Flagstaff, AZ\n8601 1-6030,USA, and Lewis Ashwal, Lunar and Planetary Institute, 3303 NASA Road One,\nHouston, TX 77058-4399,\nUSA.\n\nGranulite metamorphism represents the extremes of crustal conditions short of melting.\nThese extreme conditions place important constraints upon models that can be used to explain\nthe generation of granulites which we find exposed at the surface. In this short contribution\nwe examine these constraints and discuss the role of fluids in granulite metamorphism, with\nspecial reference to the granulites of southern India.\nRequirements of Granulite MetamorI,his\nGranulite metamorphism requireszmperatures in excess of 700OC, and pressures are\ncommonly recorded in exposed granulites indicating burial depths of 15 to 30 km. Nearly all\nexamples of exposed granulite rocks contain at least some component of supracrustal rocks,\nincluding sediments, volcanics, or other rock units which formed at or near the E r h s\nat\'\nsurface (1). These granulites are now exposed on the surface of normal thickness crust (35 to\n40 km). Thus, three components are required in any mechanism proposed to explain granulite\nmetamorphism:\n1) Transport ofsu racrustal rocks to 15-30km;\n2) Heating to 7008C or higher; and\n3) Re-exposure at the surface of normal thickness crust.\nModels for Regional Metamomhism\nModels of regional metamorphism can be divided into two basic groups for the purpose of\nunderstanding granulite metamorphism: Monogenetic and Polygenetic models. In monogenetic\nmodels the transport of supracrustals to 15 to 30 km and the re-exposure at the surface are\nassumed to result from a single tectonic event. The commonly invoked mechanism invoked in\nthis context is continental underthrusting and crustal thickening through which the\nsupracrustals are transported to depth by underthrusting, and re-exposed by isostatic uplift\nand erosion of the thickened crust. The constraint that the exposed granulites are always\nunderlain by 35 to 40 km of crust is inherent in this mechanism. In polygenetic models,\ntransport to depth and re-exposure are assumed to result from different tectonic events. The\nsupracrustals can be transported to depth either by underthrusting as in the monogenetic\nmodels, or by deep burial associated with multiple extensional crustal thinning events (isostatic\nconsiderations suggest that single events are unlikely to produce sedimentary basins much\ngreater than 10 km, even under the most favorable assumptions). Re-exposure then results\nfrom an independent tectonic event, either a compressional event in which the granulites are\noverthrust onto a normal thickness crust, or through tectonic unroofing during extension,\nalthough the normal thickness crust constraint requires that this extension takes place in an\noverthickened crust.\nWe have so far ignored the second necessary component of granulite formation, the\nheating to 7C\n0\n0\n\'\nor more. This component places important constraints upon heat transfer\nassociated with granulite metamorphism. In all monogenetic models of granulite formation, and\nthe polygenetic models which invoke tectonic unroofing during extension, a minimum of 35 to\n40 km of crust beneath the granulites is required at the time of their heating and formation.\nAs the granulite mineralogies commonly indicate burial depths of 15 to 30 km,they would\nhave been formed roughly quarter to half-way down an overthickened crustal section. Taking\na very simplistic view that the geothermal gradient is uniform throughout the crust, the Moho\ntemperature would be expected to be two to four times the temperature at the depth of\ngranulite formation: i.e., 70O0C at the granulite formation depth implies Moho temperatures of\n1400 to 2800OC. It is obviously over-simplistic to assume a uniform geothermal gradient\nthroughout the crust, but as shown by Ashwal and others (l), if steady-state conductive\nconditions are assumed, it is impossible to devise a geotherm based upon realistic heat\n\nHEAT TRANSFER BY FLUIDS\nMorgan, Paul and Ashwal, Lewis\n110\n\nproduction values and thermal conductivities that decreases sufficiently with depth to allow\ntemperatures of 70O0C o more in the mid to upper crust without super-solidus temperatures in\nr\nthe lower crust. This constraint is removed if the granulites are formed in the lower crust\nand erosionally exposed following subsequent thrusting over Crust of normal thickness.\nAlternatively, the steady-state conductive geotherm may be modified by the effects of\nconvection in the crust.\nHeat can be convected in the crust by the movement of fluids through the crust or by\nmovement of the crust itself relative to the surface. We consider the latter form of\nconvection fmt. General upward movement of the Crust in isostatic response to erosion or\ntectonic unroofing has the effect of making the geotherm convex upward and raising\ntemperature in the upper crust. Numerous examples of this effect are given by England and\nThompson (2) for a variety of initial assumptions for monogenetic regional metamorphism\nmodels. However, although the required geotherm can be modelled by this mechanism,\nexamination of pressure-temperature-time (PTt) paths for rocks initially buried in the mid to\nupper crust, shows that these rocks cool as a result of uplift and only pass through the\ngranulite formation temperature field for models in which massive melting (super-solidus\ntemperatures) is predicted in the lower crust. Maximum temperatures are attained at any\nspecified horizon when the erosion rate is a minimum after crustal thickening, Le., under\nsteady-state conditions. Significantly increased temperatures at any specified horizon can only\nbe generated by the upward convection of fluids through the crust, either magma or volatiles.\nModels of magmatic convection of heat into the crust and the resulting metamorphism\nhave been presented by Wells (3). These models show that the sustained addition o magma to\nf\nthe crust profoundly influences the geotherm, primarily at levels in the crust below the depth\nof magmatic accretion to the crust. Thus, the most efficient mode in which to produce\ngranulites by this mechanism would be through intrusions into the upper crust, above the level\nat which the granulites are formed. Unfortunately these intrusions would be eroded before\nthe granulites could be exposed, but some evidence of the passage of magmas through the\ngranulites may be expected to remain. If no evidence exists for magmatism associated with\nmetamorphism, heat transfer by volatiles may be a viable mechanism.\nAdvection of heat and matter by fluids during metamorphism has recently been studied by\nBickle and McKenzie (4) for the case in which the rock is modelled as a porous medium, and\nheat transport is laterally homogeneous. At depth, however, it is likely that fluids flow\nthrough discrete fractures, and we have started to investigate metamorphism associated with\nfracture-controlled fluid-flow. Bodvarsson (5) has presented solutions for heating associated\nwith water flowing through fractures for a limited set of flow conditions and single planaro\nfracture geometries. We use these solutions t estimate the effect of steady constanttemperature fluid-flow through a system of vertical planar-fractures in the crust. If vertical\nheat transfer in the rock medium is neglected, crustal temperatures are dominated by the\ntemperature of the ascending fluid, which is of the fom:\nT = T erfc(A(d-z))\nwhere To is the temperilture of the base o the crust and the temperature at which the fluid\n?\nenters the fracture, erfc( ) is the complementary error function, d is the crustal thickness, z is\ndepth, and A is a constant, defined by the flow rate, the ratio of fluid to rock thermal\nproperties, and the time since the flow started. Preliminary calculations suggest that, for\nwater, very modest flow rates (of the order of 0.1 g/s per m of horizontal fracture length)\ncan significantly modify the geothem, and that flows sustained over time periods of 1 ka to 1\nMa, depending upon fracture spacing, can produce temperatures compatible with granulite\nmetamorphism in the mid to upper crust without requiring melting in the lower crust. More\ncomplete numerical studies by Hoisch (6) support these results and conclusion.\nGranulites of Southern India\nSummaries of the geology of the Southern Indian Shield (7, 8) suggest that granulite\nmetamorphism in the southern portion of this shield was associated with a late Archean and/or\nearly Proterozoic mobile belt in which the crust was thickened by compressional deformation.\n\nHEAT TRANSFER BY FLUIDS\nMorgan, Paul and Ashwal, Lewis\n111\n\nThere is abundant evidence for CO -Kmetasomatism throughout the shield, and we tentatively\nsuggest that fluid-flow associatdwith this metasomatism was the primary agent of heat\ntransport for the granulite metamorphism. Definition of a plate-tectonic regime associated\nwith this deformation/metamorphism even is controversial, but it seems likely that compression\nand fluids for metasomatism/metamorphism were associated with early Proterozoic subduction.\nAcknowled eements\nThis study has benefitted greatly from discussions with Tom Hoisch, and we gratefully\nacknowledge access to the results of his numerical models studies prior to publication with\nwhich we have checked the approximations used in our analytical solutions.\nReferences\n(1) L.D. Geology, 94, pp. 127-299, 1986.\n\n\'\'\n\n\'\n\nN89- 2 2 2 2 8\n\n.\'J/\nTHE PETROGENETIC SIGNIFICANCE OF PLAGIOCLASE MEGACRYSTS\nIN ARCHEAN ROCKS.\nD. A. Morrison, W. C. Maczuga, LEMSCO,\nHouston, TX 77058\n\n112\n\n*\'\n2\n\n,\n\nIntroduction: Plagioclase-megacryst bearing rocks occur in all Archean terrains as basalts,\nas hypabyssal units, including sills which appear to have transitions t o extrusive rocks, as\nlarge scale anorthositic intrusives, and as dikes forming post-tectonic swarms emplaced over\nvery large areas [l].l of these occurrences are characterized by the presence of equant\nAl\nplagioclase megacrysts of homogeneous An content, typically greater than An80. The\nvolcanic and hypabyssal units occur in greenstone belts and me associated generally with\nsupracrustals. Some anorthosite complexes are associated with volcanics and supracrustals.\nDikes with megacrysts form large s w a r m cross-cutting both greenstone and granitegneiss\nterrains. Geochemical data suggest that the parent melt and the processes which generate\nthe megacrysts and their host rocks are the same in all tectonic settings.\nParent melt of anorthosites: Archean anorthosite complexes are cumulates composed of\nplagioclase megacrysts in a mafic matrix and range in mode from anorthositic t o gabbroic.\nThe Bad Vermilion Lake complex of Ontario [2] is a representative of such complexes.\nParent compositions corresponding t o large scale anorthositic cumulates are not directly\nobservable, however, estimates can be made through mineral-melt relationships because the\nmegacrysts represent equilibrium and isothermal crystallization conditions. Individual\nplagioclase megacrysts from two anorthositic intrusives, but particularly from the Bad\nVermilion Lake intrusive, were analyzed in detail via a multiple aliquot technique [SI. The\nmultiple aliquot technique helps to sort out the effects of alteration allowing better\nestimates to be made of indigenous trace element abundances. Results ahow that the\nmegacryeta crystallized in equilibrium with a parent liquid depleted in light rare earths\nand with abundances comparable to those commomly observed in basalts (if the\nplagioclase/melt partition coefficients of McKay [4) are employed). The possibility that\nlight rare earth depleted basalts may be parental liquids for Archean anothosite complexes\nis further suggested by the presence of plagioclase megacrysts in basalt flows and basaltic\nsills and dikes.\nThe major element compositions of megcryst-bearing volcanic rocks which are likely to\nrepresent liquids fall in a cluster corresponding to tholeiites. The average composition of\nArchean megacryst-bearing tholeiites from the Canadian Shield is shown in table 1 This\n.\ncomposition is olivine normative. The Mg* number\n(Mg*=MgO/MgO+Fe+0.9(2Fe203+FeO) where FeO/Fe 03=8.1) is 0.54 indicating a\nrelatively evolved composition. In all of the megacrysdearing basalts, iron contents are\nrelatively high (11 to 13% FeOJ and Na,O contents cluster around 2%. In relatively\nunaltered flows, the An content of lathy, zoned matrix plagioclase is lower than that of\nthe megacrysts but megacryst rims, typically a few hundred microns thick, reflect the\ncompositional ranges of the matrix plagioclase.\nRare earth abundances in megacryst-bearing volcanics and associated sills are invariably\nlight rare earth depleted and range from approximately 10 to 15 times chondrites. These\nare the characteristics predicted by the rare earth data from the anorthosite cumulates.\nRare earth contents of megacrysts in the flows (determined for plagioclase separates) allow\nequilibrium between megacrysts and matrix but there is some variation in the heavy rare\nearths, probably as a result of alteration, resulting in some ambiguity.\nParent melts of dike swarms: Megacryst bearing dikes from the Matachewan swarm of\nOntario have been analyzed using multiple aliquot techniques. Chilled margins of the dikes\nare tholeiitic but distinctive from the volcani&. An average composition, representing 36\ndikes from the swarm is shown in table 1. This composition is marginally quartz\nnormative, however the dikes vary from olivine to quartz normative. The Mg* number of\nthe averaged composition is 0.46, and dike rocks tend to have a higher alkali component\nthan megacryst-bearing volcanics. In relatively unaltered rocks, plagioclase megacrysts show\nwave like fluctuations in An content of a few (+/-2) An units, as is also observed in\n\nPetrogenetic Significance of Plagioclase Megacrysts\n..\nMorrison, D.A., Phinney, W.C., Maczuga, D E\n113\n\ncumulates of the Bad Vermilion Lake type. Groundmass plagioclase laths are more sodic\nand progressively zoned. Rims of megacrysts (5 to 6% of the total volume) reflect\ngroundmass plagioclase compositions.\nRare earth abundances in these dike rocks are somewhat higher than in the basalts.\nThe dikes can be divided into three groups, 1) depleted, 2) enriched, and 3.) highly\n.\n.\nenriched, based on the rare earth abundances. All three groups have similar major element\nabundances. Both depleted and enriched dikes occur in greenstone terrains, but only\nenriched dikes occur in granitegneiss terrains. Plagioclase megacryst/matrix rare earth\nabundance ratios equal the partition coefficients determined experimentally by McKay [a]\nfor plagioclase/lunar basalt compositions crystallizing over approximately the same\ntemperature range. This agreement, also observed in the flows and sills, indicates\nequilibrium between megacrysts and matrix, strongly suggesting that the matrix represents\nthe parent liquid of the megacrysts.\nGiven the above observations, it appears that the parent liquid from which plagioclase\nmegacrysts in intrusives, sills, flows and dikes are generated is represented by megacrystbearing sills and flows and at least some dikes. This hypothesis has been tested\nexperimentally. Powders prepared from megacry st-bearing sills from the Bird River area of\nManitoba were crystallized at one atm under FMQ conditions. The results show that\nplagioclase megacryst-bearing basalts could produce the megacrysts they contain and that\nplagioclase of the appropriate An content is on the liquidus for approximately 25\' C\nbefore cpx appears. For an An content of 80, the plagioclase/mafic ratio is 7/3\napproximately, and about 10% of the melt is transformed t o plagioclase of megacryst\ncomposition. Preliminary experiments at 10 kbs show that this composition crystallizes\naugite before plagioclase and th3t the first plagioclase t o appear is more sodic than An80.\nThe experimental data support the proposition that tholeiites of the type shown in table 1\ncould represent a parent liquid for various plagioclase megacryst-bearing rocks including\nArchean anorthositic intrusives. Compositions cluster around the one atm co-tectic on the\nPI-Di-OI pseuodoternary and the experimental data limit the process to moderate t o low\npressures. If this hypothesis is correct then limits can be placed upon the crystallization\nconditions.\nCrystallization Conditions: The homogeneity typical of Archean plagioclase megacrysts\nrequires growth in a nearly isothermal environment. Crystallization takes place in mid to\nupper crustal-level chambers. Individual megacrysts from large scale intrusives (e.g. the\nBad Vermilion lake mass) and from Matachewan dikes have smooth oscillations in An\ncontent from their cores to within a few hundred microns of their much more sodic rims.\nThese oscillations suggest replenishment of the parent liquid during crystallization of the\nmegacrysts. In addition, rare earth abundances and slopes in dike rocks vary greatly for\napproximately constant major element composition. The rare earths are de-coupled from\nthe major elements. This characteristic, together with the indications of rejuvenation of\nthe parent liquid shown by the megacryst An content, is typical of magma replenishment\nduring crystallization and the establishment of perched major element compositions in an\notherwise evolving liquid. Most, but not all, of the incompatible element de-coupling and\nenrichment observed in the dike rocks can be accounted for through replenishment\nprocesses. However replenishment processes cannot account for the range in slope and\nabundances observed between depleted (MORElike) dikes and those with highly enriched\npatterns (La/Sm > 1 8 . In these cases, source differences, and/or variation in amounts of\n.)\npartial melting of a single source may be required. (Assimilation partially resolves\ndifferences but the amount of assimilated material required is large).\ns\nAnorthosite complexes such a at Bad Vermilion Lake place further limits on\ncrystallization conditions. The Bad Vermilion Lake complex is layered on a large scale [ 6 ] .\nIndividual units, hundreds of meters thick, vary in their mode and in the size frequency\ndistribution o their megacrysts. Some units have distributions indicating sorting of\nf\nmegacrysts during cumulate formation. Contacts between units which differ in degree of\nsorting are observable. Flow and sorting during cumulate formation appear to have been\nimportant. The density of t h t liquid is equal to that of the plagioclase at the temperature\nof crystallization (about 1200 C.), consequently the megacrysts are neutrally buoyant in\n\nPetrogenetic Significance of Plagioclase Megacrysts\nMorrison, D.A., Phinney, W.C., Maczuga, D.E.\n\n114\n\nthe liquid from which they crystallize. In addition, the large size of the megacrysts\nsuggests few and scattered nuclei during crystallization and little or no supercooling. The\nBad Vermilion Lake intrusive and other large scale cumulates suggest the presence of large\nperiodically replenished magma chambers, through which very large amounts of liquid were\nmoved to become volcanics. The cumulates represent 10 to 15% of the parent liquid\nvolume.\nSummary: Archean plagioclase megacryst-bearing rocks form in mid to upper crustal level\nmagma chambers which are repeatedly replenished. Crystallization is nearly isothermal and\nis an eqilibrium process. Cumulates are formed, probably in marginal %onesof the\nchambers, and liquids bearing megacrysts are extracted to appear as volcanica. Flows and\nsome intrusives occur in arc-like environments in greenstone belts. Dikes represent large\nvolumes of melt. The areal extent of dike s w a r m like the Matachewan swarm suggests\nmultiple sources of like composition. Primitive liquid(s) evolve to Fe-rich tholeiite\ncompositions (and acquire contaminants) then move to mid- t o upper crustal levels where\nmegacrysts are formed. Complex sequences of ponding and melt migration are probable\nand involve large amounts of liquid.\n\nTABLE 1\n:\nI\n\nI\n\nAVERAGED COMPOSITIONS OF MEGACRYST-BEARING\nFLOWS AND DIKES\nSiO,\n\nM,O, TiO,\n\nFeO\n\nMgO\n\nCaO\n\nNa,O K O\n,\n\nLO1\n\nAver. Morrison and J.W. Valley, University of\nWisconsin- Madison.\nThe Adirondack Mountains of New York (USA) are a classic granulite facies\nmetamorphic terrane and as such have been the focus of many studies concerning the\nrole of fluids in the development of granulites. Most studies to date in both the\nAdirondack and the S. India granulites have addressed the nature of processes that\noperate during metamorphism as well as pre-metamorphic has been conducted on\npost-metamorphic retrogressive processes, which recent studies have shown to have\nimportant implications for granulite petrogenesis as well as geochronology and\ngeophysical properties of the crust.\nDuring the Grenville Orogeny at\n1.1 Gyr, metamorphic grade in the\nAdirondacks varied from amphibolite facies in the NW Lowlands (6.5-7.0 Kb, 650700 C) to the granulite facies (7.5-8.0 Kb, 750-800 C ) in the Highlands\xe2\x80\x99. The Marcy\nanorthosite massif, a major lithologic unit in the granulite facies of the Adirondacks,\nis a large (-12,000 km3), homogeneous batholith composed predominantly of\nplagioclase (- An4J with lesser amounts of pyroxene, garnet and Fe-Ti oxides.\nApproximately 90% of 150 anorthosite samples contain post peak-metamorphic\nalteration assemblages of calcite, chlorite, sericite, quartz, pyrite, pyrrhotite, epidote,\nscapolite and prehnite. The percentage of alteration is variable and ranges from a\ntrace to 10 volume%.\nTwo distinct textures characterize the alteration assemblages: veins and\ndisseminated phases. The veins are discrete and cross-cut plagioclase megacrysts,\ngarnet, orthopyroxene, clinopyroxene and Fe-Ti oxide. The larger veins (>0.5 mm\nwide) are often symmetrically zoned with calcite cores surrounded by chlorite then\nsericite. Smaller veins (<0.5mm wide) are generally composed of either chlorite or\ncalcite. In addition to the veins, alteration minerals occur disseminated throughout\nboth plagioclase and the mafic minerals, and as \xe2\x80\x98clots\xe2\x80\x99 within the interstitial mafics.\nThese assemblages, which document post metamorphic fluid infiltration, are readily\nvisible by normal petrographic techniques. However, transmitted light microscopy\nalone does not reveal all of the manifestations of the retrograde fluid infiltration.\nCathodoluminescence of apparently unaltered samples reveals anastomosing veins of\ncalcite (<<0.05 mm wide) that lie along cleavage o partings in mineral grains,\nr\nalong cross-cutting fractures and along grain boundaries. These calcite veins indicate\nthat the retrograde fluid infiltration was more extensive than indicated by\ntransmitted light petrography alone2.\nThis widespread retrograde fluid infiltration has important implications for\nstudies of granulite genesis. Substantial controversy surrounds the relative\nimportance of the four mechanisms that have been proposed to account for the low\nthat characterize granulites: 1) partial melting which would\nwater activities (&H20)\ncause a preferential partitioning of water into the melt phase, 2) passage of dry\nmagmas through the crust, 3) pervasive infiltration of deep-seated CO, which would\ndilute the metamorphic fluid and reduce the aHzO, or 4) metamorphism of already\ndry rocks. In particular, controversy surrounds the importance of C02-flooding3#4#5.\nThe presence of high density C02-rich fluid inclusions in granulites is often\n\n-\n\nO\n\nPost-Metamorphic Fluid Infiltration\nMDrrison, J., Valley, J.W.\n116\n\ninterpreted as evidence for infiltration of massive amounts of C 0 2 during\nmetamorphism. Lamb et a1.6 have shown that some high density C02-rich inclusions\nin samples from the Adirondacks must have been trapped after the peak of\nmetamorphism, yet the origin and nature of the retrograde fluids has been poorly\nunderstood. In some samples textural relations between high density C02-rich\ninclusions and secondary minerals indicate that entrapment of the inclusions is\nconcurrent with mineralogic alteration. For example, veins of sericite and chlorite\ncrosscut clinopyroxene and where they intersect quartz, trails of high density C02rich fluid inclusions are developed. We interpret this texture to indicate that the\nfluid inclusions have trapped the same fluids that caused the mineralogic alteration.\nThis textural association of high density C02-rich fluid inclusions and retrograde\nminerals is particularly important in light of the cathodoluminescence results which\nindicate that many apparently pristine samples have been infiltrated by retrograde\nC02-H20 fluids.\nWe have analyzed the carbon and oxygen isotope composition of calcite in 30\naltered anorthosite samples in order to evaluate the provenance of the retrograde\nfluids. Values of 6l80 (SMOW) range from 11.1 to 15.0 %o and values of 613C (PDB)\nrange from 0.2 to -4.0 960. Coexisting calcite and the host plagioclase have been\nanalyzed for 6l80 to evaluate whether the isotopic composition of the calcite is\ncontrolled by the host rock or the hydrothermal fluid. Values of Acc-p, range from\n0.9 to 5.8 which we interpret to indicate that the oxygen isotope composition of the\ncalcite was controlled primarily by the hydrothermal fluids. Mixed H20-CO, fluid\ninclusions provide minimum temperatures for the alteration of -350 C. The calcite\nvalues are intermediate between those of igneous rocks and marbles, which suggests\nthat the hydrothermal fluids exchanged with both meta-igneous and supracrustal\nlithologies.\nThe precises timing of the hydrothermal vein formation is not yet known. If the\nfluid infiltration occurred during uplift from granulite facies pressures and\ntemperatures (maximum depths = 24-26 km at\n1.1 Ga), then the alteration\nassemblages and associated fluid inclusions will provide important constraints on\npressure-temperature-time paths of uplift as well as the nature of mid crustal fluid\nmovements. Alternatively, if the fluid infiltration occurred during the Phanerozoic\nthen these veins provide important information about large scale fluid movements\nassociated with the Taconian or Acadian orogenies as suggested by Oliver\xe2\x80\x99.\n\n-\n\nREFERENCES\n1.
