SM NAQVI National\n..\n\nGeophysical Research Institute, Hyderabad 500 007\n\n~\n\n/ E 7\'\n7\n\n7\n\nINDIA\n\nThe southern part of Karnataka Nucleus is an area in which there\n\nis a strong imprint of 26 Ga metamorphism.\n.\n\nThis has affected the\n\nschist belts of Karnataka Nucleus from greenschist to upper amphibolite\nfacies.\n\nThe higher grades of metamorphism can be seen in the\n\nRolenarasipur, Nuggihalli,Krishnarajpet, Hadnur and Melkote schist\n\nI\n\nbelts in the southern part of Karnataka.\n\nI\n\nzone, around Sargur only keels of schist belts are preserved and occur\n\nIn the high grade transition\n\nas highly dismembered, disconnected belts with the top and bottom of\nthe stratigraphic column obliterated due to high grade metamorphism and\naccompanying migmatization.\n\nAbsence of high-grade metamrophic minerals\n\nas detrital heavies in the sediments of the Dharwar schist belts\nsupports the contention that high grade metamorphism post-dated the\nDharwar sedimentation and occurred around 2 6 Ga ago.\n.\n\nSargur type\n\nmetamorphism (intermediate pressure) occurred at upper crustal levels\nwhere P H20 was higher and charnockite type metamorphism occurred in\nlower crustal levels where P C02 exceeded\n\nP H20. Metamorphism in the\n\ntwo crustal levels apparently took place simultaneously.\n\nThe Sargur\n\nGroup of rocks are composed of sediments characteristic of platformal\nassemblages.\n\nThe P-T conditions for the mineral assemblage in\n\nmetapelites of Sargur Group indicate burial depths upto at least 15 km\nsuggesting that they were subducted and later obducted during the\ndevelopment of Early Proterozoic Mobile Belt along the southern border\nof the Karnataka Nucleus.\n\nN89-22235\nPETROLOGY AUD PHYSICAL C O l l D I T I O l S OF METMORPHISX OF CALCSILICATE BOCKS FROM W W - TO EIGH-GRADE TRANSITIOU AREA, DEARllbPURI\nR latara Jan and P.K.Govi1,\n.\nD I S T R I C T , TAMIL UADU; B.L.larayana,\n\nNational Geophysical Research Institute, Hyderabad-500 007 India\nCalc-silicate rocks comprising quartz, plagioclase, diopside,\nsphene, scapolite, grossularite-andradite and wollastonite occur as\nlensoid enclaves within the greasy migmatitic and charnockitic gneisses\nof the Archaean amphibolite- to granulite-facies transition zone in\nDharmapuri district, Tamil Nadu. They are associated with magnetitequartzites and corundum- sillimanite-bearing metapelite bands in which\nsegregation of leucosomes containing garnet and K-feldspar are present.\nThe calc-silicate rocks are chara-cterized by the absence of K-f eldspar\nand primary calcite, presence of large modal quartz and plagioclase and\nformation of secondary garnet and zoisite rims around scapolite and\nwollastonite.\nThe mineral distributions suggest compositional layering. Late\nretrograde rim garnet at the interfaces of plagioclase and wollastonite\nis grossular-rich (Gross72) while the other garnet is comparatively low\nin graossular content indicating variation in the bulk composition of\ndifferent layers. Microprobe analyses of the constituent minerals in\nthree calc-silicate rocks have shown that calcic-rich plagioclase\n(An88-89) is associated with scapolite of lower equivalent anorthite\nContent (eq. An67-73) w h i l e less calcic plagioclase (An55) is\nassociated with scapolite of higher equivalent anorthite content (eq.\nAn641 indicating the control of bulk composition.\nThe chemical\ncomposition and mineralogy of the calc-silicate rocks indicate that\nthey were derived from impure silica-rich calcareous sediments whose\ncomposition is similar to that of pelite-limestone mixtures.\nFrom the mineral assemblages the temperature, pressure and fluld\ncomposition during metamorphism have been estimated. The partitioning\nof Na and Ca betwe n scapolite and plagioclase yield temperatures\ngreater than 66OoCf while the scapolite composition indicates a\nminimum temperature of less th\n75OoC. The garnet-clinopyroxeneplagioclase-quart e geobaromete? and clinopyroxene-plagioclase-quartz\ngeobarometer3 give pressures of about 6 kbars.\nThe observed mineral reaction sequences require a range of X\nvalues (from about 04 to 0.12) demonstrating that an initially C8i2\n.\nrich metamorphic fluid evolved with time towards considerably more H 0rich compositions. These variations in fluid composition suggest t at\nthere were sources of water-rich f luids external to the calc-silicate\nrocks and that mixing of these fluids with those of calc-silicate rocks\nwas important in controlling fluid composition in calc-silicate rocks\nand some adjacent rock types as well. Probably the calc-silicate rocks\nbehaved as an open system for a short time only, and the reactions\nresulting from rehydration proceeded more rapidly, and never completed.\n\nf\n\nf/\n\n128\n\nP E T R O W AUD P-T COldDITIoIoS OF CALC-SILICATE\nEarayana, B,L. et al,\n\nHydration causing formation of garnet and zoisite rims in calc-silicate\nrocks is related to secondary biotite in the associated charnockitfc\ngneisses. The occurrence of leucosome segregations with garnet and Kfeldspar in metapelites indicates melting and absorption of E20 Into\nanatectic melts and this dehydration has aided the granulite-facies\nmetamorphism of the South Indian shield in addition to streaming of\nC02-rich fluids4 proposed for the metamorphism.\n\n1\n.\n\nGoldsmith, J . R . and Newton, R.C.\n\n2\n.\n\nNewton, R.C. and Perkins, 111, D. (1982) Am. Mineral., 67, 203-222.\n\n3.\n\nEllis, D . J .\n\n(1980) Contrib. Mineral. Petrol.,\n\n4 Janardhan, AS,\n.\n..\n51 1-514.\n\n(1977) Am. Mineral., 62, 1063-1081\n\nNewton, R.C. and\n\n74, 201-210.\n\nSmith, J.V. (1979) Nature, 278,\n\n129\n\nNATURE AND ORIGIN OF FLUIDS IN GRANULITE FACIES\nMETAMORPHISM R.C. Newton, Department of the Geophysical Sciences, University of\nChicago, Chicago, IL 60637,USA\nOrthopyroxene, the definitive mineral of the granulite facies, may originate in\nprograde dehydration reactions in rock systems open to fluids or may be a premetamorphic\nrelic of igneous intrusions or their contact aureoles which persisted through fluid-deficient\nmetamorphism. Terrains showing evidence of open-system orthopyroxene-forming reactions\nare those of South India and southern Norway. An example of fluid-deficient granulite facies\nmetamorphism is the Adirondack Highlands of New York, where metamorphic pyroxene\ncommonly resulted from dry recrystallization of pyroxenes of plutonic charnockites and\nanorthosites. The metamorphism recorded by the presence of orthopyroxene in different\nterrains may thus have been conservative or may have involved fluids of different origins\npervasive on various length-scales.\nMetamorphism with pervasive metasomatism is signaled by monotonous H20, C 0 2\nand 0 2 fugacities over large areas, nearly independent of lithology, by scarcity of relict\ntextures, and by pronounced depletion of Rb and other large ion lithophile (LILE) elements in\nthe highest grade areas, such as the southernmost part of the Bamble, South Norway, terrain\n(1). Primary hornblende is rare or absent in quartzofeldspathic gneisses and orthopyroxene\nis ubiquitous in acid and basic rocks in the charnockitic terrains. Pronounced major and\nminor element redistributions took place during metasomatic charnockitization of amphibole\ngneiss at Kabbaldurga, Karnataka (2).\nConservative metamorphism of originally dry rocks in the Adirondacks is evidenced\nby incipient grain-boundary garnet-forming reaction zones between plagioclase and interstitial\npyroxene in anorthosites, implying lack of a pervasive flux, by elevated l80of paragneisses,\nreflecting preservation of low-temperature processes in the protoliths, by strong lateral\ngradients in 1 8 0 (3) and in apparent volatile fugacities, especially f(02), implying lack of a\nlarge oxygen source in the form of pervasive H 2 0 or C02, and by common preservation of\nupper-crustal, premetamorphic textures, such as chilled margins of dikes, rapakivi texture,\nand thermal aureoles around intrusions. Lack of LILE depletion in high-grade granulites\nindicates fluid-deficient metamorphism.\nThe origin of fluids is a key issue in high-grade metamorphism. Such fluids must\nhave been low in H 2 0 to coexist with orthopyroxene. Dense CO2-rich fluid inclusions in\nBamble (4) and the Nilgiri Hills ( 5 ) suggest that fluids were dominantly carbonic in\nmetamorphism of the charnockitic terrains. Such fluids could have resulted from: A)\n6;\nalteration of resident pore fluids by absorption of H 2 0 into anatectic melts ( ) B) exsolution\nfrom crystallization of deep-crustal mafic (4)or intermediate (7)magma bodies; C)\ndecarbonation of crustal limestones and dolomites (8); D) an outgassing mantle hot spot (9);\nE) reaction of hydrous minerals and graphite in uplift and decompression of granulite-facies\n(10); or F) release of C 0 2 from deep crustal fluid inclusions by deformation during a\nmetamorphic episode (2). Occurrence of orthopyroxene in migmatitic leucosomes in\nNamaqualand, South Africa (1 1) is evidence for A); charnockitic margins on acid dikes in\nthe Wind River Range of Wyoming (12) is evidence for B ; massive charnockite grading\n)\nupward to patchy charnockite in hornblende gneiss overlying a massive marble bed in Sri\nLanka is evidence for C) (13); the large length-scales and high paleotemperatures nearly\n\n130\n\n0RIGI.N OF GRANULITE FLUIDS\nNewton, R.C.\nindependent of paleopressures in the South India terrain suggest a subcrustal origin of heattransporting fluids in accord with D) (9); apparent fracture control of charnockitic alteration\nof paragneisses in Kerala suggest E) (10); and late Archaean charnockitic veins around the\nmargins of possibly older granulites in southern Karnataka suggest F) (2).\nIt is likely that different kinds of fluids of different origins and in varying amounts\nwere instrumental in different granulite terrains. Resolution of the nature and extent of the\noperation of fluids in granulite metamorphism will be provided by detailed studies of oxygen\nisotopes, oxidation states of iron oxides and silicates, apparent paleofugacities of H20 and\nC02 indicated by mineral assemblages, and by open-system versus closed-system behavior\nindicated by metamorphic patterns of major and trace elements.\nREFERENCES\n(1) Smalley, P.C., Field, D., Lamb, R.C. and Clough, P.W.L. Ottawa, Ontario,\nt,\nCanada, K1A OE4\n\nJA\n..\n\nAt 300 x 300 km, the Ashuanipi complex is one of the largest massif\ngranulite terranes of the Canadian Shield (Fig. 1). It makes up the eastern\nend of the 2000-km-long, lower-grade, east-west belts of the Archean Superior\nProvince (l), permitting lithological, age and tectonic correlation (2).\nNumerous lithological, geochemical and metamorphic similarities to south\nIndian granulites suggest common processes and invite comparison of tectonic\nevolution.\nSuperior Province consists of a northern high-grade region, the Minto\nblock (Fig. l), and the well-known southern subprovinces of 3.1-2.7 Ga greenstone-granite, metasedimentary gneiss and plutonic character (1). Metasedimentary gneiss probably extends, through poorly-known territory, from the eaststriking belts into the Ashuanipi complex (1,3).\nSeveral gneissic and homogeneous lithological units are recognized on the\nregional scale in the Schefferville area (3,4). Paragneiss, consisting of\nassemblages of Grt-Opx-Bio-Plg-Qtz+Kfs, is the oldest. Although mainly psammitic, it has rare compositional variation to pelite and leptynite. Interlayered with paragneiss on the m to km scale is early tonalite, with characteristic igneous oikocrystic orthopyroxene (5), variably broken down to\nbiotite and metamorphic orthopyroxene during deformation and migmatization. It\nvaries compositionally to rare diorite and gabbro. Layered pyroxenite-peridotite sills, up to 80 m thick, with rare associated gabbro, occur as strings of\nboudinaged pods up to lo\'s of km long.\nHomogeneous intrusions make up some 90% of the terrane, The oldest bodies\nare foliated Opx-Bio+Cpx+Hbl tonalite, quartz diorite and diorite. These are\ncut by the most abundant rock type of the complex: coarse-grained to megacrystic Grt-Opx-Bio-Plg-Qtz-Kfs granodiorite, mapped as homogeneous diatexite\n(3,4,6) because of its association with, and compositional similarity to\nparagneiss, Two texturally similar units are recognized: an older, more voluminous, garnet-bearing variety, and younger pods, layers and plutons without\ngarnet. Massive to weakly foliated Cpx-bearing granite and syenite, locally\nwith nepheline, form the youngest intrusions.\nThe dominant structural elements are an SI migmatitic layering in gneisses\nand foliation in homogeneous intrusions that defines a NE-dipping homocline on\nthe regional scale. Open, upright Fa folds of SI layering form discontinuous,\neast-plunging or doubly-plunging structures, generally basins, on the 10-20 km\nscale. The folds are localized in large-scale, open "Z" warps of regional\nfoliation, possibly related to dextral transcurrent movement. Narrow concordant shear zones are accompanied by abundant migmatitic leucosome, Grt, Opxbearing pegmatite, and late, brittle fractures. Diatexite contains inclusions\nof migmatitic (SI) gneiss, but some concordant bodies are folded with gneiss\nin Fr structures, bracketing intrusion between DI and Dr.\nThe assemblage Grt-Opx-Bio-Plg-Qtz-Kfs is ubiquitous in the Schefferville\nregion, in paragneiss, diatexite, some early tonalites, and in late pegmatites. One occurrence of Grt-Crd-Sil-Bio-Plg-Qtz-Kfs has been recognized.\nMafic rocks have Opx-Cpx-Hbl-Plg+Qtz. Two generations of orthopyroxene are\npresent locally in early tonalite: igneous oikocrysts and blocky, metamorphic\nporphyroblasts surrounded by mafic depletion haloes. Minerals are fresh and\nyield Grt-Bio temperatures for paragneiss and diatexite in the 750-800\xc2\xb0C range\n\nACCRETIONARY ASHUANIPI COMPLEX\nPercival, J.A.\n133\n\nusing (7). Based on Grt-Opx-Plg-Qtz barometers (8,9), metamorphic pressure was\n.\nin the 5 to 6 5 kb range. Whole-rock geochemical analyses of migmatitic rocks\nshow no evidence of R b depletion with respect to K (avg K/Rb ratio of 210).\nPatchy retrogression of Opx and Grt to Bio is common in the western part of\nthe complex (10,2).\nDiatexites are uniformly coarse-grained, have sharp, concordant contacts\nwith adjacent gneiss, and contain angular to lenticular gneissic inclusions,\nsuggesting intrusive emplacement into gneiss at the present structural level.\nGarnet-bearing diatexite is very similar to paragneiss in terms of mineralogy,\nmineral chemistry, major, trace and rare-earth element chemistry (Fig. 2) and\nmay thus represent the fused equivalent of paragneiss. REE abunclancaa and\npattrcna ~ L \' E c ~ q m - a b l s\nfar early tanalite, p s r a g m i m and diatewite, Tunalites and diatexites have higher K/Rb ratios than gneisses (290, 257 respectively), possibly indicating igneous fractionation (11).\nZircon and monazite U-Pb ages (2) constrain the plutonic and metamorphic\nhistory. Early tonalites have discordant zircons with minimum ages greater\nthan 2 7 Ga whereas a foliated tonalite pluton is 2.69 Ga. Diatexites have\n.\nsome inherited zircon; igneous grains give 2.67-2.66 Ga. Monazite from late\npegmatite is 2.65 Ga, similar to the regional monazite cooling ages in gneiss\nand diatexite. A zircon date of 2.642 Ga on retrogressed diatexite, distinctly\nyounger than monazite cooling ages, suggests that a discrete, late, localized\nhydrothermal event caused the retrogression (2). The small age gap between\nzircon and monazite ages indicates that cooling began quickly after the\nmetamorphic peak. Proterozoic sediments of 2.15 Ga age overly the granulites\nunconformably, supporting this inference.\nCritical parameters to consider in interpreting the origin of the complex\ninclude: 1) supracrustal rocks are paragneiss, derived from homogeneous,\nimmature clastic metasediments; 2) most of the complex is made up of intrusive\nrocks, dominantly diatexite, generated, emplaced and crystallized during the\nhigh-grade metamorphism, at 2.67-2.66 Ga, at the same time as granite plutonism in along-strike low-grade belts to the west (12); 3) metamorphic pressures\nare moderate to low for granulites (17-22 km erosion level); cooling and\nerosion began quickly after metamorphism; 4) melting was the dominant process\nduring granulite metamorphism, producing migmatitic textures in gneiss and\ngenerating diatexite melts at depth.\nBased on observations at the 17-22 km erosion level in the Ashuanipi\ncomplex and 8-15 km levels exposed in belts to the west, a model of metamorphic development in a >2000 km accretionary prism is proposed (Fig. 3):\nimmature sediments derived from adjacent arcs (greenstone belts) wete accreted\nand thickened to a maximum 55 km (13) at 2.75-2.70 Ga. Thermal relaxation\nand/or arc magmas (14) heated the lowermost crust, causing fusion and upward\nheat transfer through granitic magmatism. Magmas crystallized as deep-crustal\ncharnockites (diatexite) and fractionated (15) to form higher-level peraluminous granite (12). The overthickened crust rebounded to an isostatically stable\n35 km by erosionally removing the upper 8-22 km. Post-metamorphic erosionlevel differences along the belt may be related to the amount of early\nstructural thickening. Similar features characterize some Cenozoic accretion.\nary complexes in the N American Cordillera (14,16).\nDiatexite, which forms the bulk of the Ashuanipi complex (17), is similar\n.\nto S Indian "Ponmudi-type"(l8) charnockite in terms of texture, mineralogy,\ncomposition and crystallization conditions, but probably crystallized to\ngranulite-facies assemblages directly from a melt. Before comparing models of\n\nACCRETIONARY ASHUANIPI COMPLEX\nPercival, JA\n..\n134\n\ntectonic evolution, the age of Indian charnockitization with respect to\nregional metamorphism, plutonism and crustal formation should be documented by\nprecise U-Pb studies.\nReferences: (1) Card KD, Ciesielski A (1986) Geosci Can 13:5-13; (2) Mortensen\nJM, Percival JA (1987) Geol Surv Can Pap 87-2 (in press); (3) Percival JA\n(1987) Geol Surv Can Pap 87-1A:l-10; (4) Percival JA (1988) Geol Surv Can Pap\n88-la (in press); (5) Nagerl PJ (1987) Thesis, Carleton Univ, 54p; (6) Brown M\n(1973) Proc Geol Assoc 84:371-382; (7) Thompson AB (1976) Am J Sci 276:425454; (8) Newton RC, Perkins, D (1982) Amer Mineral 67:203-222; (9) Bohlen SR\net a1 (1983) Contrib Mineral Petrol 83:52-61; (10) Herd RK (1978) Geol Surv\nCan Pap 78-10:79-83; (11) Rudnick RL et a1 (1985) Geochim Cosmochim Acta\n49:1645-1655; (12) Percival JA, Sullivan RW (1985) Lun Planet Inst Tech Rep\n86-10:167-169; (13) Platt JP (1986) Geol SOC Am Bull 93:1037-1053; (14) Hudson\nT, Plafker G (1982) Geol SOC Am Bull 93:1280-1290; (15) Frost BR, Frost (P\n(1987) Nature 327:503-506; (16) Evans BW (1987) NATO Bergen wkshp oral corn;\n(17)Eade KE (1966) Geol Surv Can Mem 339, 84p; (18) Hansen EC et a1 (1987)\nContrib Mineral Petrol 96:225-244 Granite-Greenstone\n\na&\n\nM e t asedimentar y\nHigh-gr8de Gneiss\nPlutonic\n500 km\n\nFig. 1: Superior Province (1)\nshowing Ashuanipi complex in\nrelation to E-W subprovinces,\n\nL.\n\nFig. 2 : Chondrite-normal-\n\n0\n\nc\n\n0\n\n5\n>\n\ning similarity among\nmajor rock types.\n\n1-\n\nc\n\nI\n-\n\nLaI C.\n\nNd\n\n\'\n\ns\'m\n\nE;\n\nGd\n\nDY\n\nYb\nromoved isostatically\n\nprosent -35 k m crust\n\nFig.3: Accretionary model for (A)\ncrust, later heated (e), fused and thinned by erosion,\n\n8p-\n\n135\n\nTECTONIC IMPLICATIONS OF ARCHEAN ANORTHOSITE OCCURRENCES\nW.C. Phinney, D.A. Morrison, ,Johnson Space Center and D.E. Maczuga, LEMSCO,\nHouston, TX 77058.\nIntroduction\nAnorthositic complexes occur in essentially all Archean cratons and contain large\nequidimensional plagioclase crystals (up t o 30cm. diam.) with highly calcic compositiok\n(An,, t o Ang0). Several occurrences have been described in India [ 11 ,[ 21 ,[ 31 ,[ 41.\nBecause the anorthositic complexes represent cumulates, the composition and source of\nparental melts has been a\' longstanding problem. Plagioclase having t h e s a m e\ncomposition and texture as that in anorthosites also occurs as megacrysts in basaltic\nflows, dikes, and sills in which the crysts may be scattered or concentrated in lenses or\ntrains. We suggest that the anorthosites and megacrystic basalts are petrogenetically\nrelated. However, the tectonic settings for these occurrences appear to be quite variable\nsuggesting that several environments may be represented. A brief outline of the regional\nsettings of these anorthosites and petrogenetically related basalts follows.\nArchean Occurrences\nMegacrystic Anorthosites\n1. Cumulate crystal segregations in anorthositic to gabbroic complexes associated with\nvolcanic sequences typical of low to middle metamorphic grade greenstone belts [5],[6].\n2. Cumulate crystal segregations in thick anorthositic t o gabbroic sills t h a t intrude\nvolcanic sequences typical of greenstone belts [71.\n3. Cumulate crystal segregations in anorthositic to gabbroic complexes associated with\nhigh grade metamorphic terrains containing marbles, quartzites, quartzofeldspathic\ngneisses, and amphibolites [3],[8].\nArchean Occurrences\nMegacrystic Basalts\n1. Flows, dikes, and sills in volcanic sequences typical of greenstone belts [91,[101.\n2. Dike swarms in stable cratonic areas forming parallel t o subparallel\' p a t t i r n s over\nhundreds of thousands of square kilometers intruding both high grade granitic gneisses\nand low to middle grade supracrustal belts [lo].\nYounger Occurrences\nSimilar occurrences of megacrysts in basalts of early Proterozoic age are known in\ncratonic dikes of the Bighorn Mountains of Wyoming [ll] and the Beartooth Mountains\nof Montana [12) and in volcanic flows of the Bell Island Group of t h e Northwest\nTerritories [ 131. Recent occurrences of similarly calcic plagioclase phenocrysts are known\nin oceanic volcanic flows at spreading ridges, hotspots, aseismic ridges and fracture zones\n[14]. However, these normally involve only small phenocrysts up to a few millimeters in\nsize and usually are more lathy than equidimensional in shape. In contrast t o these\ncommon oceanic occurrences, volcanic flows over the Galapagos hotspot display more\nequidimensional calcic crysts up to 3cm. across [15]. In essentially all of the Archean\nand Proterozoic occurrences the distribution coefficients for REE\'s indicate equilibrium\nbetween the megacrysts and their matrices of Fe-rich tholeiites [le]. However, use of\nthe same distribution coefficients in the more recent occurrences indicates substantial\ndisequilibrium between the. crysts and their tholeiitic matrices. Thus, the more recent\noccurrences of calcic plagioclase crysts require an additional stage of evolution before\nreaching their current environment, thereby providing a bit more uncertainty about their\npetrogenesis than t h e older occurrences and making direct comparison with ancient\ntectonic environments untenable.\nMe!ts and Magma Chambers\nUtilizing experimental petrologic studies of t h e basaltic matrices and distribution\ncoefficients with trace element analyses of plagioclase it seems clear t h a t all of the\nabove-listed megacryst occurrences are\' associated -with similar parent melts for both the\n\nTECTONIC IMPLICATIONS OF ARCHEAN ANORTHOSITE OCCURRENCES\nW. C. Phinney et al.\n136\n\nanorthosites and megacrystic basalts [le]. The melts are relatively Fe-rich, tholeiitic\nbasalts that exhibit a significant range of Fe-enrichment (50-70% on a n AFM plot) in\nassociation with the megacrysts. The basalts of the cratonic dikes are more enriched in\nK, Na, and light R E E t h a n their greenstone counterparts but follow a parallel Feenrichment trend. Furthermore, the fractionation trends and formation of the crysts\noccurred a t relatively shallow levels (<5Kb) [le]. T h e megacrysts in all of t h e\noccurrences are quite uniform in composition (*l to 2 An units) over several centimeters\nexcept for very thin rims (~100-200pm). This suggests nearly isothermal crystallization\na t a nearly constant melt composition over substantial periods of time. Geochemical\nmodeling of trace elements and subtle cyclic compositional trends in t h e plagioclase\nindicate multiple influxes of melts into the magma chambers during evolution of the\nmelts and growth of the megacrysts. The widespread occurrences of the megacrystic\nunits in both greenstone belts and huge cratonic dike swarms further suggests extensive\ndevelopment of magma chambers in which tholeiitic melts produce calcic plagioclase as a\nmajor liquidus phase under both cratonic and oceanic Archean crusts. In essentially all\noccurrences where adequate preservation of initial igneous textures and structures exists,\nthere is evidence for at least two stages of plagioclase formation. In the anorthositic\ncomplexes there are bimodal units in which very large crysts are mixed with smaller, but\nstill large, crysts. In the basalts the calcic megacrysts have thin sodic rims t h a t match\nthe composition of the lathy plagioclase in the matrices. Both situations indicate\nformation of the large crysts at locations other than their final position of emplacement,\nprobably indicating a complex series of magma chambers in the crust.\nCrustal Levels\n-- appear to have intruded\nThe anorthosites\n\nat various crustal levels. In many of the lowgrade supracrustal- -(greenstone) settings the preservation of primary sedimentary and\nvolcanic structures and textures indicate that the regions have always been at low grade\na n d t h a t t h e anorthosites intruded a t very shallow levels. In t h e higher g r a d e\noccurrences it is not always clear whether the anorthosites intruded at the higher grades\nor at low grade and were l a t e r upgraded. In M a n i t o b a there is a clear case of\nanorthosites initially intruding low grade supracrustal units b u t l a t e r a regional\nmetamorphic gradient produced a continuous sequence from low greenschist t o granulite\ngrades in all of the units [17]. In the granulite grade Shawmere anorthosite complex of\nOntario [18),however, there are some nearly undeformed enclaves where the more mafic\nunits contain well preserved olivines and pyoxenes with well preserved exsolution texture.\nFurthermore, some plagioclase contains well preserved polysynthetic twinning t h a t looks\nlike original igneous twinning. Such preservation seems unlikely if the anorthosite were\nintruded at low grade and underwent progressive metamorphism to granulite grade, unless\nthe system were essentially dry during metamorphism which also seems unlikely in view\nof the biotite- and amphibole-bearing units adjacent t o the intrusion a n d amphibolebearing pegmatitic zones within the complex.\n\nEffects of Fluids at High Grades\nSeveral petrographic observations in high-grade anorthositic complexes indicate t h e\ninfiltration of substantial amounts of fluid. Recrystallized plagioclase ranging from\nstrained patchy areas to polycrystalline areas may occur as irregularly distributed zones,\nvein-like stringers, or rims around relict cores. Generally these areas display elevated\nvalues of N a and REE\xe2\x80\x99s in the plagioclase. Inclusions of tiny amphibole needles are\ncommon in non-recrystallized plagioclase of upper greenschist a n d higher grades.\nConcentration of the inclusions is highly variable even within a single crystal. Many\nplagioclase crysts contain 10% or more by volume of these inclusions. The initial FeO\ncontent of the plagioclase is in the .4-.6% range and the FeO content of inclusion-rich\nplagioclase is ~ . 1 % . However, microprobe analyses of the amphiboles indicate 15-20%\nFeO which for 10% inclusions requires several times more FeO than was present in the\ninitial plagioclase. Similarly the heavy REE contents of these plagioclase separates are\n\nTECTONIC IMPLICATIONS OF ARCHEAN ANORTHOSITE OCCURRENCES\nW. C. Phinney et al.\n137\n\nsubstantially increased over the initial values reflecting the heavy REE enrichment in\namphiboles. Clearly there must be flow of fluids through the plagioclase in some manner\nto add Na, Fe, heavy REE, and H,O.\nConclusions for Archean and early Proterozoic Megacrystic Units\n1. Segregations of plagioclase may occur a t various d e p t h s in t h e c r u s t t o form\n- anorthosite.\n2. A n o r t h o s i t e s m a y o c c u r i n oceanic volcanic c r u s t a n d i n c r a t o n i c o r shelf\nenvironments.\n3. Megacrystic basalts may form in oceanic or stable cratonic environments.\n4. Plagioclase megacrysts in Fe-rich tholeiites indicate relatively shallow magma chambers.\n5. Large uniform crysts require extensive periods of isothermal growth at nearly constant\nmelt composition and almost certainly formed in fractionating magma chambers t h a t are\nperiodically replenished.\n6. Megacrystic tholeiitic dike swarms indicate widespread replenishing magma chambers\nunder stable cratons.\n7. It is not clear what oceanic environment is represented by megacrystic u n i t s i n\ngreenstones b u t i t does require m a g m a chambers for s u b s t a n t i a l t i m e at similar\ntemperatures and melt compositions over extensive areas.\n8. Petrogenetic conditions for formation of megacrystic anorthosites and basalts in the\nArchean and early Proterozoic were not the same as in younger times.\n9. Substantial flow of fluids accompanied by exchange of components can occur i n\nanorthosites a t high grades of metamorphism with little more effect on the plagioclase\nthan formation of amphibole inclusions and scattered recrystallization.\nI n summary, megacrystic anorthosites and basalts can occur i n a variety of geologic\nsettings and by themselves are not definitive.\nOnly with additional field, petrologic and\ngeochemical data can the settings be understood.\n\nREFERENCES\nLeelanandam,C. and Reddy,M.N. Ramakrishnan, Geological Survey of India, Precambrian Geology Division,\n\nHyderabad.\n/\n\n/y77,/\xe2\x80\x995\n\nThe southern Indian s h i e l d (Fig. 1) c o n s i s t s o f three major t e c t o n i c\nprovinces viz., (1) Dharwar Craton, (2) Eastern Ghat Mobile Belt and (3)\nPandyan Mobile Belt. An understanding of their mutual r e l a t i o n s is c r u c i a l\nf o r formulating c r u s t a l evolution models.\n\nDharwar Craton is d i v i s i b l e i n t o Western and Eastern Blocks separated by\nthe linear Closepet Granite (1). The supracrustal b e l t s of the Western Block\nare comparable t o the Early Proterozoic \xe2\x80\x99geosynclines\xe2\x80\x99 of Canada and A u s t r a l i a\nand those of the Easter Block are t y p i c a l late greenstone b e l t s . Both types of\nN-S trending supracrustals are co-eval (2600 Ma) and their d i f f e r e n c e s a r e due\nt o minor r e a c t i v a t i o n of 3000-4000 M old basement i n the Western Block, i n\na\ncontrast t o the extensive juvenile plutonism and l a r g e s c a l e c r u s t a l\nremobilization of the Eastern Block, r e s u l t i n g from anomalous heat flow from\nmantle. The favoured model for their evolution is sagging and r i f t i n g o f\ns i a l i c crust w i t h proto-ocean opening and i t s p a r t i a l c l o s u r e due t o regional\ncompression ( 2 ) . S t i l l o l d e r s u p r a c r u s t a l s (Sargur Group) a r e found i n the\ng n e i s s i c basement a s small enclaves (3) and their o r i g i n i s obscure.\n\nOrthogonal t o the trend of the supracrustal b e l t s is the E-W trending\ncharnockite b e l t extending from Madras t o Mangalore (4). A the supracrustal\ns\n\nb e l t s approach this b e l t they become narrower, more highly metamorphosed and\nmigmatised. Trains o f supracrustal enclaves cut throught the charnockite b e l t\nand after passing through a series of small d e x t r a l shear zones (Kabini,\nGundlupet, Moyar, Bhavani) are terminated by the major Palghat-Cauvery shear\nzone ( 5 ) . Curving i n t o t h i s main shear zone are the numerous n o r t h e r l y v e r t i c a l\nf a u l t zones (Chitradurga, Bababudan). The f a u l t s a r e developed\ncontemporaneously w i t h the f o l d i n g of Dharwar s u p r a c r u s t a l s and a r e fomed a s a\nconsequence of subhor.izonta1 shortening and basement up1 i f t t o the e a s t (6).\nThe Palghat-Cauvery shear zone is marked by f i s s i l e gneisses containing r o o t s of\nsupracrustal b e l t s and dismembered 1ayered b a s i c complexes. The high grade\nterrain occurring t o the north of this shear zone represents deeper c r u s t a l\nl e v e l s of the Dharwar craton (7) brought up due t o northerly t i l t of the\nPeninsular s h i e l d during Himalayan c o l l i s i o n .\nPandayan Mobile Belt: This terrain which l i e s t o the south of the Cauvery\nshear zone i s d i s c i n c t l y d i f f e r e n t from the Dharwar Craton and i s d i v i s i b l e i n t o\ntwo zones, the northern and southern. The northern zone consists e s s e n t i a l l y of\nthe orthoquartzite-carbonate-pel i t e suite (with minor basics) w i t h i n a\nmigmatitic and charnockitic terrain. I t has curving and swirling s t r u c t u r a l\np a t t e r n s 1i ke the c e n t r a l Limpopo o r Greenland. These swirling structures a r e\nprobably r e l a t e d t o movements on the Cauvery d e x t r a l shear i n the north and\nAchankovil sinistral shear i n the south. The southern zone is a l i n e a r b e l t o f\nkhondal ite-leptynite-charnockite, which is an extension of the South-West Group\nof S r i Lanka and Androyan Group of Malagasy. Contrary t o the p i c t u r e painted by\nDrury e t a1 ( 5 ) , the Achankovil shear does n o t t r u n c a t e discordant s t r u c t u r a l\nt r e n d s from the north. The comparison of this b e l t w i t h the Eastern Ghat b e l t\ni s not v a l i d due t o the absence of manganese-marble a s s o c i a t i o n , abundance o f\nquartz arenites and unfavourable structural trends. The l a c k of worthwhile\ngeochronological information Jeaves us i n doubt whether this forms p a r t of an\n\n.\n\nTectonic Evolution of the Archaean High-Grade Terrain\nRamakrishnan, M.\n145\n\no l d e r gneissic t e r r a i n o r a gounger (Proterozoic?) mobile b e l t .\nThe contact o f the Dharwar craton and t h i s b e l t i s a zone o f transcurrent\ndislocation, b u t i n p o i n t o f d e t a i l t h i s i s a zone of highly d u c t i l e structures\nw i t h both the t e r r a i n s i n t e r a c t i n g i n a d i f f u s e mobile zone. There i s no\nevidence i n t h i s zone f o r the c o l l i s i o n a l suture v i s u a l i z e d by Drury e t a l . (5).\nEastern Ghat Mobile &: This i s a long mobile b e l t f r i n g i n g t h e Singhbhum\nand Central Indian cratons and extending t o the north-east o f Dharwar craton.\nI t i s predominantly composed o f khondalites, charnockites, l e p t y n i t e s and minor\namounts o f manganiferous marbles and quartzites. This b e l t i s c u t o f f a t the\ncontinental margin near Ongole, where i t extends i n t o Napier Complex o f\nAntarctica and Highland Group of S r i Lanka. The t h r u s t a t the eastern margin o f\nthe Middle t o Late Proterozoic Cuddapah basin and s i m i l a r basins t o t h e n o r t h i s\na l a t e event i n the polymetamorphic evolution o f t h i s b e l t and i s not l i n k e d t o\nthe main movement o f Palghat-Cauvery shear zone as suggested by Drury e t a l .\n(5). The eastern Ghat b e l t appears t o be a product of continent-continent\ncollision.\nReferences\n1. Swami Nath J. and Ramakrishnan M. (1981) Geol. Surv. India, Mem 112, p. 350.\n2. Ramakrishnan M. The investigation\nhas confirmed the continuous section through a former lower crust, with\nfelsic charnockites predominating the lower part and felsic gneisses\nthe upper part.\nThe structure of originalgneisses is preserved in charnockites\nand the latter show petrographic evidence for prograde metamorphism.\nThe prograde metamorphism is of isochemical nature as revealed by the\nsimilarity of compositions of tonalitic gneisses and tonalitic\ncharnockites. However, the depletion of LIL elements particularly Rb,\ncaused variation in K/Rb ratios from low values ( 3 4 5 ) in the gneisses\nin upper part to higher values (1775) in the charnockites In the lower\ncrust. This variation in K/Rb ratio in a north to south traverse is\nrelated to the progressive break-down of hydrous minerals under\ndecreasing H20 and increasing C02 fluid conditions. Metasomatism and\npartial meltin8 has also taken place to a limited extent along shear\nplanes and weak zones. Dur ng cooling the H20 circulation affected\nsubstantial auto-regression\'\nin the transit \xe2\x82\xacon zone resulting in the\nformation of second generation biotite.\'\nGeothermometry and geobarometry of orthogneisses also show a\nprogade metamorplifsm from about 5-6 Kbars and 725+25OC near the\northopyroxene isograd at the top of the section in theyorth, to about\n7 to 85 Kbars and 775+25OC towards south. The progressive increase in\n.\nmetalaorphic grade i s demonstrated by the systematic change in the\nmine,ral composition from felsic gneisses in the north to felsic\ncharnockites in the south (eg. hornblende composition varying from\nhornblende-edenite to pargasite composition, and increase in contents\nof An in plagioclase, Ti in biotite and hornblende).\nThe mineral\nchemistry in such rocks can record a depth of equilfbration of minerals\nat 18 to 21 km and 25 to 29 km, and indicate steep geothermal gradients\nranging from 35 to 3 \' / m\n8Ck\nand 6 to 30\xc2\xb0C/km in the upper and lower\nparts of the, crust respectively\nThe presence of such rocks now at\nthe surface of the continental crust (ca. 35 km) could be cited as an\nevidence for thLs part of the Archaean crust to have been atleast 5 3\nto 64 km thick. The differences in recorded pressure conditions might\nbe related to the differences in erosional rates, rather than to\ntectonism.\n\n5.\n\n148\n\nORIGIN AND EVOIAJTION OF GBEISS-CHARNOCXIl\'E RlMXS\nBameshwar Rao, D and Narayana, B.L.\n.\n\nThe petrochemical studies do not support the formation of the\nprecursors (rocks of tonalitic and mafic co position) through primary\nf ractionation of andesitic-dacitic magma\'\nor intra-crustal partial\nmelting4 The origin of precursor may be explained by the fractional\ncrystallization of basaltic magm3 or partial melting of amphibolite,\nleaving a mafic restite containing hornblende.\n\n.\n\n1. 2) subhorizontal deep burial\nof sediments; 3) intense deformation of infra and supracrustal rocks; 4) early\n\ngranulite facies metamorphism predating F2-loss of primary structure in sediments\nand formation of charnockites from amphibole bearing gneisses and khondalites\nfrom pelites; Petrol. v.96, p. 225-244,\n\nand Ravindra\n\nN89-22245\nGEOLOGY AND GEOCEEMISTRY OF TEE MIDDLE PROTEROZOIC EASTERN\nGHAT MOBILE BELT AND ITS COMPARISON WITE TEE LOWER CRUST OF TEE\nSOUTEERN PENINSULAR SEIELD; M,V, Subba Rao, National Geophysical\nResearch Institute, Hyderabad 500 007 India\n\n-\n\nTwo prominent rock suites constitute the lithology of the Eastern\nGhat mobile belt : (1) the khondalite suite the metapelites, and (2)\nthe charnockite suite. Later intrusives include ultramafic sequences,\nanorthosites and granitic gneisses.\n\n-\n\nThe chief structural element in the rocks of the Eastern Ghats is\na planar fabric (gneissosity), defined by the alignment of platy\nminerals like flattened quartz, garnet, sillimanite, graphite, etc.\nThe parallelism between the foliation and the lithological layering is\nrelated to isoclinal folding. The major structural trend (axial plane\nfoliation trend) observed in the belt s NE-SW. Five major tectonic\nevents have been delineated in the belt\nA boundar fault along the\nwestern margin of the Eastern Ghats, bordering the Tow grade terrain\nhas been substantiated by recent gravity2 and the deep seismic sounding\nstudies3\n\n\\.\n\n.\n\nField evidence shows that the pyroxene granulites (basic\ngranulites) post-date the khondalite suite, but are older than the\ncharnockites as well as the granitic gneisses4.\nPolyphase\nmetamorphism, probably correlatable with different periods of\ndeformation is recorded.\nUsing geochemical parameters, it is inferred that the basic\ngranulites could be an earlier phase of the charnockite suite and\ngenetically related to the charnockites.\nThe relationships of\nrelatively immobile elements like Mg-Zr, Ca-Y, Zr-Y and the rare earth\nelement (REE) patterns suggest that the protoliths of these rocks are\nderived from a single source. The REE data supports the field\nrelations that the basic granulites are emplaced e a r l i e r compared t o\n\ncharnockites and the source material for these rocks could be a\nmetasomatised mantle, enriched in LREE.\nIC-Rb relations suggest that these elements have been depleted in\nall the litho-units during the granulite facies metamorphism; however,\nrestoration of some of these depleted elements to varying degrees by\nmetasomatic enrichment has been observed. This restoration may be the\nresult of the Eastern Ghat orogeny.\nThe granulites of the mobile belt as a whole are characterised by\nvariable LIL element geochemistry, while the cratonic granulites show a\nlesser degree of variation. This could be attributed to the\ndeformation and the orogenic effects in the mobile belt. The variation\nin lithology suggests that, while the lithologies of the Eastern Ghat\nbelt evolved in a geosynclinal type environment, the cratonic\n\n152\n\nAND GEOCHEMISTRY OF TEE EASTBBIO GHAT MOBILE BELT\nSubba b o , HV\n..\n\nGE0IXK;p\n\ngranulites could be the deeply eroded sections of the crust or the\nhigh-grade equivalents of the amphibolite grade terrain to the north of\nthis section, which have not witnessed much of tectonic deformation and\nthe attendant chemical changes.\nThe cratonic granulites are Na-rich, whereas the granulites of the\nEastern Ghats are in general K-rich; the latter are also enriched in\nRb, Ba and T . The immobile element concentrations like Zr, Y and REE\nh\nwhich indicate the origin of the protolith, are more in the Eastern\nGhat mobile belt granulites, compared to the cratonic granulites.\nTotal REE levels as also LREE enrichment are more in the Eastern Ghats\ngranulites. An inhomogeneous amphibolite source of variable mineral or\nchemic 1 composition has been postulated for the charnockites of the\n3\ncraton\nThe charnockites of the Eastern Ghats based on their\nimmobile element geochemistry appear to have been derived from a\nhomogeneous source.\n\n.\n\nThe field relations in the Eastern Ghats point to the intense\ndeformation of the terrain,apparently both before, during and after\nmetamorphism. This, coupled with close intermingling of gra ulites and\nthe khondalite suite and a greater abundance of khondalites\'\nindicate\nthat the Eastern Ghats granulites were developed during an intense\ndeformation (perhaps collisional) event, whereas no such evidence has\nyet been found in the southern granulite terrain.\n\nREFERENCES\n\n1. Comprehensive microthermometric measurements constrain their densities to be in t h e range of\n0.95-1.0 g/cm3 in banded charnockites, 0.87-0.97 g/cm3 in khondalites and\n0.83-0.95 g/cm3 in incipient charnockites.\nThe combined high density fluid\ninclusion isochores and t h e range of thermometric estimates from mineral\nassemblages (Fig. 1) indicate entrapment pressures in t h e range of 5.4 to\n6.1 Kbar. Carbon\ndioxide generated by decarbonation reactions would be enriched in lighter\ncarbon isotopes as compared to t h e carbonate. Isotopic exchange between an externally\nderived fluid and graphite in t h e rock would considerably enrich t h e carbon\ndioxide with lighter carbon.\nW e hence interpret t h e lighter d13C values in\nthese samples to be t h e result of the interaction of externally derived fluids\nwith graphite t h a t is ubiquitously present in the precursor gneisses and incipient\ncharnockites in these localities.\nEventhough the apparent small shift in carbon isotope composition during\ncharnockite formation is consistent with internal buffering, t h e observed carbon\ndioxide abundance in t h e incipient charnockites as compared t o their precursor\ngneisses argues for external buffering of C 0 2 . This leads us to infer that\neventhough some fluid flushing did occur, i t equilibrated with graphite present\nin t h e rocks during charnockite formation.\nREFERENCES\n(1)\n\nHarris,\nV\n.\n\nN.B.W.,\n\nHolt,\n\nR.W.\n\nand\n\nDrury,\n\nS.A.\n\n(1982)\n\nJour.\n\nGeol.,\n\n90, pp. 509-528.\n\n(2)\n\nSantosh, M. (1982) J o u r .\nG W l . , 90, 509-5270\n\n-\n\nNS 9 22 2 5 0o\nTHE ROLE OF BORON KND FLUIDS IN HIGH TEMPERATURE, SHALLOW\nLEVEL METAMORPHISM OF THE CHUGACH METAMORPHIC COMPLEX, ALASKA\nV.B. Sisson and W.P. Leeman, Dept of Geology, Rice University, Houston, TX 77251-1892\nThe possible role of boron (B) involvement in granite equilibria and generation of melts\nduring crustal metamorphism has been a focus of speculation in recent literature (1,2,3). Most\nof the evidence for such involvemert derives from experimental data which implies that the\naddition of B will lower the temperature of the granite solidus (43). Also the presence of\ntourmaline has a minor effect on the temperature of the solidus (6). Further indirect evidence\nthat B may be involved in partial melting processes is the observation that granulites are\ncommonly depleted in B (7),whereas the B content of low grade metapelites can be high (up\nto 2000 ppm, 8 & 9). Our measurements of the whole-rock B contents of granulites from the\nMadras region, India are low, ranging from 0.4 to 2.6 ppm, and Ahmad and Wilson (10)\nsuggest that B was mobilized in the fluid phase during granulite facies metamorphism of the\nBroken Hill Complex, Australia. Thus, it appears that during the amphibolite to granulite\ntransition, B is systematically lost from metasediments. The B that is released will probably\npartition into the vapor phase and/or melt phase.\nField data from a high temperature, shallow level regional metamorphic complex in the\neastern Chugach Mountains of southern Alaska indicate localized partial melting has occurred\nin response to increased heat flux from intrusion of tonalite sills and plutons (11). In addition,\nthe amount of tourmaline increased with increased metamorphic grade. However, in the\nmigmatitic core of the complex, tourmaline is absent in the partial melt zones and rare in the\nhost metasediment. The conditions of metamorphism (400 to 600 OC outside the migmatitic\ncore and 650 OC within the core at pressures of 2.5 to 3.5 kbar). and the presence of locally\nderived granitic melts, imply that B may be involved in the partial melting process.\nC\nApproximately 3 wt % B203 is needed to lower the granite solidus from 700 OC to 650 O\n(5). The breakdown of tourmaline may release the B necessary for fluxing the partial melting.\nThe boron-rich fluid or melt is inferred to have escaped and is possibly represented by late\nstage tourmaline-bearing pegmatites and tourmaline-quartz veins. Below we present our\npreliminary results from whole-rock boron analysis and fluid inclusion observations done to\nexplore the role of boron and fluids during the migmatization of the Chugach region.\nThe Chugach Metamorphic Complex (12,13,14) is developed in the Campanian to\nMaastrichtian Valdez Group, which is predominantly clastic argillite and graywacke with minor\ntuffaceous basalt deposited in either a trench setting or a deep sea fan. The entire region was\nmetamorphosed to greenschist facies at 55-60 Ma, possibly by a combination of heat\nconduction from subducted hot, young oceanic crust (15) and heat advection from dewatering\nof fluids from sediments at depth in a subduction zone setting (16). The whole-rock boron\ncontent of the greenschist package is moderate and the concentration of the B is controlled by\nthe host lithology (Table 1). The fluids involved in greenschist metamorphism are represented\nby hot, low salinity brines observed in fluid inclusions in first generation quartz veins. Later\nbrines have both lower salinities and homogenization temperatures which may reflect cooling\nof the fluid and possibly mixing with meteoric fluids. The salinity decrease is correlated with a\ndecrease in B content (Table 1). A similar relationship between B and C1\' (salinity) has been\nobserved in thermal waters (e.g. Yellowstone, 17).\nThe regional high temperature metamorphism followed the greenschist event in response to\nintrusion of tonalite sills and plutons at 55 Ma. Initial measurements of the Chugach\nwhole-rock boron content of samples from the amphibolite facies and migmatitic core are low\nsuggesting B has been lost. This may be related to the breakdown of tourmaline. However,\nsome of the highest grade samples still have B contents similar to the greenschists (compare\nsample 96 with 7, Table 1). Additionally the B content of the intrusive tonalites (samples 11\nand 103, Table 1) and the locally derived granitic melts (sample 98A1, Table 1) is low. The\nlow boron in all these rock types and lack of tourmaline in the intrusive tonalites suggests that\nB is not present in sufficient quantity to have any affect on the solidus of the melts. However,\n\nx\n\n,\n&\n\nBORON AND FLUIDS IN THE CHUGACH METAMORPHIC COMPLEX\nV.B. Sisson and W.P.Leeman\nsome of the boron originally in the melt phase may have preferentially partitioned into a vapor\nphase leaving the tonalites and locally derived granites with low B content.\nThe majority of the fluid preserved as fluid inclusions in the Chugach Metamorphic Compex\nis C02-rich and the primary fluids have isochores which pass through peak metamorphic\nconditions. The B content of the host quartz veins is low (Table 1). One vein in the\namphibolite facies region does preserve a transition from H20-CO mixture to pure CO2. The\nsalinity of the H20 component is not great enough to suggest flui immisciblity as a cause for\nthe composition change at these metamorphic conditions (550 OC and 3 kbar). However, a\npossible explanation for the composition change is that the water has been incorporated into\neither the intrusive tonalites or locally derived melts. Thus, the CO2 may represent a residual\nfluid. Olsen (18) describes a similar relationship for C02-rich fluids preserved in migmatites\nfrom Colorado.\nThese preliminary measurements imply that the boron content of rocks in the Chugach\nMetamorphic Complex is not sufficient to influence the processes of partial melting at low\npressures. Further work is needed to constrain the mass balance of B during progressive\nmetamorphism and evaluate the possiblity that both B and H20 have been incorporated into\nmelts which have since left the system\n\n8\n\nTABLE 1 BORON CONTENT OF CHUGACH METAMORPHlC COMPLEX\nSample\n6\n10\n45B\n48B\n50B\n7\n45A\n48A\n50A\n8B\n48D\n64A\n64C\n64D\n8BV\nlOBV\n89D\n93R\n12\n17G\n35\n45\n94L\n31D\n86E\n110\n96C\n98A2\n98A 1\n103\n108\n11\n105\n\nRock Type\ngraywacke\ngraywacke\ngraywacke\ngraywacke\ngraywacke\nargillite\nargillite\nargillite\nargillite\nbasalt\nbasalt\nqtz vein\nqtz vein\nqtz vein\nqtz vein\nqtz vein\nqtz vein\nqtz vein\nschist\nschist\nschist\nschist\nschist\nschist\nschist\nmigmatite\nmigmatite\nmigmatite\ngranite melt\ntonalite\ntonalite\ntonalite\ntourmaline-selvage\n\nTemperature (OC)*\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n400\n450, 3.5 wt %\n375, 2 wt %\n250, 0.5 wt %\n375, 3.5 wt %\n400, minor CO2\n600, COz-rich\n550, CO?-rich\n500\n500\n575\n540\n550\n600\n600\n650\n650\n650\nL.\n\n--\n\n----\n\n--\n\n**\n\nBoron (in ppm)\n46\n\n39\n48\n23\n37\n29\n18\n25\n18\n4.6\n2.7\n2.6\n0.7\n0.4\n0.2\n0.9\n2.1\n2.3\n2.7\n50\n8.5\n2.3\n42\n6.6\n18\n6.7\n46\n8.2\n7.6\n6.7\n2.3\n3.0\n210\n\nBORON Ah\'D FLUIDS IN THE CHUGACH METAMORPHIC COMPLEX\nV.B. Olsen SN (1987) Contrib Mineral Petrol, 96, 104-120.\n\nNag-22251\nI\n\n\\\n\nGEDCHJMISTRY AND ORIGIN OF COLD MINERALIZATION IN THE KOLAR\n\nN Siva Siddaiah and V Rajamani, School of Ehvironmental Sciences,\n.\n.\nJawaharlal Nehru University, New Delhi-110067, INDIA\nIhe Kolar Schist Belt is the most important gold producing, volcanicdominated, Archean belt of the Dharwar Craton. Gold occurs here as goldquartz-sulfide lodes and as gold-quartz-calcite veins, the latter confined\nonly to the eastern part of the belt. Profuse mineralization and extensive\nmining have been confined t o the central part of the belt, Kolar Gold Fields\n(KGF) Recently, economic concentrations of gold mineralization has been\ndiscovered in the southern part of the belt, whereas in the northern part\nmineralization is reported to be poor and uneconomic.\n\n.\n\nIhe gold-quartz-sulfide lodes occur either associated with thin units\nof banded iron formation interbanded with kamatiitic and tholeiitic amphibolites or directly with the latter. There are several parallel lodes in\nthe KGF area. \'Ihe lodes occur all along the strike, from central to southern\nparts of the belt discontinuously. The lodes in general are typically banded/\nlayered, are parallel to the schistosity of the amphibolitic host rocks and\nappear to have been confined to the contacts of different textural varieties\nof amphibolites. Wall-rock alteration, characterized by the presence of\nbiotite and/or garnet is restricted to a few centimeters on either side of\nthe lodes. In KGF, the sulfide lodes are interbanded with graphitic schists.\nGraphitic schists are not encountered in the southern part.\nSulfide lodes consist of bands/layers of cherty-quartz, sulfides and\nmafic silicates. In KGF, the lodes also include magnetite bands. Here the\nwidth of the bands decreases towards western margin of the belt. Bands/\nlayers are at places deformed because of complex folding and shearing. The\nsulfide mineralogy includes dominantly pyrrhotite and arsenopyrite Minor\nsulfide phases include loellingite, chalcopyrite, sphalerite and pyrite.\nPyrrhotite and arsenopyrite tend to occur as monomineralic layers. Pyrrhotite is present essentially as hexagonal type. Arsenopyrite occurs as\ncoarse to medium grained euhedral crystals which are often deformed. Gold\ncomnonly occurs as patchy inclusions within the deformed arsenopyrite\ncrystals and as sub-rounded inclusions within the silicates. In KGF, the\nsulfide lodes include magnetite, ilmenite and graphite. Although the major\nsulfide mineralogy is remarkably uniform among the various lodes in the\nbelt, the total sulfide and arsenopyrite contents of the lode matter are\nquite variable. However, there is no correlation among the total sulfide\ncontents, (5-35 volume per cent) concentration of base metals and that of\ngold. Base metal concentrations are significantly low except in the weste m s t and southemost lodes. \'Ihe gold concentration varies between 1 to\n6 ppn and does not correlate with arsenopyrite contents of the lades. However, in the KGF area, among the four sulfide lodes there is a definite\nmineralogical and geochemical zoning. Base metals, total sulfide, K 0,\n~l o and graphite increase from east to west; arsenopyrite, magnetize, iron\nan?! gold decrease from east to west. The sulfide gneral assemblage represents a minimum temperature of equilibration~500.\nC\n\n.\n\nGOLD MINERALIZATION\n\nSiva Siddaiah N and Rajamani V\nGold-quartz-calcite lodes, occur exclusively on the eastern side of\nthe belt, close to the felsic schists and gneisses known as the Champion\nGneiss. Although the lodes are parallel to the general strike of the belt,\nat m y places they make a small angle with the schistosity of the amphibolitic host rock. The lodes appear to be fracture-filled veins within the\ncountry rock with a narrow zone of calcite-biotite alteration. The lodes\nat many places are also sheared. They consist dominantly of quartz, calcite, albite + biotite + sulfide and tourmaline. Sulfide content is\nusually very Small, mucli less than a per cent. Galena is reported to be\nthe dominant sulfide ()\n1. The average concentration of gold is 10 ppn\noccurring essentially as native gold. Base metals are present in very low\nconcentration. However, Cr and Ni show much higher abundances, as much as\n400-500 ppn for lodes rich in quartz and calcite. The lodes have remarkable\ndepth persistence ( > 3.5 km) and there are no observable changes in the\ngold tenor, nor in the nature of alteration with depth. Fluid inclusion\nand oxygenoisotope data, suggest that the temperature of precipitation was\naround 300 C and it occurred from a uniform reservoir of fluid at least\nfor 3 km depth (2, 3, 4. Alteration and mineralization in the quartz lodes\n)\nappear cogenetic and postdate peak metamorphism.\nGeological, mineralogical, mineral-textural and geochemical data of\nthe sulfide lodes in the belt indicate that the gold mineralization could\nbe related to low temperature, low E and high pH "rk-dominated geothermal\nh\nsystems set up in the subnarine volcanic pile prior to amphibolite metamorphism. Relatively long-lived geothermal system produced an economic deposit,\nwhereas short-lived ones, because of rapid burial by younger basalts throttled the geothermal system and diffused the discharge yielding low grade\nore bodies. The source for gold and iron could be iron enriched tholeiites\nderived from source regions enriched in kamatiitic melt components ( ) and\n5\nkomatiitic rocks derived by very low extents of melting of metasamatised\nmantle sources ( 6 ) . On the other hand, the geographical restriction of the\nquartz-calcite lodes, their mineralogical and geochemical data and their\nestimated temperature of formation all seem to suggest that a major part of\nthe hydrothermal fluids, and a significant portion of gold could have been\nderived from mantle derived intrusive, sanukitoid type magma sources, similar to the champion Gneiss occurring on the eastern part of the belt ()\n7.\nHowever, the possibility of some input by remobilization of a premetamorphic\nsulfide protore to quartz lodes cannot be ruled out completely.\n(1)\n(2)\n()\n3\n()\n4\n()\n5\n(6)\n\n()\n7\n\nS Narayanaswami et a1 (1960) &on. The southern\nPeninsular shield exposes one of the largest h i g h grade\ngneiss- charnockite terrains extending between Orissa in the\nnorth-east and Cape Comorin in the South.\nThe h i g h grade\nterrain in the south is in juxtaposition with the prominant\ngranite-greenstone\nbelts\nof\nKarnataka\ncraton.\nThe\nrelationship between the low and h i g h grade regions are not\nwell understood.\nGreater attention has been paid to study\nthe granite-greenstone belts of Karnataka craton compared to\nthe adjoining granulite belts.\nThese shields are considered to represent ancient\ncontinental\nnucleii\ncomposed of the earlier\ncrustal\nmaterials.\nDetailed studies of these terrains in different\nparts of the world contributed valuable clues to the\nevolutionary history of different parts of the earth\xe2\x80\x99s\ncrust.\nExtensive work has been carried out on various\naspects of petrology, petrochemistry,\nmineral chemistry,\ngeochemistry and geochronology in major shield areas in\nother parts of the world.\nIn contrast to these studies,\nmuch less information is available on the h i g h grade regions\nof southern Peninsular shield of India. Wickham,\n.\nDepartment of the Geophysical Sciences, University of Chicago, 5734 South\nEllis Avenue, Chicago, IL 60637\nRecent models for granulite petrogenesis have involved infiltrative\nstreaming of Cop derived from deep-seated sourcesS1 removal of H20 in locally\n, or various combinations of these two\ngenerated silicate\np r o c e ~ s e s . ~ ~ ~ these models require a heat aource of some type to generate\nAll\nthe high crustal temperatures associated with anatexis and granulite-grade\nmetamorphism, and this most likely takes the form of mantle-derived basaltic\nmagma that is either underplated or intruded into the lower continental\ncrust.\nHuppert and SparksC have recently shown that such underplating is\nlikely to cause rapid, very large-scale melting of the overlying crust (roof\nrock) over time scales of only a few hundred years ( . .\neg,\na basaltic sill 500\nmeters thick can generate a melt layer between 300 and 1000 meters thick in\nless than 500 years, depending on the initial temperature of the crust).\nRefractory rocks within the melt layer, such as carbonate-rich metasediments,\nwould not melt, but are expected to sink into the underlying mafic magma.\nCalculations indicate that for plausible rock compositions (e. g , amphibolite\n.\nfacies diopside marbles) the thermal energy of the mafic magma would be enough\nto promote very high temperature decarbonation of large volumes of marble (T >\nSSO"C), generating sudden, large fluxes of C02-rich fluid that would be\nreleased upwards through the anatectic zone into the overlying crust. Such a\nprocess could explain many petrological features of those granulite terranes\nwhere C02-streaming is thought to be important, and may also be an underlying\ncause of commonly observed surface emanations of Cop at volcanic centers\nassociated with extensional tectonism (e.g.,\nin the Massif Central).\nThe\nisotopic composition of such emanations is readily interpretable in terms of\nderivation from deep-seated metacarbonate rocks.\nThis model is readily applicable to the granulite terrane of Southern\nIndia where metacarbonates occur within the deeper parts of the section\nincluding the amphibolite-granulite transition zone.\nFurthermore, it\nobviates the need to remove low melt fractions from deep crustal rocks as a\nprincipal dehydration mechanism; compaction theory has shown this to be a very\nsluggish process that is unlikely to be important over typical geological time\nIt also avoids any mechanism involving the subduction of large\nscales.*\nvolumes of sedimentary carbonate into the mantle to provide a Cop source.\nWhether or not Cop-flushing occurs during granulite-grade metamorphism of the\nlower crust may simply reflect the presence or absence of carbonate rocks\nwithin the zone of major anatexis ( . .\nie,\nat those crustal levels immediately\nadjacent to an intruded/underplated mafic layer).\nCOP fluxes generated in\nthis way may be a natural consequence of crustal growth processes involving\nunderplating of mantle material beneath a carbonate-bearing lower crust.\n\nMETAMORPHISM OF THE ODDANCHATRAM ANORTHOSITE, TAMIL\nNADU, SOUTH INDIA. R A Wiebe, Dept. of Geology, Franklin\n. .\nand Marshall College, Lancaster, PA, 17604 and A S.\n.\nJanardhan, Dept. of Geology, Manasa Gangotri, Mysore 6.\nThe Oddanchatram anorthosite [1,2] is located in the\nMadurai District of Tamil Nadu, near the town of Pahi. It\nis emplaced into a granulite facies terrain commonly presumed\nto have undergone its last regional metamorphism in the late\nArchean about 2600 m y\n. . [ 3 ] . The surrounding country rock\nconsists of basic granulites, charnockites and\nmetasedimentary rocks including quartzites, pelites and\ncalc-silicates. The anorthosite is clearly intrusive into\nthe country rock and contains many large inclusions of\npreviously deformed basic granulite and quartzite within 100\nmeters of its contact [ 2 ] . Both this intrusion and the nearby\nKaduvar anorthosite show evidence of having been affected by\nlater metamorphism and deformation.\nThe anorthosite is typical of Proterozoic anorthosites\nin that it is largely massive and coarse-grained, containing\n) and has\non average more than 90 percent plagioclase (An\nassociated lenses rich in Fe-Ti oxides. Plagioc?&g\'is\nvariably recrystallized: it generally displays abundant,\nstrongly curved secondary twinning and has strongly sutured\nboundaries. The most common mafic minerals are hornblende,\naugite and orthopyroxene. Hornblende and some pyroxenes\nprobably crystallized during metamor hism, but some pyroxene\nalso occurs in primary igneous textur 6 . Garnet occurs\nlocally as equant crystals in thin discontinuous bands, but\nhas not been found as a reaction rim between plagioclase and\npyroxene. Although delicate primary igneous features are\nlocally well preserved, this anorthosite appears to have been\nstrongly affected by deformation and metamorphism after its\nemplacement. The rocks do not appear to have suffered\nsignificant strain after the growth of garnet.\nIntrusive contacts of the anorthosite with the\nsurrounding country rock a r e w e l l exposed. Sharply bounded\ndikes of relatively fine-grained anorthosite occur at a few\nlocations; some are tightly folded. Anorthosite near the\ncontact commonly contains abundant elongate inclusions Of\nbasic granulite and lesser amounts of garnet-bearing\nquartzite. Post-emplacement deformation is indicated by a\nlocally strong penetrative fabric and by boudinage of some\ninclusions. Assimilation of metasedimentary rocks appears\ncommon along some portions of the contact: where\ncalc-silicate rocks have been incorporated the anorthosite is\nabnormally calcic and where pelitic rocks have been\nincorporated the anorthosite contains discontinuous zones\nwith disseminated quartz and equant garnets 121. Most\ngarnets are partly or completely replaced by delicate\nsymplectites of hypersthene and anorthite.\nMineral assemblages useful for thermobarometry are found\nin the anorthosite and in the surrounding country rock.\n\n\\\n\nMETAMORPHISM OF THE ODDANCHATRAM ANORTHOSITE\nWiebe, R.A.\n\n190\n\n, Janardhan,\n\nA.S.\n\nAnorthositic rocks locally contain garnet, quartz,\northopyroxene and clinopyroxene in addition to the dominant\nintermediate plagioclase. Pelitic country rocks contain an\nearly assemblage of rutile, garnet, sillimanite, and quartz\nwhich has partly reacted to produce prominant rims of\ncordierite between garnet and sillimanite. A charnockite\nlocated roughly t w o km south of Oddanchatram contains the\nassemblage, quartz-plagioclase-orthopyroxene-garnet.\nAlthough some garnets in anorthosite lack symplectite\nrims and occur in sharp contact with primary intermediate\nplagioclase, they more typically have broad, essentially\nunzoned cores and narrow rims depleted in Ca where they are\nin contact with surrounding symplectites of orthopyroxene and\nanorthite. Garnet in the pelitic rocks is much lower in\ngrossularite component and essentially unzoned. In the\ncharnockite it is also unzoned and very low in MgO.\nOrthopyroxenes in anorthositic rocks have Mg/(Fe+Mg) of\nroughly 0.55. Neither pyroxene is significantly zoned.\nOrthopyroxene in the charnockite has much lower Mg/(Mg+Fe).\nPrimary plagioclase in the anorthosite ranges from about An46\nto An55. Plagioclase in the symplectites is between An95 and\n-5.\n\nIn the charnockite it is An34.\n\nMetamorphic equilibration temperatures in the\nanorthosites, based on coexisting garnet and cliBopyroxene,\nrange from a maximum of about 92OoC to about 700 C It has\n.\nnot been possible to determine a maximum temperature of\nmetamorphism in the country rocks. The assemblage,\ngarnet-cordierite, is widespread in the pelitic rocks but is\nretrogressive. These minerals are essegtially unzoned and\nyield temperatures between 780 and 700 C\ntemperatures that\nclosely match the minimum temperatures recorded by\nsymplectites in the anorthosite. The relict assemblage,\ngarnet-sillimanite-quartz-rutile, could have been stable at\nmuch higher temperatures.\nEstimates of pressures within the anorthositic rocks are\nbased on the association of garnet-plagioclase-quartz with\northopyroxene or clinopyroxene. Garnets that lack\nsymplectite rims and the cores ofoother garnets yield\nestimates of about 11.3 kb at 920 C\n. Garnet rims in\nequilibrium with surrounding anorthite-hypersthene\nsymplectites yield estimates of from 7.3 to 5.6 kb at 775OC.\nPressures estimated for the pelitic rock are based on the\nretrogressive assemblage, garnet-cordierite. The model of\nAranovich and Podlesskii [ 4 ] yields pressures of from 7 7 to\n.\n7.2 kb. These pressures are consistent with the minimum\nvalues recorded by symplectite assemblages in the\nanorthosite. The relict assemblage,\ngarnet-sillimanite-quartz- rutile, could have been stable at\nthe highest pressures and temperatures determined for the\nanorthosite. In the charnockite, the assemblage,\nquartz-plagioclase-orthopyroxene- garnetd yields an estimate\nof 8 8 kb, assuming a temperature of 900 C\n.\n.\nBecause the Oddanchatram anorthosite should be similar\n\n-\n\nMETAMORPHISM OF THE ODDANCHATRAM ANORTHOSITE\nWiebe, R.A.,\n\nJanardhan, A.S.\n\nin age (ca. 1400 my) to the Chilka Lake anorthosite [5] the\nmetamorphism of the Oddanchatram anorthosite should record\ncrustal conditions in this part of the south Indian shield\nduring the middle to late Proterozoic. Temperatures and\npressures reported for other rocks in this portion of the\nshield (e.g. rocks near Madurai and Kodaikanal [6]) may\ntherefore be a record of Proterozoic rather than late Archean\nmetamorphism.\nThe maximum pressures reported here require that Archean\nsupracrustal rocks in the southeastern portion of the south\nIndian shield were buried to depths of 35 km in the middle\nProterozoic. Because the present crustal thickness is still\nabout 40 km (71 and because there is no evidence for\npost-anorthosite underplating, the crustal thickness in this\npart of the shield during the middle Proterozoic should have\nbeen roughly 75 km. The production of such abnormally thick\ncrust could be explained by continental collision and\nunderthrusting of the eastern margin of the south Indian\nshield beneath a converging continent. The Eastern Ghat\norogenic belt, which lies roughly 100 km east of the\nOddanchatram anorthosite, is thought to be such a\nmid-Proterozoic collisional belt [8]. Metamorphic mineral\nages of 1000 my [9] in this belt suggest that the Eastern\nGhat orogenic event could have been responsible for the\nmetamorphism and deformation of the Oddanchatram anorthosite.\nAcknowledgement: We are extremely grateful to Dr. S\n.\nSaravanan, Chairman and Managing Director, Tamil Nadu\nMinerals, Ltd., Madras, for all of his help in field\nlogistics.\nReferences\n[l] Narasimha Rao, P (1964) Jndian Min, 5 , 99-104.\n.\n[2] Janardhan, A S. & Wiebe, R A (1985) JLGeol.
